Definition of an-Nazar and Its Obligation
Definition of nazar and its obligation with lexical and Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of an-nazar (rational inquiry) in Islamic theology and why it is obligatory for all Muslims.

Core Idea
Rational inquiry (nazar) is obligatory because it is the path to truth and is commanded by God.

In this passage, the author defines the term 'an-nazar' (rational inquiry) by distinguishing its multiple meanings, focusing on the heart's contemplation. He then establishes its obligation using Quranic verses and notes the importance the Mu'tazila place on reason. This foundation is crucial for understanding the role of intellect in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the lexical meanings of nazar
The author lists four meanings: sight, benevolence, mercy, and heart's contemplation. Notice that the intended meaning is the last.
The distinction between physical sight and intellectual contemplation is important.
3
Study the Quranic evidence
The author cites verses urging reflection on creation. These verses serve as proof for the obligation of nazar.
The Quranic call to reflection is a central argument.
4
Understand the obligation and its implications
The author states nazar is obligatory for all Muslims, leading to the invalidity of blind imitation (taqlid).
This is a key point: obligation of inquiry invalidates uncritical acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يطلق النظر على معان فيراد به الرؤية والإحسان والرحمة ونظر القلب، وهو المقصود هنا لأنه يقوم على التفكير الذي يكشف عن الحقيقة وقد دعا الله إليه أو لم ينظروا في ملكوت السموات والأرض وفي الأرض آيات للموقنين) (وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون). وهو واجب عند المسلمين جميعاً ويعول عليه المعتزلة كل التعويل لأن العقل قبل السمع. وكان الناس قبل ورود الشرائع يحتكمون إلى عقولهم فيحسنون ويقبحون، وما دام النظر واجباً فالتقليد فاسد ويؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة. (راجع المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار ص د هـ والمقدمة ج (۱۲).
The term 'an-nazar' is applied to meanings: it is used for sight, benevolence, mercy, and the heart's contemplation, and it is what is intended here because it is based on thinking that reveals the truth. God has called to it: 'Do they not look at the kingdom of the heavens and the earth?' and 'And in the earth are signs for the certain' and 'And in yourselves, do you not then see?' It is obligatory for all Muslims, and the Mu'tazila rely on it completely because the intellect precedes revelation. People before the coming of laws used to refer to their intellects, deeming things good or bad. And as long as reflection is obligatory, imitation is invalid and leads to denial of the necessary. (Reference: al-Mughni by al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar p. d. h. and al-Muqaddima vol. 12.)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological definition of 'an-nazar' (rational inquiry). The author explains that nazar has multiple meanings: sight (ru'ya), benevolence (ihsan), mercy (rahma), and the heart's contemplation (nazar al-qalb). The intended meaning here is the heart's contemplation because it involves thinking that uncovers truth. He supports this with Quranic verses urging reflection on the heavens, earth, and oneself. He then states that nazar is obligatory (wajib) for all Muslims, and the Mu'tazila particularly rely on it because they hold that reason precedes revelation. He notes that before the coming of revealed laws, people used their intellects to judge good and bad. Since nazar is obligatory, imitation (taqlid) is invalid and leads to denying necessary truths. The passage ends with a bibliographic reference.

Key Terms
نظر
تقليد
عقل
سمع
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar (rational inquiry) is optional or only required for scholars.
Correction: The author states it is obligatory for all Muslims.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is imitation (taqlid) considered invalid?
A) Because it is easier than reflection.
B) Because reflection (nazar) is obligatory.
C) Because the Mu'tazila reject it.
D) Because it leads to skepticism.

Key Takeaway
An-nazar (rational inquiry) is an obligatory act of reflection that leads to truth and invalidates blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that nazar (rational inquiry) is obligatory for all Muslims. Have you personally engaged in systematic reflection on the proofs for God's existence, or do you rely on what you were taught without question? If the latter, what is stopping you from beginning today?
?
The passage warns that imitation leads to denying necessary truths. Is there a core belief about Allah that you hold simply because your family or community holds it, without having examined the evidence yourself? Will you commit to investigating it with your own intellect?
?
God calls us to look at the heavens and ourselves. When was the last time you truly pondered over a verse of the Quran or a sign in creation to strengthen your certainty? Write down one specific step you will take this week to practice an-nazar.

Refutation of the First Proof: Distinguishing Concept from Occurrence
Refutation of al-Razi's first proof via concept/occurrence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi's first proof is refuted by distinguishing between the concept of knowledge and its occurrence, and how the principle of the restricted requiring the absolute is used.

Core Idea
Concept is not occurrence; the first proof blurs them.

The author begins by stating that both of al-Razi's proofs fail because they confuse the concept of knowledge with its actual occurrence in the soul. The first proof, which argued that knowledge must be self-evident to avoid circularity, is addressed by showing that what is needed for acquiring a concept is the occurrence of the other concept, not its own concept. This is similar to the principle that the restricted cannot occur without the absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the first proof
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the central distinction: concept vs. occurrence
The passage begins by stating that the proofs are based on not distinguishing between taṣawwur (concept) and ḥuṣūl (occurrence). This is key.
3
Understand the muqayyad/muṭlaq principle
The author applies the principle that the restricted (muqayyad) cannot occur without the absolute (muṭlaq). Notice how this is used to explain why the concept of the other depends on occurrence, not concept.
This principle is a logical axiom in Islamic thought.
4
Note the critique of al-Mawāqif and Sharḥ al-Mukhtaṣar
The author criticizes earlier formulations that tried to define knowledge, pointing out their leniency. This shows the precision required in theological reasoning.
The critique is not central but reinforces the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الوجهين بأن مبناهما على عدم التفرقة بين تصور العلم وحصوله . أما الأول : فلان تصور العلم على تقدير اكتسابه يتوقف على تصور غيره، وتصور الغير لا يتوقف على تصوره ليلزم الدور، بل على حصوله بناء على امتناع حصول المقيد بدون المطلق، حتى لو لم يقل بوجود الكلي في ضمن الجزئيات لم يتوقف على حصوله أيضاً، وعبارة المواقف : إن الذي نحاول أن نعلمه بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم، وقد تسامح حيث حاول العلم بتصور الحقيقة والأحسن ما في شرح المختصر أن الذي يراد حصوله بالغير إنما هو تصور حقيقة العلم ، إلا أنه تسامح فيه أيضاً حيث قال : لا يمكن تعريفه . وأما الثاني : فلأن البديهي لكل أحد ليس هو تصور العلم بأنه موجود بل حصول العلم بذلك، وهو لا يستدعي تصور العلم به فضلا بداهته، كما عن أن كل أحد يعلم أن له نفساً ولا يعلم حقيقتها .
And it was answered concerning the two proofs that they are based on not distinguishing between the concept of knowledge (tasawwur al-'ilm) and its occurrence (husul). As for the first: because the concept of knowledge, on the assumption of its acquisition, depends on the concept of something else, and the concept of that something else does not depend on its concept to necessitate circularity, but rather on its occurrence, based on the impossibility of the occurrence of the restricted (muqayyad) without the absolute (mutlaq). Even if one does not affirm the existence of the universal in particulars, it does not depend on its occurrence either. And the wording of al-Mawaqif: 'What we seek to know by means other than knowledge is the concept of the reality of knowledge.' And he was lenient when he sought knowledge of the concept of reality. The best is what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: that what is intended to be obtained by means of something else is the concept of the reality of knowledge, but he was also lenient in it when he said: 'It cannot be defined.' As for the second: because what is self-evident for everyone is not the concept of knowledge as existing, but rather the occurrence of knowledge of that, and that does not require the concept of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence, just as everyone knows that he has a soul but does not know its reality.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of al-Razi's two proofs. The answer first states that both proofs are based on failing to distinguish between the concept (tasawwur) of knowledge and its occurrence (husul) in the soul. For the first proof, the answer explains that acquiring the concept of knowledge would depend on the concept of something else, but that something else's concept does not depend on the concept of knowledge (avoiding circularity); rather it depends on the occurrence of knowledge--similar to the principle that the restricted (muqayyad) cannot occur without the absolute (mutlaq). The answer then notes a leniency in al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Mukhtasar regarding what is sought. For the second proof, the answer says that what is self-evident for everyone is not the conceptual understanding (tasawwur) of knowledge as existing, but the actual occurrence (husul) of knowledge of something. That occurrence does not require the concept of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence. An analogy is given: everyone knows they have a soul but not its true nature.

Key Terms
تصور
حصول
مقيد
مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of the first proof implies knowledge cannot be self-evident at all.
Correction: The refutation only shows that al-Razi's argument for self-evidence fails; self-evidence of knowledge is not denied—its nature is simply not proven by this argument.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first proof, the concept of the 'something else' depends on which of the following?
A) The concept of knowledge
B) The occurrence of knowledge
C) The definition of knowledge
D) The self-evidence of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The first proof of al-Razi fails because it confuses the concept of knowledge with its actual occurrence; acquiring a concept depends on the occurrence of the other, not its concept, applying the principle that the restricted cannot occur without the absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you can define 'knowledge' or 'faith' (i.e., have a concept) that you actually possess it in your heart? How does this lesson's distinction between concept and occurrence challenge you to examine whether your knowledge has truly occurred (settled) in your soul?
?
The first proof's error is blurring concept and occurrence. In your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge, are you more concerned with acquiring concepts (definitions, arguments) or with ensuring their occurrence (realization) in your life and worship? What specific step will you take today to move from concept to occurrence?
?
The principle 'the restricted cannot occur without the absolute' reminds us that any limited form of knowledge depends on a broader reality. How does this expose the inadequacy of a purely academic study of Allah's names and attributes without experiential grounding? Will you commit to seeking the occurrence of that knowledge through sincere worship and reflection?

Refutation of the Second Proof: Self-Evidence of Occurrence, Not Concept
Refutation of al-Razi's second proof via self-evidence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof is refuted by showing that what is self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge, not the concept, just as everyone knows they have a soul without knowing its reality.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is about occurrence, not concept.

The author now turns to the second proof, which argued that knowledge must be self-evident because everyone knows it. The refutation clarifies that what is universally self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge (the fact that one knows something), not the conceptual understanding of what knowledge is. An analogy with the soul illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the second proof
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof and its translation. Notice how it directly follows the first refutation.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Distinguish between self-evidence of occurrence vs. concept
The key point: what is self-evident (badihi) for everyone is not the concept of knowledge as existing, but the occurrence of knowledge. This distinction undermines al-Razi's second proof.
3
Understand the soul analogy
The author provides an analogy: everyone knows they have a soul (occurrence) but no one knows its true reality (concept). This reinforces the distinction.
This analogy is a common tool in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الوجهين بأن مبناهما على عدم التفرقة بين تصور العلم وحصوله . أما الأول : فلان تصور العلم على تقدير اكتسابه يتوقف على تصور غيره، وتصور الغير لا يتوقف على تصوره ليلزم الدور، بل على حصوله بناء على امتناع حصول المقيد بدون المطلق، حتى لو لم يقل بوجود الكلي في ضمن الجزئيات لم يتوقف على حصوله أيضاً، وعبارة المواقف : إن الذي نحاول أن نعلمه بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم، وقد تسامح حيث حاول العلم بتصور الحقيقة والأحسن ما في شرح المختصر أن الذي يراد حصوله بالغير إنما هو تصور حقيقة العلم ، إلا أنه تسامح فيه أيضاً حيث قال : لا يمكن تعريفه . وأما الثاني : فلأن البديهي لكل أحد ليس هو تصور العلم بأنه موجود بل حصول العلم بذلك، وهو لا يستدعي تصور العلم به فضلا بداهته، كما عن أن كل أحد يعلم أن له نفساً ولا يعلم حقيقتها .
And it was answered concerning the two proofs that they are based on not distinguishing between the concept of knowledge (tasawwur al-'ilm) and its occurrence (husul). As for the first: because the concept of knowledge, on the assumption of its acquisition, depends on the concept of something else, and the concept of that something else does not depend on its concept to necessitate circularity, but rather on its occurrence, based on the impossibility of the occurrence of the restricted (muqayyad) without the absolute (mutlaq). Even if one does not affirm the existence of the universal in particulars, it does not depend on its occurrence either. And the wording of al-Mawaqif: 'What we seek to know by means other than knowledge is the concept of the reality of knowledge.' And he was lenient when he sought knowledge of the concept of reality. The best is what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: that what is intended to be obtained by means of something else is the concept of the reality of knowledge, but he was also lenient in it when he said: 'It cannot be defined.' As for the second: because what is self-evident for everyone is not the concept of knowledge as existing, but rather the occurrence of knowledge of that, and that does not require the concept of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence, just as everyone knows that he has a soul but does not know its reality.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of al-Razi's two proofs. The answer first states that both proofs are based on failing to distinguish between the concept (tasawwur) of knowledge and its occurrence (husul) in the soul. For the first proof, the answer explains that acquiring the concept of knowledge would depend on the concept of something else, but that something else's concept does not depend on the concept of knowledge (avoiding circularity); rather it depends on the occurrence of knowledge--similar to the principle that the restricted (muqayyad) cannot occur without the absolute (mutlaq). The answer then notes a leniency in al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Mukhtasar regarding what is sought. For the second proof, the answer says that what is self-evident for everyone is not the conceptual understanding (tasawwur) of knowledge as existing, but the actual occurrence (husul) of knowledge of something. That occurrence does not require the concept of knowledge, let alone its self-evidence. An analogy is given: everyone knows they have a soul but not its true nature.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge itself is self-evident to everyone.
Correction: What is self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge (that one knows something), not the conceptual definition of knowledge.

According to the passage, what is self-evident for everyone?
A) The concept of knowledge

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on your conceptual understanding of Allah when you have faith, or is your certainty based on the direct occurrence of belief in your heart? Reflect on whether you have ever doubted your faith because you couldn't fully grasp Allah's essence. Can you affirm Allah's existence even without comprehending His reality? Commit today to trusting the self-evident occurrence of iman in your heart, even when your mind struggles with concepts.
?
Think about your own soul: you know you have a soul, but do you know its true nature? Has this ever troubled you? The author uses this analogy to show that not knowing the essence does not negate certain knowledge. How does this apply to your relationship with Allah? What is one area of your life where you demand conceptual clarity before taking action? Identify it and commit to acting based on the self-evident truth you already possess.
?
The lesson refutes a proof that might have caused doubt. Have you ever encountered an intellectual objection to your faith that made you question the self-evident basics? How did you respond? Recognize that the occurrence of iman is self-evident to you. What steps will you take to strengthen that foundational certainty so that sophisticated arguments cannot shake it?

Dependence of Conception on Particular Knowledge
Sanusi argues that conception of something else depends on particular knowledge, and self-evidence applies to occurrence of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the conception of something other than knowledge depends on having particular knowledge of that thing, and that what is self-evident is the occurrence of knowledge, not its conception.

Core Idea
To conceive something, you need specific knowledge of it, not just any knowledge.

This passage from Sanusi clarifies a subtle point about how we come to conceive things. He argues that when we conceive an object, it depends on a particular knowledge of that object, not on the absolute knowledge of knowledge itself. He also distinguishes between the self-evident occurrence of knowledge and the conception of knowledge itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument
Read the first sentence and grasp that the conception of something other than knowledge depends on having a particular knowledge of that thing. Notice the phrase 'there is no meaning to a thing depending on its own occurrence.'
This refutes the idea that knowledge of something depends on knowledge of knowledge itself.
2
Analyze the second argument
Examine the second part which distinguishes what is self-evident. It states that the occurrence of knowledge is self-evident, not the conception of knowledge. Consider the example of the soul: everyone knows they have a soul but not its true reality.
This shows that self-evidence does not extend to the concept of knowledge itself.
3
Connect the two points
Reflect on how these two points together establish that conception requires particular knowledge, and that the self-evident nature of the occurrence of knowledge does not imply self-evidence of the concept of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن توقف تصور غير العلم إنما هو على حصول العلم به أعني علماً جزئياً متعلقاً بذلك الغير، إذ لا معنى لتوقف الشيء على حصوله . وأما الثاني : فلأن البديهي لكل أحد ليس هو تصور العلم بأنه موجود بل حصول العلم بذلك، وهو لا يستدعي تصور العلم به فضلا بداهته، كما عن أن كل أحد يعلم أن له نفساً ولا يعلم حقيقتها .
The dependence of the conception of something other than knowledge is only upon the occurrence of knowledge of it, meaning a particular knowledge pertaining to that other thing, since there is no meaning to a thing depending on its own occurrence. As for the second: because the self-evident to everyone is not the conception of knowledge that it exists, but rather the occurrence of knowledge of that, and it does not require the conception of knowledge of it, let alone its self-evidence, just as everyone knows that he has a soul but does not know its reality.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments. The first point: the conception (tasawwur) of something other than knowledge (i.e., the object of knowledge) depends on having a particular knowledge of that object, not on the absolute knowledge of knowledge itself. The second point: what is self-evident (badihi) to everyone is the occurrence (husul) of knowledge, not the conception of knowledge. For example, everyone knows that he has a soul but does not know its true essence. Thus, the self-evidence of the occurrence of knowledge does not entail the self-evidence of the conception of knowledge.

Key Terms
توقف
تصور
علم جزئي
بديهي
حصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the self-evidence of one type of knowledge (e.g., knowledge of one's own existence) implies the self-evidence of the conception of knowledge itself.
Correction: The passage shows that self-evidence applies to the occurrence of knowledge, not to the conception of knowledge. Everyone knows they have a soul but does not conceive its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the conception (tasawwur) of something other than knowledge depend on?
A) The absolute knowledge of knowledge itself.
B) A particular knowledge pertaining to that other thing.
C) The self-evidence of the existence of knowledge.
D) The conception of knowledge as such.

Key Takeaway
The conception of an object requires particular knowledge of that object, and self-evidence pertains to the occurrence of knowledge, not to the conception of knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You routinely claim to know many things: that you exist, that God exists, that you have a soul. But have you ever stopped to ask yourself: do I truly conceive what these things are, or do I merely have an occurrence of knowledge? Be honest—where in your daily life do you assume self-evident understanding when you only have surface-level awareness? What would change if you pursued genuine conception instead of settling for occurrence?
?
The passage says everyone knows they have a soul but does not know its reality. What do you know about your own soul? Is your knowledge of it limited to the fact of its existence? How does this ignorance sit with you? Are you content to live without conceiving the very thing that is most intimately you?
?
Sanusi argues that conception depends on particular knowledge. Think of something you believe you understand—like faith (iman) or knowledge (ilm). Did you arrive at that understanding through particular, specific reflection, or did you inherit it without examination? If your conception is vague, what specific knowledge do you need to seek to truly conceive it? What step will you take today to move from occurrence to conception?

Distinguishing Actual and Conceptual Existence in the Soul
Answer to the objection by distinguishing actual and conceptual existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between actual and conceptual existence in the soul and how it resolves the objection that knowledge is self-evident.

Core Idea
Knowledge in the soul can be an attribute without being conceived.

The author anticipates an objection: if knowledge is a mental state, its occurrence seems to make its conception self-evident. He responds by distinguishing between the actual existence of a mental state (which results in being described by it) and its conceptual existence (which results in conceiving it). This distinction is crucial for understanding how we can have knowledge without necessarily having a conception of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that if knowledge is a mental state, its occurrence in the soul is itself knowledge and conception, leading to self-evidence and circularity.
Notice that the objection assumes that occurrence of a mental state implies conception of that state.
2
Identify the two types of existence
In the answer, find the two types: original existence (actual attribute) and non-original existence (conceptual existence like a shadow).
These are key terms: وجود متأصل and وجود غير متأصل.
3
Analyze the example of the disbeliever
The example shows that a disbeliever has disbelief as an actual state but does not conceive it; he conceives faith without being described by it. This illustrates the distinction.
Apply this to knowledge: the essence of knowledge can occur as an attribute without conception.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
Conclude that the occurrence of the essence of knowledge is not a conception but an attribute, so the objection's assumption is false and the argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا معنى للعلم إلا وصول النفس إلى المعنى وحصوله فيها، والعلم من المعاني النفسية فحصوله في النفس علم به وتصور له، فإذا كان حصول العلم لوجوده بديهياً . كان تصور العلم به بديهياً، ويلزم منه أن يكون تصور مطلق العلم بديهياً وهو المطلوب، وكذا إذا كان تصور الغير الذي يكتسب به تصور العلم متوقفاً على حصول مطلق العلم كان متوقفاً على تصوره هو الدور. قلنا : قد سبق أن حصول المعاني النفسية في النفس، قد تكون بأعيانها المراد بالوجود المتأصل، وذلك اتصاف بها لا تصور لها، وقد يكون تصورها وهو المراد بالوجود الغير المتأصل بمنزلة الظل للشجر، وذلك تصور لها لا اتصاف بها ألا ترى أن الكافر يتصف بالكفر بحصول الإنكار في نفسه، وإن لم يتصوره، وهو يتصور الإيمان بحصول مفهومه في نفسه غير اتصاف به، فحصول عين العلم بالشيء في النفس لا يكون تصوراً لذلك العلم، كما أن حصول مفهوم العلم بالشيء في النفس لا يكون اتصافاً بالعلم به بل ربما يستلزمه نعم يكون ذلك اتصافاً بالعلم بمفهوم العلم بناء على أن المفهوم حاصل بعينه.
For if it is said: There is no meaning to knowledge except the soul's reaching the meaning and its occurrence in it, and knowledge is among the internal meanings (al-ma'ani al-nafsiyya), so its occurrence in the soul is knowledge of it and a conception of it. Therefore, if the occurrence of knowledge of its existence is self-evident, then the conception of knowledge of it would be self-evident, and it would follow that the conception of absolute knowledge would be self-evident, which is the desired conclusion. Likewise, if the conception of the other thing by which the conception of knowledge is acquired depends on the occurrence of absolute knowledge, it would depend on its own conception, which is circular. We say: It has already preceded that the occurrence of internal meanings in the soul may be by their very selves, which is intended by 'original existence' (al-wujud al-muta'asil), and that is an attribute (ittisaf) of it, not a conception (tasawwur) of it; and it may be their conception, which is intended by 'non-original existence' (al-wujud ghayr al-muta'asil), like the shadow of a tree, and that is a conception of them, not an attribute of them. Do you not see that the disbeliever is described (yattasif) by disbelief by the occurrence of denial in his soul, even though he does not conceive (yataṣawwar) it, and he conceives faith by the occurrence of its concept in his soul without being described by it. So the occurrence of the very essence of knowledge of a thing in the soul is not a conception of that knowledge, just as the occurrence of the concept of knowledge of a thing in the soul is not an attribute of knowledge of it, but rather it may entail it. Indeed, that is an attribute of knowledge of the concept of knowledge, based on the fact that the concept occurs by its very essence.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that knowledge is a mental state (ma'na nafsi), so its occurrence in the soul is itself knowledge and conception of that knowledge. Therefore, if the occurrence of knowledge is self-evident, its conception is also self-evident, and the conception of absolute knowledge follows. Also, if the conception of the object depends on absolute knowledge, that leads to circularity. The answer distinguishes between two types of occurrence in the soul: (1) the actual existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) of the mental state itself, which results in the soul being described (ittisaf) by that state without necessarily having a conception (tasawwur) of it; and (2) the conceptual existence (al-wujud ghayr al-muta'asil) which is a conception without attribution. Examples: a disbeliever has disbelief as an actual state in his soul without conceiving it, and he may conceive faith without being described by it. Similarly, the occurrence of the essence of knowledge (ayn al-ilm) in the soul is not a conception of that knowledge, but rather an attribute. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
معاني نفسية (internal meanings)
وجود متأصل (original existence)
وجود غير متأصل (non-original existence)
اتصاف (attribution)
تصور (conception)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The occurrence of a mental state like knowledge in the soul necessarily implies having a conception of that state.
Correction: The occurrence may be actual existence (attribute without conception) or conceptual existence (conception without attribute). The essence of knowledge occurs as an attribute, not necessarily as a conception.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what does the occurrence of the actual essence (ayn) of knowledge in the soul result in?
A) A conception (tasawwur) of that knowledge.
B) An attribute (ittisaf) by that knowledge.
C) Both conception and attribute simultaneously.
D) Neither conception nor attribute.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of a mental state in the soul does not automatically give us a conception of that state; it may simply be an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so deeply that you considered it an attribute of your soul, yet never truly conceived (tasawwur) its reality—like the disbeliever's denial that he doesn't even conceive? What belief in your life might be similarly unexamined? Will you take time to truly conceive it?
?
You may conceive of faith and its virtues without being described by them—like the disbeliever who conceives faith but lacks it. Where in your life do you have a clear conception (tasawwur) of a virtue without actually being characterized (ittisaf) by it? What would it take to move from conception to true attribution?
?
The occurrence of knowledge in your soul (ayn al-ilm) is not itself a conception of that knowledge. Does your certainty about what you know rest on the raw occurrence of knowledge, or have you worked to conceive it? Are you content with just having knowledge, or will you strive to conceive it clearly and act upon it?

Objection from al-Ghazali's Formulation and Its Refutation
The author refutes an objection from al-Ghazali's formulation regarding the self-evidence of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part objection from al-Ghazali's formulation regarding the acquisition and self-evidence of knowledge, and how the author refutes it by challenging the timing of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of particular knowledge cannot be self-evident without circularity.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from Imam al-Ghazali's epistemological framework. He argues that the acquisition of knowledge leads to an infinite regress unless some knowledge is self-evident, but then he claims even that self-evident knowledge is a judgment (tasdiq) that does not require its components to be self-evident. The author responds by challenging the premise that the judgment is known before acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the objection
Read the passage carefully and note that the objection has two distinct arguments: one about the acquisition of knowledge leading to impossibility, and another about self-evidence of knowledge.
The first argument is labeled 'من الأول' and the second 'والثاني'.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument states that acquiring knowledge of something depends on prior knowledge of that thing, which would require knowing that you know (particular knowledge) before absolute knowledge—an impossibility.
This creates a regress problem for acquired knowledge.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument claims that everyone's knowledge of their own existence is self-evident, and if particular knowledge is self-evident, then absolute knowledge must also be self-evident. Al-Ghazali anticipates that this might be challenged as a tasdiq (judgment) whose self-evidence does not imply self-evidence of its tasawwurat (conceptions), and he counters by saying this tasdiq is self-evident even for those incapable of reflection.
This argument aims to show that some knowledge is necessarily self-evident.
4
Examine the author's response
The author responds by denying the claim that the knowledge 'he is knowing' is a judgment known before acquisition. He says either this is not conceded, or if taken generally, it doesn't help because the occurrence of the possible (knowledge) could happen after acquisition.
This refutation preserves the possibility that knowledge is acquired without prior self-evident knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : في تقرير الإمام ما يدفع الجواب المذكور لأنه قرر : من الأول : بأن اكتساب العلم يتوقف على حصول العلم بالغير، وهو يستلزم إمكان العلم بأنه عالم بذلك الغير، وعلى تقدير وقوع ذلك الممكن يلزم حصول العلم بالعلم الخاص قبل حصول العلم بمطلق العلم وهو محال . واكتساب العلم يكون ملزوماً لتصور الغير الملزوم، لإمكان المحال فيكون محالاً. والثاني : بأن علم كل أحد بأنه عالم بوجوده بديهي، وعلمه بوجود علم خاص، ومتى كان العلم الخاص بديهياً، كان العلم بمطلق العلم بديهياً، ولما كان مظنة أن يقال العلم بأنه عالم تصديق وبداهته، لا تستدعي بداهة تصوراته، لأنه مفسر بما لا يتوقف بعد تصور طرفيه على نظر أشار إلى دفعه بأن هذا التصديق بديهي، بمعنى أنه لا يتوقف على كسب ونظر أصلا، لا في الحكم ولا في طرفيه، سواء جعل تصور الطرفين شطراً له أو شرطاً، وذلك لحصوله لمن لا يتأتى منه النظر والاكتساب كالبله والصبيان . قلنا : العلم بأنه عالم بالشيء تصديق عالم به قبل اكتساب حقيقة العلم فغير مسلم، أو في الجملة فغير مفيد لجواز أن يكون وقوع الممكن بعد الاكتساب .
For if it is said: In the formulation of the Imam (al-Ghazali) there is something that repels the mentioned answer, because he established: First: that the acquisition of knowledge depends on the occurrence of knowledge of the other thing, and it entails the possibility of knowledge that he is knowing of that other thing; and on the supposition of the occurrence of that possible thing, it necessitates the occurrence of knowledge of the particular knowledge before the occurrence of knowledge of absolute knowledge, which is impossible. And the acquisition of knowledge would be a concomitant of the conception of the other thing which is the necessary condition, because of the possibility of the impossible, so it would be impossible. Second: that the knowledge of everyone that he is knowing of his existence is self-evident, and his knowledge of the existence of particular knowledge; and when the particular knowledge is self-evident, knowledge of absolute knowledge would be self-evident. And since it is a place of suspicion that it be said: 'knowledge that he is knowing is a judgment (tasdiq), and its self-evidence does not require the self-evidence of its conceptions (tasawwurat), because it is defined as that which does not depend, after the conception of its two terms, on reflection', he indicated to repel this by [saying] that this judgment is self-evident, in the sense that it does not depend on acquisition and reflection at all, neither in the judgment nor in its two terms, whether the conception of the two terms is made a part of it or a condition; and that is because it occurs to one who cannot engage in reflection and acquisition, such as the feebleminded and children. We say: [The claim that] knowledge that he is knowing of a thing is a judgment (tasdiq) knowing of it before the acquisition of the reality of knowledge is not conceded; or [if taken] in general, it is not useful, because it is possible that the occurrence of the possible thing occurs after acquisition.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from Imam al-Ghazali's formulation and its answer. The objection has two parts. First: The acquisition of knowledge depends on knowledge of the object, which implies possibility of knowing that one knows that object. If that possibility occurs, it would necessitate knowledge of particular knowledge before knowledge of absolute knowledge, which is impossible. Therefore, the acquisition of knowledge would entail an impossibility, so it is impossible. Second: Everyone's knowledge of his own knowledge of his existence is self-evident, and so is his knowledge of particular knowledge. If particular knowledge is self-evident, then absolute knowledge is self-evident. Anticipating the objection that this knowledge is a judgment (tasdiq) whose self-evidence does not imply self-evidence of its component conceptions (tasawwurat), al-Ghazali responds that this judgment is self-evident in a way that does not depend on reflection at all, and it occurs even to those incapable of reflection, like the feebleminded. The author's answer states that the claim that this knowledge (that he is knowing) is a judgment 'knowing of it before acquisition' is not conceded; or if taken generally, it is not useful because the possible occurrence could happen after acquisition.

Key Terms
تقرير الإمام (Imam's formulation)
اكتساب العلم (acquisition of knowledge)
علم خاص (particular knowledge)
علم مطلق (absolute knowledge)
تصديق (judgment/assent)
تصور (conception)
بديهي (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a judgment (tasdiq) is self-evident, then its component conceptions (tasawwurat) must also be self-evident.
Correction: Al-Ghazali's objection assumes this, but he argues that the tasdiq in question is self-evident even without self-evident tasawwurat because it occurs to those incapable of reflection. However, the author's response does not rely on this distinction; instead, he questions whether the judgment is known before acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the objection from Imam's formulation, what would happen if the acquisition of knowledge depends on knowledge of the other thing and that possibility occurs?
A) Knowledge of absolute knowledge would occur before knowledge of particular knowledge.
B) Knowledge of particular knowledge would occur before knowledge of absolute knowledge, which is impossible.
C) Both knowledge of particular and absolute knowledge would occur simultaneously.
D) No knowledge would occur at all.

Key Takeaway
The objection from al-Ghazali is refuted by distinguishing between knowing a judgment before acquisition and after acquisition, preserving the possibility of acquired knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something new, do you silently assume you already know what 'knowing' means? How does al-Ghazali's objection expose that assumption, and are you willing to question it today?
?
Think of a time you confidently claimed to know something before fully understanding it. How does the author's refutation challenge you to re-examine the order of your own learning?
?
If self-evidence of a judgment does not guarantee self-evidence of its parts, what does that mean for beliefs you've never questioned? Will you dare to break them down today?

Objection on Inseparability and Its Clarification
Objection that inseparability is contradicted by separation, answered by redefining 'لزوم' and invoking custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to the definition of theoretical knowledge is answered by clarifying the meaning of 'لزوم' as conditioned by power and custom.

Core Idea
Inseparability in kalam is not absolute; it is conditioned by power and customary occurrence.

This passage addresses a key objection to the definition of theoretical knowledge. The objector argues that even if al-Qadi meant negation of power to separate, separation itself still contradicts inseparability. The answer resolves this by reinterpreting 'لزوم' as fixedness or impossibility of separation by power, and by noting that the connection between reflection and knowledge is based on custom, not necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objector concedes a point but still raises a problem.
The objector says 'سلمنا' (we grant) but 'السؤال باق' (the question remains).
2
Analyze the reinterpretation of 'لزوم'
The answer redefines 'لزوم' as either 'ثبوت' (fixedness) or 'امتناع الانفكاك بالقدرة' (impossibility of separation by power). Understand why this answers the objection.
This shows that 'لزوم' does not mean absolute inseparability but a qualified one.
3
Understand the customary nature of the connection
Notice that the author says theoretical knowledge is not separated from sound reflection 'بطريق جري العادة' (by way of custom), not by logical necessity. Reflect on why the author explicitly avoids saying 'ما يحصل عقيب النظر الصحيح'.
This distinguishes theoretical knowledge from necessary knowledge that follows reflection, like pleasure or pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : سلمنا أن مراد القاضي نفي الاقتدار على الانفكاك . إلا أن السؤال باق بعد - لأن الانفكاك سواء كان مقدوراً أو غير مقدور ينافي اللزوم. قلنا : أراد باللزوم الثبوت أو امتناع الانفكاك بالقدرة على أن يكون آخر الكلام تفسيراً لأوله، وفسر النظري بما يتضمنه النظر الصحيح ، أنه لا ينفك عنه بطريق جري العادة عند حصول الشرائط، ولم يقل ما يحصل عقيب النظر الصحيح لأن من الضروريات ما هو كذلك كالعلم بما يحدث حينئذ من اللذة أو الألم
If it is said: We grant that al-Qadi meant the negation of power to separate. But the question remains afterward, because separation, whether it is within power or not, contradicts inseparability (لزوم). We say: He meant by inseparability (اللزوم) fixedness (الثبوت) or the impossibility of separation by power, such that the end of the speech is an interpretation of its beginning. And he interpreted the theoretical (النظري) as that which includes sound reflection, that it is not separated from it in the customary way when conditions are fulfilled, and he did not say what results from sound reflection, because among necessary things there is what is like that, such as knowledge of what occurs at that time of pleasure or pain.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the definition. The objector concedes that al-Qadi meant the negation of power to separate (نفي الاقتدار على الانفكاك) but still argues that separation itself, whether within one's power or not, contradicts the notion of inseparability (اللزوم). The answer clarifies that 'لزوم' here means either fixedness (ثبوت) or the impossibility of separation by power (امتناع الانفكاك بالقدرة). Thus, the last part of the statement interprets the first. It further explains that the theoretical (النظري) is defined as what includes sound reflection (النظر الصحيح) and that it is not separated from it by way of custom (بطريق جري العادة) when conditions are fulfilled. The author did not say 'what results from sound reflection' because among necessary things there are those that are also produced by reflection, like knowledge of pleasure or pain occurring at that moment.

Key Terms
لزوم
ثبوت
امتناع الانفكاك
النظري
النظر الصحيح
جري العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: لزوم in theoretical knowledge means absolute inseparability, i.e., it is logically impossible to separate.
Correction: لزوم here means fixedness or impossibility of separation by the knower's power, and the connection is based on customary occurrence, not logical necessity.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say about the nature of the inseparability (لزوم) in theoretical knowledge?
A) It is absolute and unchangeable.
B) It is based on customary occurrence, not necessity.
C) It is impossible to separate even by power.
D) It only applies to necessary knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge is inseparable from sound reflection by custom, not by absolute necessity, and this distinguishes it from necessary knowledge that merely follows reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson defines theoretical knowledge as inseparable from reflection by custom, not necessity. In your own life, do you assume that your knowledge of Allah and the unseen comes automatically from reflection, or have you neglected the condition of sound reflection? Examine today whether you truly engage in sound reflection (nazar sahih) or rely on mere habit.
?
The objection assumed that separation contradicts inseparability absolutely. But the answer shows that inseparability is conditioned by power. Is there an area in your faith where you have assumed absolute impossibility of doubt or change, only to realize that your conviction depends on conditions you have neglected? Identify one belief that has become habitual and ask yourself: is it truly grounded in sound reflection, or just custom?
?
The passage distinguishes theoretical knowledge from necessary knowledge that also results from reflection (like pleasure/pain). How often do you mistake the immediate comfort of religious routine (like feeling good after a lecture) for genuine theoretical knowledge that demands ongoing reflection? Commit to one action today that restores sound reflection over emotional satisfaction.

Imam al-Razi's Proof That Concepts Are Necessary
Imam al-Razi argues that conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired and must be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that all conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) is necessary and cannot be acquired through effort.

Core Idea
Concepts cannot be acquired; they are all necessary from the start.

Imam al-Razi presents a rigorous argument to prove that conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) is not something we acquire through effort. He examines two aspects: the object of knowledge and the knower. This lesson walks through his reasoning and shows why he concludes that all concepts are innate in a sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the dense argument sink in before interpreting.
Notice the structure: it starts with the conclusion, then two perspectives.
2
Identify the two perspectives
Al-Razi divides his argument into two parts: from the side of the object (المطلوب) and from the side of the acquirer (الكاسب). Find these sections in the text.
Each perspective aims to show that acquisition (اكتساب) is impossible.
3
Understand the object side argument
For the object: either known absolutely, unknown absolutely, or partially known. In none of these cases can it be sought. Contrast with assents (تصديق).
This shows the object cannot be the target of acquisition.
4
Understand the acquirer side and the reply
The acquirer must know parts, leading to regress. The reply: acquisition is of the sum of parts as a single conceptualization. Also discusses definition by external.
The conclusion is that conceptual knowledge is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واختار الإمام أن ما يحصل من التصورات ضروري لامتناع الاكتساب أما من جهة المطلوب فلأنه إما معلوم مطلقاً فلا يطلب، أو مجهول مطلقاً فلا يمكن التوجه إليه، أو معلوم من وجه دون وجه فلا يمكن طلب شيء من وجهيه بخلاف التصديق، فإنه يطلب بحصول تصوراته، ورد بعض جهات المجهول كاف في التوجه إليه، وأما من جهة الكاسب فلأنه إما جميع الأجزاء أو بعضها، وفيه تعريف بالخارج أو خارج وهو يتوقف على العلم، بالاختصاص المتوقف على تصوره وتصور ما عداه تفصيلا، ورد بأنه مجموع تصورات الأجزاء والكسب تصور مجموعها وأثر الكسب في استحضارها مجموعة مرتبة فهي من حيث تعلق تصور واحد بها محدود، ولشدة اتصال الاعتبارين قد يتوهم اتحادهما، وإنما المتحد مجموع الأجزاء والماهية لا تصوراتها وتصور الماهية وأيضاً تعريف الجزء للماهية إنما يستلزم تعريف شيء من أجزائها لو لم يكن مجرد تمييزها عما عداها تعريفاً لها وكان العلم بها نفس العلم بالأجزاء كما أنها نفسها ، وأيضاً التعريف بالخارج إنما يتوقف على الاختصاص لا العلم به ولو سلم فيكفي تصوره بوجه وتصور ما عداه إجمالاً)
He said: And al-Imam chose that what is obtained of conceptualizations is necessary because acquisition is impossible. As for the aspect of the object (المطلوب): it is either known absolutely and thus not sought, or unknown absolutely and thus cannot be directed towards, or known in one respect and unknown in another, and thus it is impossible to seek it from one of its two aspects, unlike assents (التصديق), for an assent is sought by obtaining its conceptualizations, and repelling some aspects of the unknown is sufficient for directing towards it. And as for the aspect of the acquirer (الكاسب): it is either all the parts or some of them. In it there is definition by something external or external, and it depends on knowledge, which depends on specification, which depends on conceptualizing it and conceptualizing what is other than it in detail. It is replied that it is the sum of conceptualizations of the parts, and the acquisition is the conceptualization of their sum, and the effect of acquisition is in bringing them together in an ordered manner. Thus from the perspective of being related to a single conceptualization, it is limited, and due to the strong connection of the two considerations, one might imagine their unity. Rather, what is unified is the sum of the parts and the essence, not their conceptualizations and the conceptualization of the essence. Also, the definition of the part for the essence only necessitates the definition of a thing from its parts if it were merely distinguishing it from what is other than it as a definition, and knowledge of it would be the same as knowledge of the parts just as it is itself. Also, definition by the external only depends on specification, not knowledge of it. And even if it is granted, then conceptualizing it in one way and conceptualizing what is other than it in a general way is sufficient.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's argument that all conceptual knowledge (تصورات) is necessary (ضروري) because acquisition (اكتساب) is impossible. The argument is divided into two parts: from the side of the object (المطلوب) and from the side of the acquirer (الكاسب). From the object side: the sought concept is either already known (thus cannot be sought), or completely unknown (cannot be directed towards), or known in one aspect and unknown in another—but seeking it from either aspect is impossible. This contrasts with assents (تصديقات), which can be sought by first having their conceptualizations. From the acquirer side: the concept is either a whole composed of parts or parts. The acquirer would need to know the parts in detail, leading to an infinite regress. The answer is that the acquisition is of the sum of parts as a single conceptualization, and the parts are brought together. The rest refines this by discussing definition by external things and the role of specification. The outcome is that conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired and is thus necessary.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
اكتساب
مطلوب
كاسب
ضروري
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that concepts are acquired through reflection or definition.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that acquisition leads to impossibility; therefore, conceptual knowledge must be necessary and not acquired.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, why is conceptual knowledge (تصور) impossible to acquire from the side of the object?
A) Because the object is always unknown.
B) Because the object is either already known or completely unknown, and if partially known, it cannot be sought from one aspect.
C) Because acquisition requires effort which is not possible.
D) Because concepts are innate.

Key Takeaway
According to Imam al-Razi, all conceptual knowledge is necessary because acquisition leads to an infinite regress or impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that every concept you hold is necessary and cannot be acquired. Think of a concept you believed you worked hard to understand—like the oneness of God. Does this mean your effort was in vain? Or does it challenge you to recognize that deep down you already knew it? How will you now approach learning new ideas, knowing that the building blocks are already within you?
?
If conceptual knowledge is necessary, then what is the point of seeking knowledge? The passage says assents can be acquired. Are you spending your energy on building correct assents (like affirmations of faith) or on chasing concepts that are already there? Decide today to focus your study on connecting concepts into true judgments that transform your heart.
?
The argument from the acquirer shows that trying to acquire a concept would require infinite prior steps. Have you ever felt overwhelmed when learning something new, as if you needed to know everything first? Al-Razi tells you that the root concepts are already present. Can you trust that you possess the necessary tools and simply need to arrange them? Will you let go of the anxiety of 'not knowing' and instead actively order what you already have?

Al-Razi's Argument on Necessary Conceptualizations and Responses to Objections
Taftazani restates al-Razi's argument and answers two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that all conceptualizations are necessarily obtained, why acquisition is impossible, and how he responds to two key objections.

Core Idea
Conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired; it must be innate.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic epistemology about the origin of concepts. Al-Razi argues that all concepts are necessarily obtained because acquisition is impossible from both the side of the object and the way of acquisition. The commentator then defends this view against two objections, clarifying the difference between concepts and assents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the argument's structure come through.
Note the terms 'مطلوب' (object) and 'كاسب' (way of acquisition).
2
Identify al-Razi's main argument
Identify the premise: acquisition is impossible from the object side (either known or unknown). Then see the conclusion: all conceptualizations are necessary.
Al-Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the two objections
The first objection: the object could be known in one respect and unknown in another. The second objection: assents are acquired through proof, so why not concepts?
These objections challenge al-Razi's dichotomy.
4
Understand the answers
The answer to the first: seeking from the known aspect is impossible (already had), from the unknown aspect direction is impossible. The answer to the second: in assents, the conceptual parts are known, but the truth value is unknown; for concepts, no prior knowledge exists.
This preserves the distinction between concepts and assents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: اختار الإمام الرازي أن كل ما يحصل من التصورات فهو ضروري لأن الاكتساب يمتنع من جهة المكتسب أعني المطلوب والكاسب أعني طريق اكتسابه . أما الأول: فلأن المطلوب إما أن يكون معلوماً فلا يمكن طلبه واكتسابه لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، أو يكون مجهولاً فلا يمكن التوجه إليه، ثم اعترض أحدهما: أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون معلوماً من وجه فيتوجه إليه مجهولاً من وجه فيطلب. وثانيهما: النقض باكتساب التصديق مع جريان الدليل فيه ـ فأجاب عن الأول: بأنه إما أن يطلب من وجهه المعلوم وهو محال لامتناع تحصيله، أو من وجهه المجهول وهو محال لامتناع التوجه إليه . وعن الثاني: بأن ما يتعلق به التصديق كالقضية أو النسبة معلوم بحسب التصور، فلا يمنع التوجه إليه، ومجهول بحسب التصديق يمتنع فعلا طلب حصوله . وهذا بخلاف التصور، فإن ما يكون مجهولاً بحسب التصور يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً، إذ لا علم قبل التصور
I say: al-Imam al-Razi chose that all that is obtained of conceptualizations is necessary because acquisition is impossible from the perspective of the acquirer—the object (المطلوب) and the acquirer (الكاسب) meaning the way of acquisition. As for the first: because the object is either known and thus its seeking and acquisition are impossible due to impossibility of obtaining what is already present, or unknown and thus cannot be directed towards. Then one of them objected: why is it not permissible that it be known in one respect and directed towards as unknown in another respect and sought? And the second: the objection by the acquisition of assent (تصديق) even though the proof runs therein. So he answered the first: that it either sought from its known aspect, which is impossible due to impossibility of its acquisition, or from its unknown aspect, which is impossible due to impossibility of directing towards it. And regarding the second: that what pertains to assent, such as a proposition or relation, is known in terms of conceptualization, so it does not prevent directing towards it, and unknown in terms of assent, so actually seeking its acquisition is impossible. This is unlike conceptualization, for what is unknown in terms of conceptualization is unknown absolutely, since there is no knowledge before conceptualization.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's (Taftazani) elucidation of Imam al-Razi's argument. It restates the argument from the object side and then presents two objections and their answers. The first objection is that the object might be known in one respect and unknown in another, allowing direction towards it. The answer distinguishes that if one seeks it from the known aspect, it is impossible to acquire what is already had; if from the unknown aspect, one cannot direct towards it. The second objection is that assents (تصديقات) are acquired through proof, yet they involve conceptualization. The answer is that in assents, the subject and predicate are known conceptually, while their relation or truth value is unknown, so one can direct towards the unknown aspect. In contrast, if a concept is unknown, it is unknown absolutely because there is no prior knowledge to base direction. Thus, al-Razi's argument stands: conceptual knowledge is necessary.

Key Terms
أقول
تصور
تصديق
مطلوب
كاسب
اكتساب
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that concepts can be acquired through a process like assents, since assents are acquired through proof.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in assents, the conceptual parts are already known, whereas for concepts themselves, if unknown, they are absolutely unknown with no basis for direction.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's argument as presented, why is it impossible to acquire a concept that is unknown?
A) Because it is already known.
B) Because one cannot direct the mind towards something completely unknown.
C) Because it is impossible to obtain what is already present.
D) Because assents are acquired through proof.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument that conceptualizations are necessary is defended by showing that acquisition is impossible from both the object and the way, and that objections fail to distinguish concepts from assents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a concept you believe you truly understand—like 'justice' or 'God'. How much of that understanding came from your own reasoning, and how much was absorbed from your environment? If al-Razi is correct that all conceptualizations are innate, what does that mean for the concepts you take for granted? Are you willing to examine them as already present within you, or do you fear what you might find?
?
Al-Razi argues that direction towards an unknown concept is impossible. Think of a time you tried to understand something completely new. How did you even begin? Was there a spark of prior knowledge that guided you? If all concepts are necessary, then your deepest confusion might be a veil over what you already know. What would it take to remove that veil?
?
The objection about assents shows that we can seek truth about propositions even when concepts are known. Where in your life do you seek truth about things you already conceptually understand? Are you satisfied with mere assent, or do you actively pursue certainty? If assents can be acquired through proof, what are you doing to acquire true assents in your faith?

Al-Razi's Core Argument on Conceptions
Al-Razi states his thesis and gives the first part of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's foundational argument that all acquired conceptions are necessary because the sought cannot be acquired.

Core Idea
Nothing can be acquired that is either already known or completely unknown.

Al-Razi begins with a bold claim: every conception that occurs is necessary, meaning it cannot be acquired through a voluntary process of seeking. He argues that acquisition is impossible both from the side of the sought and from the side of the method. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp al-Razi's claim and his two-premise proof.
Notice the structure: first a thesis, then two cases for the sought.
2
Identify the two cases
The proof splits the sought into two possibilities: known or unknown. Write down why each leads to impossibility.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If all conceptions are necessary, how can we ever learn new concepts? This sets up the objections in the next lesson.
This is the starting point for a deeper debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اختار الإمام الرازي أن كل ما يحصل من التصورات فهو ضروري لأن الاكتساب يمتنع من جهة المكتسب أعني المطلوب والكاسب أعني طريق اكتسابه. أما الأول : فلأن المطلوب إما أن يكون معلوماً فلا يمكن طلبه واكتسابه لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، أو يكون مجهولاً فلا يمكن التوجه إليه، ثم اعترض أحدهما : أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون معلوماً من وجه فيتوجه إليه مجهولاً من وجه فيطلب. وثانيهما: النقض باكتساب التصديق مع جريان الدليل فيه ـ فأجاب عن الأول : بأنه إما أن يطلب من وجهه المعلوم وهو محال لامتناع تحصيله، أو من وجهه المجهول وهو محال لامتناع التوجه إليه . وعن الثاني : بأن ما يتعلق به التصديق كالقضية أو النسبة معلوم بحسب التصور، فلا يمنع التوجه إليه، ومجهول بحسب التصديق يمتنع فعلا طلب حصوله . وهذا بخلاف التصور، فإن ما يكون مجهولاً بحسب التصور يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً، إذ لا علم قبل التصور، وحاصله أن متعلق التصديق يجوز أن يتعلق به قبل التصديق علم بخلاف متعلق التصور. وأجيب بأن تختار أنه معلوم من وجه ولا نسلم امتناع التوجه حينئذ إلى وجهه المجهول، وإنما يمتنع لو لم يكن الوجه المعلوم من وجوهه واعتباراته بحيث يخرجه عن كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، وذلك كما إذا علمنا أن لنا شيئاً به الحياة والإدراكات فتطلبه بحقيقته أو بعوارضه المميزة له عن جميع ما عداه، على ما هو المستفاد من الحد أو الرسم. فالمجهول المطلوب لا ينحصر في الحقيقة ولا في العارض وما ذكر في المواقف من أن المجهول هو الذات والمعلوم بعض الاعتبارات تحقيق لما هو الأهم، أعني إمكان اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة وتنبيه على أن مجهولية الذات لازمة فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو على الشيءء بحقيقته، وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا التعريف، ولو قصد اكتساب العارض نفسه كان هو مجهولاً بحقيقته، وما ذكر في تلخيص المحصل من أن كلا من الوجه المجهول والمعلوم حاصل لأمر ثالث هو المطلوب؛ إلزام للإمام بما اعترف به من أن المعلوم إجمالاً : معلوم من وجه . مجهول من وجه : والوجهان متغايران أحدهما معلوم لا إجمال فيه، والآخر ليس بمعلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء ظن أن هناك علماً إجمالياً. وإلا فقد ذكر هو في نقد تنزيل الأفكار : أن المطلوب المجهول هو حقيقة الماهية
I say: Imam al-Razi chose that every conception that occurs is necessary, because acquisition is impossible from the perspective of the acquired, namely the sought, and the acquirer, namely the way of its acquisition. As for the first: the sought is either known, so it cannot be sought and acquired due to the impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained, or it is unknown, so direction to it is impossible. Then one of them objected: why may it not be known in one respect and directed to as unknown in another respect and thus sought? And the second objection is the counterexample of acquiring a judgment (taṣdīq) while the proof is running. So he answered the first: that it must be sought either from its known aspect, which is impossible due to the impossibility of obtaining it, or from its unknown aspect, which is impossible due to the impossibility of directing to it. And as for the second: that the thing to which the judgment relates, such as a proposition or relation, is known in terms of conception, so it does not prevent direction to it, and is unknown in terms of judgment, but actually seeking its attainment is impossible. And this is contrary to conception, because what is unknown in terms of conception is absolutely unknown, since there is no knowledge before conception. The upshot is that the object of judgment can have knowledge attaching to it before judgment, unlike the object of conception. And it was answered that we choose that it is known in one respect, and we do not concede the impossibility of directing then to its unknown aspect; it is only impossible if the known aspect is not among its aspects and considerations in a way that removes it from being absolutely unknown. This is like when we know that we have something through which life and perceptions exist, and we seek it by its essence or by its distinguishing accidents from everything else, according to what is derived from definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm). So the unknown sought is not confined to the essence nor to the accident. And what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that the unknown is the essence and the known is some considerations is a verification of what is most important, namely the possibility of acquiring conception in terms of essence, and a reminder that the unknowability of the essence is necessary in what is sought to be conceived, so that even if something is sought by its essence, and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that is by proof (dalīl) not definition. And if one intends to acquire the accident itself, it is unknown in its essence. And what is mentioned in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that each of the unknown and known aspects occurs for a third thing that is the sought is an objection to the Imam based on his admission that what is known in summary is: known in one respect, unknown in one respect. And the two aspects are distinct: one is known without summary, and the other is not known at all, but when they come together in a thing, it is thought that there is summary knowledge. Otherwise, he himself mentioned in Naqd Tanzīl al-Afkār that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about the acquisition of conceptions (taṣawwurāt). It begins by stating Imam al-Razi's view that all acquired conceptions are necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning they cannot be acquired through voluntary acquisition. The reasoning is that acquisition requires the seeker to either already know the sought (impossible, as it is already had) or not know it (impossible to direct the mind to it). An objection is raised: the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, allowing direction and acquisition. A second objection uses the example of acquiring a judgment (taṣdīq), which is possible despite the proof being known. The response to the first objection argues that seeking from the known aspect is impossible (cannot acquire what is already known), and seeking from the unknown aspect is also impossible (cannot direct to the unknown). The response to the second objection distinguishes between conception and judgment: in judgment, the subject matter is known in conception, so direction is possible, but the judgment itself is unknown; this does not apply to conception, where if the thing is unknown in conception, it is absolutely unknown. A further response (from the objector's side) counters that the sought can be known in one respect and unknown in another, using the example of knowing we have a thing that gives life and perception, then seeking its essence or distinguishing accidents. The author then discusses works like al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal to clarify the nature of the unknown and known aspects, concluding that the unknown sought is the essence of the quiddity. The passage is dense and engages with multiple layers of objection and response.

Key Terms
تصور
ضروري
اكتساب
معلوم
مجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we don't know something, we can simply seek it through reasoning.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that if it is completely unknown, we cannot even direct our mind to it, so acquisition is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible to acquire a conception if the sought is known?
A) Because it is impossible to direct the mind to something already known.
B) Because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible.
C) Because the method of acquisition fails.
D) Because the sought is not a conception.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's initial argument reduces the sought to two impossible scenarios, concluding that conceptions must be necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every conception you have is necessary and not acquired by your effort, what does that mean for your sense of control in learning? Will you humble yourself before the One who grants understanding, or will you continue to rely on your own striving as if you produce knowledge yourself? Today, before you study, will you beg Allah to make His truth occur in your heart as a necessary conception?
?
Al-Razi argues that you cannot direct your mind to something completely unknown. Think of a time you were utterly confused about a concept. Did the understanding come only after Allah opened your heart? If all conception is from Him, what desire or pursuit of yours is truly independent of His giving? Will you start each moment of learning with a prayer that feels real—acknowledging your absolute need?

Objections to Al-Razi and His Responses
Two objections and al-Razi's immediate responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections to al-Razi's argument and his responses, including the crucial distinction between conception and judgment.

Core Idea
An object known in one respect can be sought, but judgment acquisition differs from conception.

Having presented his argument, al-Razi now faces two objections. First, the sought might be known in one respect and unknown in another, allowing direction. Second, the acquisition of a judgment (taṣdīq) seems possible even when the proof is known. Al-Razi responds by clarifying the nature of seeking and distinguishing conception from judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the two objections carefully. Note how the first objection introduces the idea of 'known in one respect' and the second uses the example of judgment.
These objections are common critiques of al-Razi's position.
2
Analyze the responses
Al-Razi answers each objection. For the first, he splits seeking into aspects. For the second, he distinguishes the object of conception vs. judgment.
The response to the second objection is key to understanding later epistemology.
3
Compare conception and judgment
Note how the object of judgment can be known in conception, making direction possible, while conception itself cannot be directed to if unknown in conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the first: the sought is either known, so it cannot be sought and acquired due to the impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained, or it is unknown, so direction to it is impossible.
