The Division of Reasoning and Its Core Components
Definition of reasoning, material, form, and their roles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic division of reasoning into correct and invalid based on material and form, and the roles of definition (muʿarrif) and proof (dalīl).

Core Idea
Reasoning's correctness depends on both its building blocks and their arrangement.

The author opens the second investigation by establishing that reasoning, whether seen as the mental movement or the resulting arrangement, must have a proper structure. He introduces two key outcomes: conceptualization (taṣawwur) through definition, and assent (taṣdīq) through proof. The 'material' is the known premises, and the 'form' is the structured configuration that emerges from their arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting that the author first defines reasoning's two possible interpretations.
Pay attention to the words 'mādda' and 'ṣūra'.
2
Identify the two results of reasoning
Notice that reasoning leads either to conceptualization (taṣawwur) via a definition, or to assent (taṣdīq) via a proof.
3
Understand material and form
Relate the terms: the 'present sciences' (known premises) are the material; the resulting configuration is the form.
The author notes a nuance: form is not simply the arrangement but the attribute that makes the whole one.
4
Grasp the main criterion
Learn that if both material and form are sound, reasoning is correct and leads to the desired result; otherwise it is invalid.
This is the core of the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
تقسيم النظر إلى صحيح وفاسد
قوله : (المبحث الثاني: النظر إن صحت مادته وصورته فصحيح وإلا ففاسد) سواء جعلناه نفس الترتيب أو الحركة المفضية إليه يستدعي علوماً مرتبة على هيئة مخصوصة، يسمى الموصل منها إلى التصور معرفاً، وإلى التصديق دليلا، وتكون العلوم أي الأمور الحاضرة مادة لذلك الموصل، والهيئة المحصلة صورة له، وقد يضافان إلى النظر لهذه الملابسة، أو اطلاقا للفكر والنظر على العلوم المرتبة كما في عبارة الإمام، وهذا معنى كلام المواقف : أن لكل ترتيب مادة وصورة ثم الشائع في عبارة البعض أن الصورة هي ذلك الترتيب، إلا أن المحققين على أن الترتيب هو أن يكون لبعض أجزاء ذلك المجموع عند البعض وضع ما، أو جعلها بهذه الحيثية والصورة هي الهيئة العارضة للأجزاء بعد الترتيب بسببها يقال لها إنها واحدة واتفقوا على أنه إن صحت المادة والصورة، فالنظر صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب وإلا ففاسد لا يؤدي إليه، وصحة المادة في المعرف أن يكون المذكور في معرض الجنس جنساً للماهية، وفي معرض الفصل فصلا لها وفي معرض الخاصة خاصة شاملة لازمة وأن يكون المذكور في الحد التام الجنس والفصل القريبين إلى غير ذلك من الشرائط، وفي الدليل أن تكون المقدمات مناسبة للمطلوب، صادقة قطعاً أو ظناً أو فرضاً بحسب المطالب على ما بين في الصناعات الخمس، وصحة الصورة في المعرف أن يقدم الأعم فيقيد بالفصل أو الخاصة بحيث تحصل صورة وجدانية موازية أو مميزة لصورة المطلوب، وفي الدليل أن يكون على الشرائط المعتبرة في الانتاج على ما فصل في أبواب القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل من المنطق، فظهر أن في تقسيمه النظر إلى الصحيح والفاسد باعتبار المادة والصورة تجوزاً فلا يبعد تقسيم إلى الجلي والخفي بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً فإن أجزاء كل من المعرف والدليل قد تكون ضرورية تتفاوت في الجلاء والخفاء وقد تكون نظرية تنتهي إلى الضروري بوسائط أقل أو أكثر وكذا الصورة القياسية للأشكال، وعبارة المواقف ربما توهم اختصاص التجوز بانقسام النظر إلى الجلي والخفي واختصاصه بالدليل دون المعرف وابتناء انقسام النظر إلى الصحيح والفاسد باعتبار المادة والصورة على تفسيره بالترتيب.
The second investigation: Dividing reasoning into correct and invalid. His statement: (The second investigation: reasoning—if its material and form are sound, then it is correct; otherwise it is invalid) whether we consider it as the very arrangement or the movement leading to it, it requires sciences arranged in a specific manner. That which leads from them to conceptualization is called a definition (muʿarrif), and to assent is called a proof (dalīl). The sciences—i.e., the present matters—are the material (mādda) for that which leads, and the resulting configuration (hayʾa) is its form (ṣūra). These two may be attributed to reasoning due to this connection, or by a figurative extension of thought and reasoning to the arranged sciences as in the expression of the Imam. This is the meaning of the words of al-Mawāqif: that every arrangement has a material and a form. Then the common usage in the expression of some is that the form is that arrangement, but the verifiers hold that the arrangement is that some parts of that whole have a certain position in relation to others, or are placed in that respect, and the form is the attribute that occurs to the parts after the arrangement, due to which they are said to be one. They agreed that if the material and form are sound, then the reasoning is sound and leads to the desired result; otherwise it is invalid and does not lead to it. The soundness of material in the definition (muʿarrif) is that what is mentioned in the context of genus be a genus for the essence, in the context of differentia be a differentia for it, and in the context of property be a comprehensive necessary property, and that what is mentioned in the complete definition be the proximate genus and differentia, and other conditions. In the proof (dalīl), the soundness of material is that the premises be appropriate for the desired conclusion, true with certainty, probability, or assumption according to the demands, as explained in the five arts. The soundness of form in the definition is that the more general be presented first and then qualified by the differentia or property so that a direct mental image is produced that corresponds to or distinguishes the image of the desired thing. In the proof, it is that it conform to the conditions considered for production, as detailed in the chapters on syllogism (qiyās), induction (istiqrāʾ), and analogy (tamthīl) from logic. Thus it appears that in his division of reasoning into correct and invalid in terms of material and form, there is a figurative extension. So it is not far-fetched to divide it into clear and obscure also by this consideration, for the parts of both the definition and proof may be necessary, varying in clarity and obscurity, and may be theoretical, ending in the necessary through fewer or more intermediates, and likewise the syllogistic form for the figures. The expression of al-Mawāqif might suggest that the figurative extension is specific to the division of reasoning into clear and obscure, and specific to the proof without the definition, and that the division of reasoning into correct and invalid in terms of material and form is based on interpreting reasoning as arrangement.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It opens with the second investigation: dividing reasoning into correct and invalid based on material and form. The author first clarifies that reasoning, whether defined as the arrangement itself or the mental movement leading to it, requires arranged sciences. The arrangement that leads to conceptualization is called a definition (muʿarrif), and that which leads to assent is called a proof (dalīl). The present sciences (known premises) are the material, and the resulting configuration is the form. He then addresses a nuance: some say form is the arrangement, but the verifiers distinguish between arrangement (tartīb) as the positional relation of parts and form as the resulting attribute that makes the whole one. They agree: if both material and form are sound, the reasoning is sound and yields the desired result; otherwise it is invalid. Next, he details conditions for sound material in definitions: the genus must truly be the genus, the differentia the differentia, and the property comprehensive and necessary; in proofs, premises must be appropriate and true (certain, probable, or assumed). For sound form in definitions, the more general is presented first then qualified; in proofs, the form must meet syllogistic conditions as taught in logic. Finally, he notes that the division into correct/invalid is metaphorical, and thus a division into clear/obscure is also possible, as components vary in clarity. He clarifies that al-Mawāqif's wording might mistakenly restrict this metaphor to the clear/obscure division.

Key Terms
نظر
مادة
صورة
تصور
تصديق
معرف
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that form is simply the order of premises, but the author clarifies that form is the attribute that arises from the arrangement, giving unity.
Correction: Form is not the arrangement itself but the resultant logical structure that makes the whole coherent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two possible interpretations of reasoning (النظر)?
A) The arrangement itself or the movement leading to it
B) Conceptualization or assent
C) Definition or proof
D) Material or form

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is correct only when both its material (premises) and its form (structural arrangement) are sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain you were right. Did you ever pause to check both the accuracy of your information (material) and the logical flow of your reasoning (form)? If not, could your certainty have been misplaced?
?
When you engage in arguments with others, do you tend to focus on winning the point (form) or on the truth of your premises (material)? How does this lesson challenge you to be more balanced?
?
This lesson says that if either material or form is unsound, reasoning is invalid. Is there a belief you hold dear that you have never subjected to this test? What would it cost you to examine its material and form honestly?

Module 15 · Lesson 53

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Conditions for Sound Material and Form
Detailed conditions for sound material and form in definitions and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the specific conditions that make the material and form sound in both definitions and proofs.

Core Idea
Sound material and form have precise criteria in definitions and proofs.

After establishing the general division, the author now explains what 'soundness' entails. For definitions, the material must include proper genus, differentia, and property; the form must present the more general first. For proofs, the premises must be appropriate and true, and the form must follow syllogistic rules. These conditions ensure that reasoning leads to its goal.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditions for definitions
Find the list of requirements for sound material in a definition: genus, differentia, property, and in complete definitions the proximate genus and differentia.
Note the conditions for form: 'present the more general first then qualify'.
2
Read the conditions for proofs
Identify the requirements for material in proofs: premises must be appropriate and true (certain, probable, or assumed). For form: must meet syllogistic conditions.
The author references 'the five arts' and logic chapters.
3
Reflect on the unifying principle
Consider how both material and form must be sound for reasoning to be correct; the conditions ensure this.

Checkpoint
What is required for the material of a proof (dalīl) to be sound?
A) Premises must be appropriate for the conclusion and true
B) The more general must be presented first
C) The terms must be genus, differentia, and property

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a definition or proof without examining its logical soundness? In what area of your life do you most need to apply the conditions from this passage to ensure your reasoning is correct? What step will you take today to verify one of your own arguments?
?
The passage requires that in definitions the more general must come first. In your understanding of Islamic teachings, do you sometimes focus on specific rulings without establishing foundational principles? How can you reorder your learning to build from general to specific?
?
Are there assumptions you hold that you have never questioned whether they are true with certainty, probability, or mere assumption? Which one assumption about your faith or life would you commit to examining using the criteria of sound material? How will you do it by next week?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
4 min
Figurative Extension and Division into Clear and Obscure
Figurative nature of the division and introduction of clear/obscure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical nature of the division into correct/invalid and how it allows a further division into clear and obscure reasoning.

Core Idea
The division into correct/invalid is figurative, leading to a further division into clear and obscure.

The author concludes by noting that the division of reasoning into correct and invalid based on material and form is used figuratively. This opens the door to another division: clear and obscure. He explains that the components of definitions and proofs can vary in clarity (some are necessary, some theoretical) and that the syllogistic forms also differ. He also warns against a possible misinterpretation of al-Mawāqif's words.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the concept of figurative extension and the new division.
Pay attention to 'tajawwuz' (figurative extension).
2
Understand the clear/obscure division
Note that the parts of definitions and proofs can be clear (necessary) or obscure (theoretical, requiring intermediates). The syllogistic forms also vary.
3
Note the caution about al-Mawāqif
The author mentions that al-Mawāqif's wording might wrongly restrict the figurative extension only to the clear/obscure division and to proofs only.
This shows the author's precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author first clarifies that reasoning, whether defined as the arrangement itself or the mental movement leading to it, requires arranged sciences. The arrangement that leads to conceptualization is called a definition (muʿarrif), and that which leads to assent is called a proof (dalīl). The present sciences (known premises) are the material, and the resulting configuration is the form. He then addresses a nuance: some say form is the arrangement, but the verifiers distinguish between arrangement (tartīb) as the positional relation of parts and form as the resulting attribute that makes the whole one. They agree: if both material and form are sound, the reasoning is sound and yields the desired result; otherwise it is invalid. Next, he details conditions for sound material in definitions: the genus must truly be the genus, the differentia the differentia, and the property comprehensive and necessary; in proofs, premises must be appropriate and true (certain, probable, or assumed). For sound form in definitions, the more general is presented first then qualified; in proofs, the form must meet syllogistic conditions as taught in logic. Finally, he notes that the division into correct/invalid is metaphorical, and thus a division into clear/obscure is also possible, as components vary in clarity. He clarifies that al-Mawāqif's wording might mistakenly restrict this metaphor to the clear/obscure division.

Key Terms
تجوز
جلي
خفي
ضروري
نظري
وسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the division into correct/invalid is literal, but the author clarifies it is figurative.
Correction: The division uses figurative language, and it is therefore possible to also divide reasoning into clear and obscure.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it permissible to divide reasoning into clear and obscure?
A) Because the division into correct/invalid is figurative
B) Because reasoning always deals with necessary truths
C) Because al-Mawāqif stated it explicitly
D) Because definitions and proofs are the same

Key Takeaway
Because the division into correct/invalid is figurative, we can also divide reasoning into clear and obscure based on varying clarity of components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your beliefs about Allah, which aspects feel immediately clear (jalī) and which feel obscure (khafī)? Are you content with the obscurity, or are you actively seeking to reduce it through study and reflection? What is one area of faith you will commit to clarifying this week?
?
The passage warns against misinterpreting al-Mawāqif's phrasing. Think of a time you misunderstood a subtle point in your faith because you assumed the classification was literal when it was figurative. How can you guard against such mistakes in the future?
?
Your understanding of Tawheed likely involves a chain of intermediates (wasāʾiṭ) from theoretical premises to necessary certainty. Have you ever examined each link in that chain? Identify one intermediate step you have taken for granted and investigate whether your reasoning there is sound.

The Dispute Over Certainty in Reasoning
Sanusi states the claim and the dispute over certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while there is consensus that reasoning yields opinion, the core debate is whether it yields certainty, and which groups deny that.

Core Idea
Reasoning yields opinion, but certainty is the real theological battleground.

In this passage, Sanusi asserts that correct reasoning yields knowledge, but his commentator quickly clarifies that the real disagreement is over certainty, not opinion. The Sumaniyya and some philosophers denied that reasoning can produce certainty, especially in metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from assertion to clarification.
The passage is short but packs an important distinction.
2
Distinguish opinion from certainty
The commentator says there is no dispute that reasoning yields opinion (ẓann). The dispute is only about certainty (yaqīn). Make sure you see this contrast.
3
Identify the opposing groups
Find the two groups that denied that reasoning yields certainty: the Sumaniyya and a group of philosophers. Note that the Sumaniyya denied it absolutely, while the philosophers denied it specifically in metaphysics.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this matter? If reasoning cannot give certainty, then rational arguments for God's existence or attributes would be undermined. This sets up the need for kalam to defend the value of reason.
This connects to the broader purpose of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الصحيح
قال : والصحيح المقرون بشرائطه يفيد العلم .
قال الإمام : لا نزاع في أن النظر يفيد الظن، وإنما النزاع في إفادته اليقين، فأنكره السمنية مطلقاً وجمع من الفلاسفة في الإلهيات
The correct reasoning. He said: And the correct reasoning accompanied by its conditions benefits knowledge (al-ʿilm). Al-Imām said: There is no dispute that reasoning benefits opinion (al-ẓann), but the dispute is about its benefiting certainty (al-yaqīn); the Sumaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of philosophers [denied it] in metaphysics (al-ilāhiyyāt).
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement and an outline of a dispute. It begins with the author's assertion: correct reasoning, when accompanied by its proper conditions, yields knowledge (al-ʿilm). Then the commentator (referred to as al-Imām) clarifies that there is unanimous agreement that reasoning yields opinion (al-ẓann); the real debate is whether it yields certainty (al-yaqīn). He then identifies two groups that denied that reasoning can produce certainty: the Sumaniyya (a sect from India) denied it entirely, and a group of philosophers denied it specifically in metaphysical matters. This sets up the theological controversy over the epistemological value of rational inquiry.

Key Terms
نظر صحيح
علم
ظن
يقين
سمنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is about whether reasoning yields any knowledge at all (opinion or certainty).
Correction: In fact, there is consensus that reasoning yields opinion; the dispute is only about whether it yields certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the point of agreement and dispute regarding whether reasoning yields knowledge?
A) Dispute about both opinion and certainty
B) No dispute about either
C) Dispute only about opinion
D) No dispute about opinion, dispute about certainty

Key Takeaway
Correct reasoning is accepted to produce opinion, but whether it produces certainty is debated, with the Sumaniyya and some philosophers denying it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Sumaniyya denied reasoning yields certainty in any matter. Are there areas of your life where you treat your beliefs about Allah as mere opinion—hesitating to act on them with full trust?
?
If you are certain about the core truths of Islam, how did you reach that certainty? Was it through reasoning, or did you rely on something else? Does your answer match what this passage teaches about the dispute?

The Four Positions on How Nazar Yields Knowledge
Enumeration of four positions on nazar yielding knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct positions held by different theological schools on how speculative thought (nazar) produces knowledge.

Core Idea
Nazar yields knowledge by habit, creation, generation, or emanation — four different accounts.

The commentator begins by quoting the matn that enumerates the main positions on the mechanism by which nazar gives rise to 'ilm (knowledge). This sets the stage for the detailed discussion that follows, contrasting the Ash'ari, Mu'tazili, and philosophical views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text listing the four positions. Notice the concise phrasing.
This is a direct quote from the matn.
2
Identify each position
Identify the four accounts: habitual (with/without kasb), rational necessity (God's creation), generation (tawlid), and necessity from disposition/emanation.
Associate each with its school.
3
Connect to the broader discussion
Understand that this enumeration frames the entire discussion that follows in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بمعنى حصوله عقيبه عادة مع الكسب أو بدونه، أو لزوماً عقلياً بخلق الله تعالى عندنا وتوليداً عند المعتزلة ووجوباً بتمام الاستعداد وكمال الفيض عند الحكماء. يشير إلى كيفية إفادة النظر للعلم فعندنا هي بخلق الله تعالى العلم عقيب تمام النظر، بطريق إجراء العادة أي تكرر ذلك دائماً من غير وجنوب، بل مع جواز أن لا يخلقه عن طريق خرق العادة وذلك لما سيجيء من استناد جميع الممكنات إلى قدرة الله تعالى واختياره ،ابتداء وأثر المختار لا يكون واجبا تم القائلون بهذا المذهب فرقتان . منهم من جعله بمحض القدرة القديمة من غير أن يتعلق به قدرة العبد وإنما قدرته على إحضار المقدمتين وملاحظة وجود النتيجة فيهما بالقوة ومنهم من جعله كسبياً مقدوراً، وعند المعتزلة بطريق التوليد ومعناه أن يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر كحركة اليد الحركة المفتاح، فالنظر على أي تفسير فسر فعل للناظر يوجب فعلاً آخر هو العلم، إذ معنى الفعل ههنا الأثر الحاصل بالفاعل لا نفس التأثير، ليرد الاعتراض بأن العلم ليس بفعل وكذا النظر على أكثر التفاسير، ألا ترى أن الحركة أيضاً ليست كذلك؟ وقد اتفقوا على أن حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح فعلان الفاعل واحد واحتج بعض أصحابنا بعد إبطال التوليد مطلقاً على بطلانه ههنا بأن تذكر النظر لا يولد العلم اتفاقاً، فكذا النظر ابتداء لاشتراكهما في النظرية، واعترض بأن هذا لا يفيد اليقين، لكونه عائداً إلى القياس الشرعي، وإن ادعى بصورة قياس منطقي بأن يقال : لو كان النظر مولداً لكان تذكره مولداً لعدم الفرق، واللازم باطل وفاقاً، ولا لا الزام ، لأنهم إنما قالوا بالحكم أعني عدم التوليد في الأصل، أعني التذكر لعلة لا توجد في الفرع أعني ابتداء النظر، وهو كونه حاصلا
His statement: 'Meaning that it (knowledge) occurs after it (nazar) habitually, with or without acquisition; or as a rational necessity by the creation of God – according to us (Ash'aris); and as generation (tawlid) according to the Mu'tazila; and as necessary by complete disposition and perfection of emanation according to the philosophers.' He indicates the manner in which nazar yields knowledge. According to us, it is by God creating knowledge after the completion of nazar, by way of the habitual course (jari al-'ada), i.e., it is repeated constantly without any necessity, but with the possibility that He does not create it by way of breaking the habit. This is because all contingent things depend on God's power and choice; the effect of the chosen is not necessary. Then those who hold this doctrine are two groups: some of them make it purely by the eternal power without any involvement of the servant's power – rather, the servant's power is only to bring the two premises and to observe the potential existence of the result in them; and some of them make it acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power. According to the Mu'tazila, it is by way of generation (tawlid), meaning that an act of an agent necessitates another act, like the movement of the hand necessitating the movement of the key. So nazar, on any interpretation, is an act of the nazir that necessitates another act which is knowledge. For the meaning of 'act' here is the effect produced by the agent, not the act of producing itself, to avoid the objection that knowledge is not an act, and similarly nazar according to most interpretations. Do you not see that movement is also not that? And they agreed that the hand's movement and the key's movement are two acts with one agent. And one of our companions, after absolutely invalidating tawlid in general, argued for its invalidity here by saying that the recollection (tadhakkur) of nazar does not generate knowledge by consensus; likewise nazar initially, because they share in being theoretical. And it was objected that this does not yield certainty, because it amounts to legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), even if he claims it to be in the form of a logical syllogism, by saying: If nazar were generative, then its recollection would be generative, since there is no difference; but the consequent is false by consensus. There is no binding force, because they only made the judgment, i.e., the non-generation in the original case, i.e., recollection, due to a cause that does not exist in the branch case, i.e., initial nazar, namely that it is already present...
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a combined classification and argument. It quotes the matn that lists four positions on how nazar (speculative thought) yields 'ilm (knowledge): (1) habitual accompaniment, with or without acquisition; (2) rational necessity through God's creation (the Ash'ari view); (3) generation (tawlid) according to the Mu'tazila; and (4) necessary by complete disposition and perfection of emanation according to the philosophers. The commentator then explains the Ash'ari view in detail: knowledge is created by God after the completion of nazar, following the habitual course (jari al-'ada), constantly without any necessity, and with the possibility of a miracle. This is because all contingent things depend on God's power and choice. Within the Ash'ari school there are two factions: one holds that the act is purely by God's eternal power without any involvement of the servant's capacity (the servant only brings the premises); the other holds that it is acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power. Next, the Mu'tazila view is explained: nazar is an act of the agent that necessitates another act (knowledge), analogous to the hand moving a key. The commentator clarifies that 'act' here means the effect (athar) not the act of influencing (ta'thir), to avoid the objection that knowledge is not an act. Then a refutation of tawlid is presented: one Ash'ari argues that if nazar were generative, its recollection would also be generative, but recollection does not generate knowledge by consensus, so nazar does not. An objection follows: this argument is merely legal analogy, not a logical demonstration, so it does not yield certainty. Even if cast as a logical syllogism, it is not binding because the non-generation in the case of recollection has a cause (it is already present) that is absent in initial nazar.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
عادة
توليد
كسب
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many positions does the matn list for how nazar yields knowledge?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
There are four main positions on how nazar yields knowledge: habitual, creation, generation, and emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use your intellect to reach a conclusion about God, do you ever secretly believe that your reasoning alone is sufficient? How does this lesson’s distinction between habitual, created, generated, and emanated knowledge challenge your unspoken assumptions about the source of your understanding? Commit to acknowledging God’s role in every insight you gain.
?
In your daily life, when you reflect and come to a conviction, do you attribute that insight to your own mental effort or to God’s creation of it in your heart? Which of the four positions (habit, creation, generation, emanation) best describes your actual lived experience—and are you comfortable with the theological implications of that? Take a moment to repent for any intellectual pride.
?
If knowledge after nazar is merely habitual—following God’s custom but not necessary—then your most profound theological understandings are not guaranteed by your method. How does this realization affect your confidence in your own reasoning? Will you now rely more on God’s grace and less on your intellectual ability? Decide one concrete change in your study habits that reflects humility before God.

The Ash'ari View: Habitual Creation and Two Factions
Explanation of Ash'ari view and its internal division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God creates knowledge after nazar by habitual course, and the two factions within the school regarding the servant's role.

Core Idea
God creates knowledge after nazar habitually, without necessity, and scholars differ on whether the servant's power suffices.

Having listed the positions, the commentator elaborates the Ash'ari view: knowledge is created by God after the completion of nazar, following a habitual pattern that is constant but not necessary. Within this view, there are two factions differing on the role of the servant's power (qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the text describing the Ash'ari view. Note the key phrase 'by way of the habitual course' (jari al-'ada).
2
Identify the two factions
Find the two groups: one says it is purely by God's power without servant capacity; the other says it is acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power.
Both agree that God creates knowledge, but differ on human involvement.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Understand why the Ash'aris insist on habit without necessity: all contingent things depend on God's will, and the effect of a free agent is not necessary.

?

?

?

Refuting Tawlid: The Mu'tazili View and the Recollection Argument
Explanation of Mu'tazili tawlid and the Ash'ari refutation via recollection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili theory of tawlid (generation) and the Ash'ari refutation based on the parallel of recollection.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim nazar generates knowledge, but the Ash'aris argue that if it did, recollection would too, which is false.

After explaining the Ash'ari view, the commentator turns to the Mu'tazili position of tawlid (generation), where nazar as an act necessarily produces another act (knowledge). He then presents an Ash'ari argument against tawlid in this specific case, using the analogy of recollection (tadhakkur), along with an objection to that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the section on Mu'tazili tawlid and the subsequent argument. Note the analogy between nazar and recollection.
2
Understand tawlid
Tawlid means an act necessitates another act, like moving a hand moves a key. The Mu'tazila apply this to nazar producing knowledge.
3
Follow the refutation and objection
One Ash'ari argues that if nazar generates knowledge, then recollection (which is also theoretical) should generate it, but consensus says recollection does not. An objection says this is not a logical proof but legal analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بمعنى حصوله عقيبه عادة مع الكسب أو بدونه، أو لزوماً عقلياً بخلق الله تعالى عندنا وتوليداً عند المعتزلة ووجوباً بتمام الاستعداد وكمال الفيض عند الحكماء. يشير إلى كيفية إفادة النظر للعلم فعندنا هي بخلق الله تعالى العلم عقيب تمام النظر، بطريق إجراء العادة أي تكرر ذلك دائماً من غير وجنوب، بل مع جواز أن لا يخلقه عن طريق خرق العادة وذلك لما سيجيء من استناد جميع الممكنات إلى قدرة الله تعالى واختياره ،ابتداء وأثر المختار لا يكون واجبا تم القائلون بهذا المذهب فرقتان . منهم من جعله بمحض القدرة القديمة من غير أن يتعلق به قدرة العبد وإنما قدرته على إحضار المقدمتين وملاحظة وجود النتيجة فيهما بالقوة ومنهم من جعله كسبياً مقدوراً، وعند المعتزلة بطريق التوليد ومعناه أن يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر كحركة اليد الحركة المفتاح، فالنظر على أي تفسير فسر فعل للناظر يوجب فعلاً آخر هو العلم، إذ معنى الفعل ههنا الأثر الحاصل بالفاعل لا نفس التأثير، ليرد الاعتراض بأن العلم ليس بفعل وكذا النظر على أكثر التفاسير، ألا ترى أن الحركة أيضاً ليست كذلك؟ وقد اتفقوا على أن حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح فعلان الفاعل واحد واحتج بعض أصحابنا بعد إبطال التوليد مطلقاً على بطلانه ههنا بأن تذكر النظر لا يولد العلم اتفاقاً، فكذا النظر ابتداء لاشتراكهما في النظرية، واعترض بأن هذا لا يفيد اليقين، لكونه عائداً إلى القياس الشرعي، وإن ادعى بصورة قياس منطقي بأن يقال : لو كان النظر مولداً لكان تذكره مولداً لعدم الفرق، واللازم باطل وفاقاً، ولا لا الزام ، لأنهم إنما قالوا بالحكم أعني عدم التوليد في الأصل، أعني التذكر لعلة لا توجد في الفرع أعني ابتداء النظر، وهو كونه حاصلا
His statement: 'Meaning that it (knowledge) occurs after it (nazar) habitually, with or without acquisition; or as a rational necessity by the creation of God – according to us (Ash'aris); and as generation (tawlid) according to the Mu'tazila; and as necessary by complete disposition and perfection of emanation according to the philosophers.' He indicates the manner in which nazar yields knowledge. According to us, it is by God creating knowledge after the completion of nazar, by way of the habitual course (jari al-'ada), i.e., it is repeated constantly without any necessity, but with the possibility that He does not create it by way of breaking the habit. This is because all contingent things depend on God's power and choice; the effect of the chosen is not necessary. Then those who hold this doctrine are two groups: some of them make it purely by the eternal power without any involvement of the servant's power – rather, the servant's power is only to bring the two premises and to observe the potential existence of the result in them; and some of them make it acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power. According to the Mu'tazila, it is by way of generation (tawlid), meaning that an act of an agent necessitates another act, like the movement of the hand necessitating the movement of the key. So nazar, on any interpretation, is an act of the nazir that necessitates another act which is knowledge. For the meaning of 'act' here is the effect produced by the agent, not the act of producing itself, to avoid the objection that knowledge is not an act, and similarly nazar according to most interpretations. Do you not see that movement is also not that? And they agreed that the hand's movement and the key's movement are two acts with one agent. And one of our companions, after absolutely invalidating tawlid in general, argued for its invalidity here by saying that the recollection (tadhakkur) of nazar does not generate knowledge by consensus; likewise nazar initially, because they share in being theoretical. And it was objected that this does not yield certainty, because it amounts to legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), even if he claims it to be in the form of a logical syllogism, by saying: If nazar were generative, then its recollection would be generative, since there is no difference; but the consequent is false by consensus. There is no binding force, because they only made the judgment, i.e., the non-generation in the original case, i.e., recollection, due to a cause that does not exist in the branch case, i.e., initial nazar, namely that it is already present...
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a combined classification and argument. It quotes the matn that lists four positions on how nazar (speculative thought) yields 'ilm (knowledge): (1) habitual accompaniment, with or without acquisition; (2) rational necessity through God's creation (the Ash'ari view); (3) generation (tawlid) according to the Mu'tazila; and (4) necessary by complete disposition and perfection of emanation according to the philosophers. The commentator then explains the Ash'ari view in detail: knowledge is created by God after the completion of nazar, following the habitual course (jari al-'ada), constantly without any necessity, and with the possibility of a miracle. This is because all contingent things depend on God's power and choice. Within the Ash'ari school there are two factions: one holds that the act is purely by God's eternal power without any involvement of the servant's capacity (the servant only brings the premises); the other holds that it is acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power. Next, the Mu'tazila view is explained: nazar is an act of the agent that necessitates another act (knowledge), analogous to the hand moving a key. The commentator clarifies that 'act' here means the effect (athar) not the act of influencing (ta'thir), to avoid the objection that knowledge is not an act. Then a refutation of tawlid is presented: one Ash'ari argues that if nazar were generative, its recollection would also be generative, but recollection does not generate knowledge by consensus, so nazar does not. An objection follows: this argument is merely legal analogy, not a logical demonstration, so it does not yield certainty. Even if cast as a logical syllogism, it is not binding because the non-generation in the case of recollection has a cause (it is already present) that is absent in initial nazar.

Key Terms
توليد
تذكر
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili tawlid is the only view and that nazar must produce knowledge necessarily.
Correction: The Ash'aris deny tawlid and argue it leads to contradictions, as shown by the recollection parallel.

Checkpoint
What is the key example used in the Ash'ari refutation of tawlid?
A) The movement of a key
B) The recollection of nazar
C) The creation of the world
D) The acquisition of goods

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris refute tawlid by arguing that recollection of nazar does not generate knowledge, so nazar itself cannot generate it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall something you once learned through study (nazar), do you assume it is still true without renewing your investigation? How does the Mu'tazili-Ash'ari debate on tawlid challenge your reliance on memory as a source of knowledge?
?
If recollection (tadhakkur) does not generate new knowledge, what does that mean for your own beliefs that are based on past learning? Are you willing to re-examine them as if for the first time?
?
The Ash'aris argue that nazar, not recollection, is the path to knowledge. In your daily life, how much of your understanding comes from active, fresh investigation versus passively recalling what you already know? What would change if you took this distinction seriously?

Objection to the Tadhakkur Argument and Analysis of Combined Qiyas
Objection and analysis of combined qiyas in the tadhakkur argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the combined qiyas (قياس مركب) and how the objection to the tadhakkur argument is analyzed in the context of kalam epistemology.

Core Idea
A combined qiyas shows that the ruling's cause is disputed, not the ruling itself.

This lesson dives into a sophisticated objection raised against the argument from recollection (tadhakkur). The critic claims the argument only yields a legal analogy, not certainty. The response recasts it as a logical syllogism, but then a deeper analysis reveals it is a 'combined qiyas' where the objector can challenge the common cause. We will walk through this technical exchange and examine two possible reasons why tadhakkur does not generate knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for overall flavor, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from objection to response to further objection.
The passage is dense; do not worry about every detail on first read.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the tadhakkur argument does not yield certainty because it is really a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i). Understand why legal analogy is considered less certain than logical syllogism in kalam.
3
Analyze the combined qiyas structure
The passage explains that this is a 'combined qiyas' (قياس مركب): both parties agree on the ruling (hukm) for the original case (tadhakkur), but each gives a different cause. This allows the opponent to either deny commonality or deny the ruling under certain conditions.
See how the opponent has two lines of attack.
4
Examine the two proposed causes for non-generation
Two causes from Nihayat al-'Uqul are mentioned: (1) impossibility of two agents (اجتماع الموجبين) on one effect, and (2) obtaining what is already obtained (حصول الحاصل). Understand each and how they block generation.
These are alternative explanations for why tadhakkur does not generate knowledge.
5
Connect to broader kalam methodology
Reflect on how this detailed analysis shows the rigor of kalam: arguments are dissected into qiyas types, causes are examined, and multiple possible explanations are considered. This is not simple proof but dialectical engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن هذا لا يفيد اليقين، لكونه عائداً إلى القياس الشرعي، وإن ادعى بصورة قياس منطقي بأن يقال : لو كان النظر مولداً لكان تذكره مولداً لعدم الفرق، واللازم باطل وفاقاً، ولا الزام ، لأنهم إنما قالوا بالحكم أعني عدم التوليد في الأصل، أعني التذكر لعلة لا توجد في الفرع أعني ابتداء النظر، وهو كونه حاصلا بغير قدرة العبد واختياره، حتى لو كان التذكر بقصد العبد لكان مولداً، فيصير الحاصل أن هذا قياس مركب، وهو أن يكون حكم الأصل متفقاً عليه بين المستدل والخصم لكن يعلل عند كل منهما بعلة أخرى، والخصم بين منع وجود الجامع بين الأصل والفرع، إذ ابتداء النظر لا يشارك تذكره في عدم المقدورية وبين منع وجود الحكم في الأصل؛ أي لا نسلم أن التذكر لا يولد العلم عند كونه بقدرة العبد، وإنما ذلك عند كونه سانحاً للذهن من غير قصد العبد فإنه يكون فعل الله تعالى . فلو قلنا : يتولد العلم عنه لكان أيضاً فعل الله تعالى ، فلا يصح تكليف العبد به وفي نهاية العقول ما يشعر بأن علة عدم التوليد في التذكر هو لزوم اجتماع الموجبين على أثر واحد لأنه قال : التذكر عبارة عن وجود علمين أحدهما العلم بالمقدمات التي سبقت، والآخر العلم بأنه كان قد أتى بتلك العلوم، ثم ليس أحد العلمين أولى بالتوليد من الآخر فيلزم أن يكون كل منهما مولداً للعلم بالنتيجة، وهو محال . ويجوز أن يكون العلة هو لزوم حصول الحاصل . إذ التذكر إنما يكون بعد النظر، وقد حصل به العلم ، وعلى هذا لا يكون التذكر مفيداً للعلم أصلاً، بطريق الوجوب، لتمام القابل مع دوام الفاعل
And it was objected that this does not yield certainty, because it reduces to legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), even if he claims it in the form of a logical syllogism by saying: 'If nazar were generative, then its recollection would be generative due to the absence of difference, and the consequent is false by agreement, so the antecedent is false.' But it is not conclusive, because they (the opponents) only stated the ruling, i.e., the absence of generation in the original, i.e., tadhakkur, due to a cause that does not exist in the branch, i.e., initial nazar, which is that it (tadhakkur) occurs without the power and choice of the servant, such that if tadhakkur were by the servant's intent it would be generative. Thus the result is that this is a combined qiyas (qiyas murakkab), which is that the ruling of the original is agreed upon between the prover and the opponent, but each explains it by a different cause. The opponent is between denying the existence of the commonality between the original and the branch, since initial nazar does not share with tadhakkur in the lack of being within power, and between denying the existence of the ruling in the original; i.e., we do not grant that tadhakkur does not generate knowledge when it is within the servant's power; rather that is only when it occurs to the mind without the servant's intent, for then it is an act of Allah the Most High. So if we said that knowledge is generated from it, that too would be an act of Allah, so it would not be valid to charge the servant with it. And in Nihayat al-'Uqul there is an indication that the cause of the absence of generation in tadhakkur is the necessity of the concurrence of two agents upon a single effect, because he said: Tadhakkur is the existence of two knowledges: one is the knowledge of the premises that preceded, and the other is the knowledge that one had indeed come with those knowledges. Then neither of the two knowledges is more entitled to generation than the other, so it necessarily follows that each of them would be generative of the knowledge of the result, which is impossible. And it is possible that the cause is the necessity of obtaining what has already been obtained (husul al-hasil), since tadhakkur only occurs after nazar, and knowledge has already been obtained by it. And on this basis, tadhakkur is not useful for knowledge at all, by way of obligation, due to the completeness of the recipient with the continuance of the agent.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection stating that the argument from tadhakkur does not yield certainty because it is based on legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), not logical. The author then responds by recasting the argument in the form of a logical syllogism: 'If nazar were generative, then its recollection would also be generative, since there is no difference. The consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is false.' However, the opponent counter-objects that the claim (hukm) in the original (tadhakkur) is agreed upon but each party gives a different cause. The opponent can deny the commonality between the original and the branch, because initial nazar shares the attribute of being beyond the servant's power? Actually, the text explains that the opponent denies that the cause (the lack of power of the servant) applies to the branch, or denies the ruling in the original when it is by the servant's power. Then it presents two possible causes for why tadhakkur does not generate: either the impossibility of two agents on one effect (as in Nihayat al-'Uqul), or the impossibility of obtaining what has already been obtained (husul al-hasil). This detailed analysis shows the depth of the kalam methodology.

Key Terms
القياس الشرعي
القياس المنطقي
القياس المركب
العلة
حصول الحاصل
اجتماع الموجبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a combined qiyas is a logical flaw, but it is actually a valid structure in kalam dialectic.
Correction: In a combined qiyas, the ruling is agreed upon but the causes differ; this is not a fallacy but a recognition that the opponent's own reasoning can be used against them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one of the possible causes for why tadhakkur does not generate knowledge, as indicated in Nihayat al-'Uqul?
A) The impossibility of two agents on a single effect.
B) The servant's lack of power over tadhakkur.
C) The fact that tadhakkur is a type of nazar.
D) The agreement between prover and opponent.

Key Takeaway
The tadhakkur argument is defended as a combined qiyas, and the two possible causes—concurrence of two agents and obtaining what is already obtained—illustrate the depth of kalam's causal analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the author shows that your certainty about a logical argument can be undermined when the cause of a ruling is disputed. Where in your own life have you been certain of a conclusion only to realize later that you assumed a shared cause that was not actually shared? What would it look like to step back and examine the causes behind your beliefs, even the ones you hold most firmly?
?
Two possible causes are given for why recollection does not generate knowledge: the impossibility of two agents or obtaining what is already obtained. Think of a time when you tried to achieve something you already had—perhaps a feeling of closeness to Allah, or a spiritual insight. Did your effort actually add anything, or was it a form of 'obtaining what is already obtained'? How can you avoid that redundancy and instead truly seek new growth?
?
The passage distinguishes between what is within your power (like initial nazar) and what is not (like involuntary recollection). This touches on the line between your responsibility and Allah's direct act. Consider a recent situation where you felt frustrated or proud of an outcome. Can you honestly separate what was your effort from what was Allah's decree? What does it mean for your sense of accountability to accept that some things are beyond your control?

Recollection and the Composite Syllogism
The author states the claim and describes the composite syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that recollection is not within human power and how it leads to a composite syllogism.

Core Idea
Recollection is not within our power; otherwise it would be generated.

The author begins by stating that recollection is not within the servant's power. He then explains that if it were, it would be a generated act, which is impossible. This leads to a composite syllogism where the ruling is agreed but the causes differ. In this lesson, we will unpack this initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and syllogism
Read the Arabic passage that asserts recollection is not within the servant's power and introduces the composite syllogism. Let the author's words sink in.
Notice the term 'muwallad' (generated).
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms: recollection (التذكر), generated (مولد), composite syllogism (قياس مركب). Understand their meanings in context.
3
Reflect on the structure
Consider why the author introduces the composite syllogism. What is the 'original case' and what is the 'branch'?
The original case is the beginning of reflection (?), the branch is recollection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بغير قدرة العبد واختياره، حتى لو كان التذكر بقصد العبد لكان مولداً، فيصير الحاصل أن هذا قياس مركب، وهو أن يكون حكم الأصل متفقاً عليه بين المستدل والخصم لكن يعلل عند كل منهما بعلة أخرى، والخصم بين منع وجود الجامع بين الأصل والفرع، إذ ابتداء النظر لا يشارك تذكره في عدم المقدورية وبين منع وجود الحكم في الأصل ؛ أي لا نسلم أن التذكر لا يولد العلم عند كونه بقدرة العبد، وإنما ذلك عند كونه سانحاً للذهن من غير قصد (۱) العبد فإنه يكون فعل الله تعالى . فلو قلنا : - يتولد العلم عنه لكان أيضاً فعل الله تعالى ، : تكليف العبد به
Without the servant's power and choice, such that if recollection were by the servant's intention it would be generated, so the result is that this is a composite syllogism, which is that the ruling of the original case is agreed upon between the arguer and the opponent but is justified by each with a different cause, and the opponent is either by denying the existence of the common link between the original and the branch — since the beginning of reflection does not share with recollection in lack of power — or by denying the existence of the ruling in the original case; meaning we do not concede that recollection does not generate knowledge when it is within the servant's power, but rather that is when it occurs to the mind without the servant's intention, for then it is an act of God. If we say: knowledge is generated from it, it would also be an act of God, burdening the servant with it.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about recollection (tadhakkur) not being within the servant's power. The author begins by stating that recollection is not within the servant's power and choice. If recollection were by the servant's intention, it would be generated (muwallad), but that is not the case. He then explains that this results in a composite syllogism (qiyas murakkab) where the ruling of the original case is agreed upon but each party justifies it with a different cause. The opponent either denies the common link between original and branch (since beginning of reflection does not share with recollection in lacking power) or denies the ruling in the original case. The author clarifies that recollection does not generate knowledge when it is within the servant's power; rather, when it occurs to the mind without intention, it is an act of God. Then he adds that if we say knowledge is generated from it, it would also be an act of God, imposing a burden on the servant.

قياس مركب

Key Takeaway
Recollection is not within human power; if it were, it would be a generated act, leading to a composite syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Opponent's Objections on Recollection
The author presents and refutes the opponent's objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's two possible objections to the composite syllogism and the author's response clarifying that recollection does not generate knowledge when within the servant's power.

Core Idea
The opponent can deny either the common link or the ruling in the original case.

The author now addresses the opponent's potential objections to the composite syllogism. The opponent can either deny that the original case (beginning of reflection) shares the same characteristic of lacking power with the branch (recollection), or deny that the ruling applies to the original case. The author clarifies that recollection does not generate knowledge when it is within the servant's power; it only does so when it occurs without intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the passage describing the opponent's two possible objections. Identify each one.
The first objection is about the common link; the second about the ruling in the original case.
2
Understand the author's reply
The author clarifies that recollection does not generate knowledge when within the servant's power, but only when it occurs without intention (an act of God).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final statement: if generated knowledge were within power, it would still be God's act, burdening the servant. Why is this a problem?
This relates to the Ash'ari principle that only God creates actions.

The author clarifies that recollection does not generate knowledge when it is within the servant's power; rather, when it occurs to the mind without intention, it is an act of God. Then he adds that if we say knowledge is generated from it, it would also be an act of God, imposing a burden on the servant.

Key Terms
جامع
أصل
فرع
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think recollection always generates knowledge regardless of intention.
Correction: The author specifies that recollection generates knowledge only when it occurs without the servant's intention (an act of God), not when intentionally done.

Checkpoint
What is one way the opponent can object to the composite syllogism?
A) Deny the existence of the common link
B) Deny that recollection is a form of knowledge
C) Assert that recollection is always within power
D) Claim that generated acts are not possible

Key Takeaway
The author rejects the opponent's objections by clarifying that recollection only generates knowledge when it occurs without intention, making it an act of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you tried hard to remember something and succeeded. Did you attribute that success to your own effort or to God? How does this lesson's distinction between recollection with and without intention challenge your usual assumption?
?
When you encounter a logical objection to a belief you hold, do you tend to deny the common link or the ruling? Reflect on a specific belief you feel defensive about. What would it mean to honestly engage with the objection as this lesson models?
?
If recollection only generates knowledge when it occurs without your intention, then your own mind is not fully under your control. How does that truth make you feel? Are you willing to admit your lack of power over your own thoughts, and what would change if you did?

Two Causes for Recollection Not Generating Knowledge
Al-Razi cites two causes for recollection not generating knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reasons from al-Razi why recollection (tadhakkur) does not generate knowledge.

Core Idea
Recollection fails to generate knowledge because two equal knowledges cannot both produce one effect.

This lesson examines a key passage from al-Razi's Nihayat al-Uqul where he explains why recollection does not generate knowledge. Al-Razi presents two possible causes: the conjunction of two necessitators and the acquisition of what is already obtained. Understanding these arguments helps clarify the kalam theory of knowledge generation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two causes mentioned by al-Razi.
The passage includes footnote markers (۲) and (۳) from the original edition.
2
Identify the first cause
Look for the reasoning about 'conjunction of two necessitators' (اجتماع الموجبين). Understand why two knowledges cannot both generate the result.
Al-Razi argues that each knowledge would have equal right, leading to impossibility.
3
Identify the second cause
Find the second cause: 'obtaining what has already been obtained' (حصول الحاصل). Why does this make recollection useless?
Recollection occurs after reflection; knowledge is already present, so repetition adds nothing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
See how al-Razi concludes that recollection does not give knowledge by way of obligation (wujub), due to recipient completeness and agent perpetuity.
This confirms that recollection is not a productive epistemic act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نهاية العقول (۲) ما يشعر بأن علة عدم التوليد في يصح التذكر هو لزوم اجتماع الموجبين على أثر واحد لأنه قال : التذكر عبارة عن وجود علمين أحدهما العلم بالمقدمات التي سبقت، والآخر العلم بأنه كان قد أتى بتلك العلوم، ثم ليس أحد العلمين أولى بالتوليد من الآخر فيلزم أن يكون كل منهما مولداً للعلم بالنتيجة، وهو محال . ويجوز أن يكون العلة هو لزوم حصول الحاصل (۳). إذ التذكر إنما يكون بعد النظر، وقد حصل به العلم ، وعلى هذا لا يكون التذكر مفيداً للعلم أصلاً، بطريق الوجوب، لتمام القابل مع دوام الفاعل
And in Nihayat al-Uqul there is an indication that the cause of non-generation in recollection is the necessity of the conjunction of two necessitators on a single effect, because he said: Recollection is the existence of two knowledges: one is the knowledge of the premises that preceded, and the other is the knowledge that he had already attained those sciences. Then not one of the two knowledges has more right to generation than the other, so it follows that each of them generates knowledge of the result, which is impossible. And it is possible that the cause is the necessity of obtaining what has already been obtained. For recollection only occurs after reflection, and knowledge has been obtained by it, and thus recollection is not beneficial for knowledge at all, by way of obligation (wujub), due to the completeness of the recipient with the perpetuity of the agent.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage cites al-Razi's Nihayat al-Uqul to explain why recollection does not generate knowledge. Two possible causes are given. First, the necessity of the conjunction of two necessitators (luzum ijtima' al-mujibayn) on a single effect: recollection involves two knowledges (the premises and the awareness of having attained them), and neither is more entitled to generate the conclusion, so both would generate it, which is impossible. Second, the cause could be the necessity of obtaining what has already been obtained (luzum husul al-hasil), because reflection already produced the knowledge, so recollection would be a repetition without new benefit. Thus, recollection does not give knowledge by way of obligation (wujub) at all, due to the completeness of the recipient and the perpetuity of the agent.

Key Terms
نهاية العقول
اجتماع الموجبين
حصول الحاصل
تولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage suggests that recollection never produces any knowledge at all.
Correction: It specifically argues that recollection does not generate knowledge by way of obligation (wujub) due to the completeness of the recipient and the perpetuity of the agent. It does not deny that recollection may have other benefits or that it can remind one of previously known facts.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is one cause for recollection not generating knowledge?
A) The reflection is incomplete.
B) The recollection involves two equal knowledges necessitating one effect.
C) The agent is not powerful.
D) The knowledge is not present.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that recollection does not generate knowledge because either it involves two equal necessitators (impossible) or it repeats already obtained knowledge (pointless).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you 'remember' a proof or argument you once knew, do you ever treat that recollection as if it were new knowledge, without re-examining the premises? How might that lead you to false certainty?
?
Think of a time you relied on the feeling of 'I know this' from memory, only to realize you never truly understood it. What does this lesson teach you about the difference between genuine knowledge and mere recollection?
?
If recollection cannot generate new knowledge, then how must you approach learning? What must you do differently to ensure you are not just recalling but truly understanding? Will you commit to restudying the premises of your most important beliefs?

Al-Razi's and al-Ghazali's Doctrine on Necessity of Knowledge from Reflection
Al-Razi and al-Ghazali's doctrine on necessity of knowledge from reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's and al-Ghazali's position that reflection entails knowledge necessarily without generation, and how they defend it against the objection that necessity contradicts divine free agency.

Core Idea
Reflection entails knowledge necessarily, but not by generation—only by impossibility of separation.

This passage presents a key Ash'ari doctrine on the relationship between reflection and knowledge. Al-Razi and al-Ghazali argue that reflection necessarily leads to knowledge, but this necessity is not causal generation like the Mu'tazila claim; it is a necessary concomitance that does not impinge on divine freedom. This nuanced position requires understanding the distinction between logical necessity and causal compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms and structure.
The passage includes a doctrine, proof, objection, and answer.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage contrasts three views: Mu'tazili generation, the first doctrine (customary concomitance), and al-Razi's necessity. Identify each.
3
Understand al-Razi's proof
Al-Razi argues that if one knows the world is changing and everything changeable is possible, it is impossible not to know the world is possible. This shows necessary entailment.
The proof uses an example to show logical necessity.
4
Grasp the objection and answer
The objection from al-Mawaqif says necessity contradicts free agency. The answer says necessity here means impossibility of separation, not compulsion, and is compatible with divine choice.
This distinction is crucial for Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مذهب آخـر اختاره الإمام الرازي ، وذكر حجة الإسلام الغزالي : أنه المذهب عند أكثر أصحابنا، وهو أن النظر يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة بطريق الوجوب الذي لا بد منه لكن لا بطريق التوليد على ما هو رأي المعتزلة وهذا ما نقل عن القاضي أبي بكر (۱) وإمام الحرمين (٢) أن النظر يستلزم العلم بطريق الوجوب، من غير أن يكون النظر علة أو مولداً، أو صرح بذكر الوجوب، لئلا يحمل الاستلزام على الاستعقاب العادي، فيصير هذا هو المذهب الأول، وقد صرح الإمام الغزالي، بأن هذا مذهب أكثر أصحابنا والأول مذهب بعضهم، واستدل الإمام الرازي على الوجوب بأن من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن فمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يمتنع أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضروري، وكذا في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات وعلى بطلان التوليد بأن العلم في نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدور الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قـدرتـه فتوجه اعتراض المواقف بأنه لما كان فعل القادر امتنع أن يكون واجباً، فإنه الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، من غير وجوب عليه أو عنه . لا يقال المراد الوجوب بالاختيار على ما سيجيء لأنا نقول فحينئذ يجوز أن لا يقع بأن لا تتعلق به القدرة والاختيار ويكون هذا هو المذهب الأول بعينه . والجواب : أن وجوب الأثر كالعلم مثلاً بمعنى امتناع انفكاكه عن أثر آخر كالنظر لا ينافي في كونه أثراً لمختار جائز الفعل والترك، بأن لا يخلقه ولا
And here is another doctrine chosen by Imam al-Razi, and Huijat al-Islam al-Ghazali mentioned that it is the doctrine of most of our companions: that reflection entails knowledge of the result by way of necessity that cannot be otherwise, but not by way of generation as is the view of the Mu'tazila. And this is what was transmitted from al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni): that reflection entails knowledge by way of necessity, without reflection being a cause or generator, or they explicitly mentioned necessity, so that entailment is not interpreted as customary concomitance, for then this would be the first doctrine. And al-Ghazali explicitly stated that this is the doctrine of most of our companions and the first is the doctrine of some of them. And Imam al-Razi argued for necessity by saying: whoever knows that the world is changing and every changing thing is possible, then with the presence of these two knowledges in the mind, it is impossible not to know that the world is possible, and knowledge of this impossibility is necessary; similarly for all necessary concomitants with their subjects. And on the invalidity of generation: because knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within God's power, and therefore it is impossible for it to occur without His power. Then the objection of al-Mawaqif was directed: since it is an act of a powerful agent, it is impossible for it to be necessary, for He wills it if He wishes and leaves it if He wishes, without any obligation on Him or from Him. It cannot be said: what is meant is necessity by choice as will come later, because we say: then it is possible that it does not occur by not being connected to power and choice, and this would be the first doctrine itself. And the answer: that the necessitation of the effect, such as knowledge, meaning the impossibility of its separation from another effect like reflection, does not contradict it being an effect of a free agent who may act or refrain, by not creating it and not...
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a distinct doctrine regarding the relationship between reflection (nazar) and knowledge. The author states that Imam al-Razi chose a view, supported by al-Ghazali as the majority position among Ash'aris, that reflection entails knowledge necessarily without being a cause or generator. This is contrasted with the Mu'tazili view of generation (tawallud) and also with the first doctrine which might interpret entailment as mere custom. Al-Razi provides a proof: if one knows the world is changing and everything changeable is possible, then it is impossible not to know the world's possibility—this necessity is evident. Similarly for all necessary concomitants. He also argues against generation: knowledge is possible, thus within divine power, so it cannot occur without it. However, an objection from al-Mawaqif arises: if something is necessary, it cannot be an act of a free agent. The objection is that if reflection necessitates knowledge necessarily, then God has no choice. The author explains that the objection is answered by distinguishing necessity as impossibility of separation, not compulsion. That does not conflict with the agent being free to create or not create the effect.

Key Terms
مذهب آخر
الوجوب
التوليد
المعتزلة
القاضي أبو بكر
إمام الحرمين
اعتراض المواقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity implies that God is compelled and has no choice in creating knowledge.
Correction: The necessity is logical entailment (impossibility of separation), not causal compulsion. God freely creates reflection and the knowledge that necessarily follows by His power.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that necessity contradicts free agency?
A) By denying necessity.
B) By saying necessity means impossibility of separation.
C) By saying God is not free.
D) By invoking custom.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi and al-Ghazali affirm that reflection necessarily yields knowledge by logical entailment, not by generation, preserving divine freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed the insight from your reflection to your own intelligence rather than to God who created that knowledge in you? How does this lesson challenge you to change that habit?
?
If it is impossible for knowledge not to follow from correct reflection, then why do you sometimes stop short of fully pondering God's signs? What prevents you from committing to deep reflection?
?
The Ash'ari doctrine says reflection is not a cause but an occasion—God creates knowledge directly. When you reflect on a verse or a proof, do you actively seek God to grant you understanding, or do you rely merely on your mental effort? How can you make your study a prayer?
