The View of al-Razi and al-Ghazali: Reasoning Necessitates Knowledge Necessarily
Taftazani presents al-Razi and al-Ghazali's position on the reasoning-knowledge relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between necessary entailment (wujub) and generation (tawlid) in the relationship between reasoning and knowledge, as advocated by al-Razi and al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Reasoning yields knowledge by necessary entailment, not by causal generation.

Al-Taftazani introduces a second major position within the Ash'ari school on how reasoning produces knowledge. Unlike the Mu'tazila who hold that reasoning generates knowledge (tawlid) as a causal effect, al-Razi and al-Ghazali argue for a necessary but non-causal entailment. This lesson presents their view and its roots in earlier Ash'ari authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the passage and identify the central assertion about how reasoning relates to knowledge. Note the specific terms used: 'wujub' (necessity) and 'tawlid' (generation).
The claim is that reasoning necessitates knowledge necessarily, not by generation.
2
Compare with the Mu'tazilite position
Contrast this view with the Mu'tazilite concept of tawlid. Understand why al-Razi and al-Ghazali reject generation.
Tawlid implies a causal link, which would make knowledge dependent on reasoning as a cause, conflicting with divine power.
3
Note the authorities cited
Identify the scholars named: al-Razi, al-Ghazali, al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani), and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). See how they support the same view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مذهب آخر اختاره الإمام الرازي، وذكر حجة الإسلام الغزالي: أنه المذهب عند أكثر أصحابنا، وهو أن النظر يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة بطريق الوجوب الذي لا بد منه لكن لا بطريق التوليد على ما هو رأي المعتزلة وهذا ما نقل عن القاضي أبي بكر وإمام الحرمين أن النظر يستلزم العلم بطريق الوجوب، من غير أن يكون النظر علة أو مولداً، أو صرح بذكر الوجوب، لئلا يحمل الاستلزام على الاستعقاب العادي، فيصير هذا هو المذهب الأول، وقد صرح الإمام الغزالي، بأن هذا مذهب أكثر أصحابنا والأول مذهب بعضهم، واستدل الإمام الرازي على الوجوب بأن من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن فمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يمتنع أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضروري، وكذا في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات وعلى بطلان التوليد بأن العلم في نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدور الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قدرته فتوجه اعتراض المواقف بأنه لما كان فعل القادر امتنع أن يكون واجباً، فإنه الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، من غير وجوب عليه أو عنه. لا يقال المراد الوجوب بالاختيار على ما سيجيء لأنا نقول فحينئذ يجوز أن لا يقع بأن لا تتعلق به القدرة والاختيار ويكون هذا هو المذهب الأول بعينه. والجواب: أن وجوب الأثر كالعلم مثلاً بمعنى امتناع انفكاكه عن أثر آخر كالنظر لا ينافي في كونه أثراً لمختار جائز الفعل والترك، بأن لا يخلقه ولا ملزومه، لا بأن يخلق الملزوم ولا يخلقه كسائر اللوازم الممكنة، مثل وجود الجوهر لوجود العرض وتحقيقه أن جواز الترك أعم من أن يكون بوسط أو بلا وسط، وأن جواز ترك المقدور لا يمتنع أن يكون مشروطاً بارتفاع مانع هو أيضاً مقدور، وهذا كالمتولدات عند من يقول من المعتزلة بكونها بقدرة العبد، وإنما المنافي له امتناع انفكاكه عن المؤثر بأن لا يتمكن من تركه أصلاً، ولو صح هذا الاعتراض لارتفع علاقة اللزوم بين الممكنات، فلم يكن تصور الابن مستلزماً لتصور الأب، ووجود العرض مستلزماً لوجود الجوهر، إلى غير ذلك، والحاصل أن لزوم العلم للنظر عقلي عندهم، حتى يمتنع الانفكاك كتصور الأب لتصور الابن وعادي عند الأولين حتى لا يمتنع الانفكاك بطريق خرق العادة كالإحراق بالنار، وإلى المذاهب الثلاثة لأصحابنا أشار في المتن بقوله: عادة مع الكسب أو بدونه أو لزوماً عقلياً.
Here is another school chosen by Imam al-Razi, and Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali mentioned: that it is the school of most of our companions, namely that reasoning necessitates knowledge of the conclusion by way of the necessity that is indispensable, but not by way of generation as is the view of the Mu'tazila. And this is what was transmitted from al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Haramayn: that reasoning necessitates knowledge by way of necessity, without reasoning being a cause or a generator, or they explicitly mentioned necessity, lest the necessitation be interpreted as habitual consequence, making this the first school. And Imam al-Ghazali explicitly stated that this is the school of most of our companions, while the first is the school of some of them. Imam al-Razi adduced for necessity that whoever knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is contingent, then with the presence of these two cognitions in the mind it is impossible that he not know that the world is contingent, and knowledge of this impossibility is necessary. Similarly for all necessary concomitants with their principals. And he argued for the falsity of generation by saying that knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within the power of God, so it is impossible for it to occur without His power. Then the objection of al-Mawaqif was raised: since it is the act of a free agent, it is impossible for it to be necessary, for the agent is one who if He wills acts and if He wills refrains, without obligation upon Him or from Him. It cannot be said that what is meant is necessity with choice as will come, because we say then it is permissible that it not occur, by power and choice not attaching to it, and this would be the first school itself. The answer: that the necessity of the effect, like knowledge for example, in the sense of the impossibility of its being separated from another effect like reasoning, does not contradict its being an effect of a free agent who can act or refrain, by His not creating it nor its principal — not by His creating the principal and not creating it — like other possible concomitants, such as the existence of substance for the existence of accident. Its verification: that the possibility of refraining is more general than being with or without an intermediary, and that the possibility of refraining from the act of power does not cease to be conditional upon the removal of a barrier that is also an act of power. This is like generated acts according to those Mu'tazilites who hold they are by the servant's power. What is incompatible with it is the impossibility of separation from the agent such that one cannot abandon it at all. If this objection were valid, the relation of necessary concomitance between possibles would be lifted, so the conception of son would not necessitate the conception of father, nor the existence of accident necessitate the existence of substance, and so on. The upshot is that the necessary concomitance of knowledge to reasoning is rational according to them, such that separation is impossible like the conception of father for the conception of son, and habitual according to the earlier ones such that separation is not impossible by way of breaking habit, like burning by fire. And to the three schools of our companions he alluded in the text with his saying: 'habitually with acquisition or without it, or rational necessary concomitance.'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological debate about how reasoning (nazar) produces knowledge (ilm). It begins by stating the view of Imam al-Razi and al-Ghazali: reasoning necessitates knowledge of the conclusion by a necessary entailment (wujub) that is indispensable, but not by generation (tawlid) as the Mu'tazila hold. This view is attributed to earlier Ash'ari figures like al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni, who also held that reasoning necessitates knowledge by necessity, without being a cause or generator. The concern is that if the entailment were merely habitual, it would collapse into the first school (which views it as customary). Al-Razi's proof for necessity is psychological: if one knows that the world is changing and that all changing things are contingent, it is impossible not to know the world is contingent when both premises are present; this impossibility is self-evident. Against tawlid, he argues that knowledge is possible, so it must be created by God's power, hence cannot occur without it. The objection from al-Mawaqif counters that if reasoning necessitates knowledge necessarily, it would negate divine freedom (the agent can act or not). The author refutes this: the necessity of the effect (e.g., knowledge) does not mean the agent is compelled; rather, God may choose not to create the principal (the knowledge's ground) or may remove the barrier. This is analogous to generated acts among the Mu'tazila. The author then distinguishes two types of necessary concomitance: rational (aqli), where separation is impossible as in the relation of son and father, and habitual (adi), where separation is possible by miracle as in burning by fire. The passage concludes by referencing the three schools of the Ash'aris: (1) habitual without acquisition, (2) habitual with acquisition, (3) rational necessary concomitance. This nuanced discussion defends divine power while maintaining the reliability of rational inference.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
وجوب
توليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of knowledge from reasoning means reasoning is a cause of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi and al-Ghazali explicitly reject that reasoning is a cause (illa) or generator (muwallid); it is a necessary concomitant but not causal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi and al-Ghazali, how does reasoning relate to knowledge?
A) Reasoning generates knowledge through causal production.
B) Reasoning necessitates knowledge by necessary entailment, not generation.
C) Reasoning habitually leads to knowledge but not necessarily.
D) Reasoning is irrelevant to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi and al-Ghazali hold that reasoning necessitates knowledge necessarily, not by generation, maintaining God's exclusive creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about Islam, do you feel that your own intellectual effort is what produces certainty? How does this lesson's claim that knowledge is a gift from God challenge that feeling? What practical step can you take to acknowledge God's role in your understanding?
?
The passage warns against treating the entailment as mere habit. In your daily life, do you tend to see cause-and-effect as just routine, forgetting God's direct involvement? How can this lesson remind you to see God's action behind every result?
?
Al-Ghazali says the view of necessary entailment is the school of most Ash'aris. Does your own view of reasoning align with that? If not, what needs to change in your heart to fully rely on God for knowledge, not on your own reasoning?

Al-Razi's Proof and the Objection from al-Mawaqif
Al-Razi's argument for necessity and the objection from al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's proof that reasoning necessarily produces knowledge and the objection that this conflicts with divine free will.

Core Idea
The impossibility of knowing premises without knowing the conclusion proves necessity, but does it compromise God's freedom?

Al-Razi gives a psychological argument: given the two premises, it is impossible not to know the conclusion. This proves necessity. He also argues against tawlid: knowledge is possible, so it must be created by God, so it cannot occur without His power. However, an objection arises: if reasoning necessitates knowledge, then God is forced to create it, which contradicts His free agency. This lesson presents the proof and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Follow al-Razi's proof step by step
Read the argument: If one knows the world is changing and everything changing is contingent, then with both premises present, it is impossible not to know that the world is contingent. This impossibility is self-evident.
The proof uses logical impossibility to establish necessary entailment.
2
Understand the argument against tawlid
Al-Razi argues that knowledge is possible (mumkin), so it is within God's power; therefore it cannot occur without His power. This refutes the idea that reasoning generates knowledge independently.
3
Grasp the objection from al-Mawaqif
The objection states: If reasoning necessitates knowledge, then God would be obligated to create it, which contradicts His free will. A free agent can act or refrain; necessity would imply compulsion.
This objection challenges the compatibility of necessity with divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مذهب آخر اختاره الإمام الرازي، وذكر حجة الإسلام الغزالي: أنه المذهب عند أكثر أصحابنا، وهو أن النظر يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة بطريق الوجوب الذي لا بد منه لكن لا بطريق التوليد على ما هو رأي المعتزلة وهذا ما نقل عن القاضي أبي بكر وإمام الحرمين أن النظر يستلزم العلم بطريق الوجوب، من غير أن يكون النظر علة أو مولداً، أو صرح بذكر الوجوب، لئلا يحمل الاستلزام على الاستعقاب العادي، فيصير هذا هو المذهب الأول، وقد صرح الإمام الغزالي، بأن هذا مذهب أكثر أصحابنا والأول مذهب بعضهم، واستدل الإمام الرازي على الوجوب بأن من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن فمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يمتنع أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضروري، وكذا في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات وعلى بطلان التوليد بأن العلم في نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدور الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قدرته فتوجه اعتراض المواقف بأنه لما كان فعل القادر امتنع أن يكون واجباً، فإنه الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، من غير وجوب عليه أو عنه. لا يقال المراد الوجوب بالاختيار على ما سيجيء لأنا نقول فحينئذ يجوز أن لا يقع بأن لا تتعلق به القدرة والاختيار ويكون هذا هو المذهب الأول بعينه. والجواب: أن وجوب الأثر كالعلم مثلاً بمعنى امتناع انفكاكه عن أثر آخر كالنظر لا ينافي في كونه أثراً لمختار جائز الفعل والترك، بأن لا يخلقه ولا ملزومه، لا بأن يخلق الملزوم ولا يخلقه كسائر اللوازم الممكنة، مثل وجود الجوهر لوجود العرض وتحقيقه أن جواز الترك أعم من أن يكون بوسط أو بلا وسط، وأن جواز ترك المقدور لا يمتنع أن يكون مشروطاً بارتفاع مانع هو أيضاً مقدور، وهذا كالمتولدات عند من يقول من المعتزلة بكونها بقدرة العبد، وإنما المنافي له امتناع انفكاكه عن المؤثر بأن لا يتمكن من تركه أصلاً، ولو صح هذا الاعتراض لارتفع علاقة اللزوم بين الممكنات، فلم يكن تصور الابن مستلزماً لتصور الأب، ووجود العرض مستلزماً لوجود الجوهر، إلى غير ذلك، والحاصل أن لزوم العلم للنظر عقلي عندهم، حتى يمتنع الانفكاك كتصور الأب لتصور الابن وعادي عند الأولين حتى لا يمتنع الانفكاك بطريق خرق العادة كالإحراق بالنار، وإلى المذاهب الثلاثة لأصحابنا أشار في المتن بقوله: عادة مع الكسب أو بدونه أو لزوماً عقلياً.
Here is another school chosen by Imam al-Razi, and Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali mentioned: that it is the school of most of our companions, namely that reasoning necessitates knowledge of the conclusion by way of the necessity that is indispensable, but not by way of generation as is the view of the Mu'tazila. And this is what was transmitted from al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Haramayn: that reasoning necessitates knowledge by way of necessity, without reasoning being a cause or a generator, or they explicitly mentioned necessity, lest the necessitation be interpreted as habitual consequence, making this the first school. And Imam al-Ghazali explicitly stated that this is the school of most of our companions, while the first is the school of some of them. Imam al-Razi adduced for necessity that whoever knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is contingent, then with the presence of these two cognitions in the mind it is impossible that he not know that the world is contingent, and knowledge of this impossibility is necessary. Similarly for all necessary concomitants with their principals. And he argued for the falsity of generation by saying that knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within the power of God, so it is impossible for it to occur without His power. Then the objection of al-Mawaqif was raised: since it is the act of a free agent, it is impossible for it to be necessary, for the agent is one who if He wills acts and if He wills refrains, without obligation upon Him or from Him. It cannot be said that what is meant is necessity with choice as will come, because we say then it is permissible that it not occur, by power and choice not attaching to it, and this would be the first school itself. The answer: that the necessity of the effect, like knowledge for example, in the sense of the impossibility of its being separated from another effect like reasoning, does not contradict its being an effect of a free agent who can act or refrain, by His not creating it nor its principal — not by His creating the principal and not creating it — like other possible concomitants, such as the existence of substance for the existence of accident. Its verification: that the possibility of refraining is more general than being with or without an intermediary, and that the possibility of refraining from the act of power does not cease to be conditional upon the removal of a barrier that is also an act of power. This is like generated acts according to those Mu'tazilites who hold they are by the servant's power. What is incompatible with it is the impossibility of separation from the agent such that one cannot abandon it at all. If this objection were valid, the relation of necessary concomitance between possibles would be lifted, so the conception of son would not necessitate the conception of father, nor the existence of accident necessitate the existence of substance, and so on. The upshot is that the necessary concomitance of knowledge to reasoning is rational according to them, such that separation is impossible like the conception of father for the conception of son, and habitual according to the earlier ones such that separation is not impossible by way of breaking habit, like burning by fire. And to the three schools of our companions he alluded in the text with his saying: 'habitually with acquisition or without it, or rational necessary concomitance.'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological debate about how reasoning (nazar) produces knowledge (ilm). It begins by stating the view of Imam al-Razi and al-Ghazali: reasoning necessitates knowledge of the conclusion by a necessary entailment (wujub) that is indispensable, but not by generation (tawlid) as the Mu'tazila hold. This view is attributed to earlier Ash'ari figures like al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni, who also held that reasoning necessitates knowledge by necessity, without being a cause or generator. The concern is that if the entailment were merely habitual, it would collapse into the first school (which views it as customary). Al-Razi's proof for necessity is psychological: if one knows that the world is changing and that all changing things are contingent, it is impossible not to know the world is contingent when both premises are present; this impossibility is self-evident. Against tawlid, he argues that knowledge is possible, so it must be created by God's power, hence cannot occur without it. The objection from al-Mawaqif counters that if reasoning necessitates knowledge necessarily, it would negate divine freedom (the agent can act or not). The author refutes this: the necessity of the effect (e.g., knowledge) does not mean the agent is compelled; rather, God may choose not to create the principal (the knowledge's ground) or may remove the barrier. This is analogous to generated acts among the Mu'tazila. The author then distinguishes two types of necessary concomitance: rational (aqli), where separation is impossible as in the relation of son and father, and habitual (adi), where separation is possible by miracle as in burning by fire. The passage concludes by referencing the three schools of the Ash'aris: (1) habitual without acquisition, (2) habitual with acquisition, (3) rational necessary concomitance. This nuanced discussion defends divine power while maintaining the reliability of rational inference.

وجوب
توليد
قادر
إرادة
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's proof shows that reasoning compels God to create knowledge.
Correction: The proof only shows a logical necessity in the relation between premises and conclusion, not a compulsion on God's will.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-Razi's argument for the necessity of knowledge from reasoning?
A) It is a revealed truth from scripture.
B) It is impossible to have the premises and not know the conclusion.
C) It is a matter of habit and custom.
D) It is proven by the generation theory.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves necessity by the impossibility of separating knowledge of premises from knowledge of conclusion, but the objection challenges this as compromising God's free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a clear truth about Allah from the Quran or reason, do you feel compelled to accept it in your heart? Does that compulsory assent make your belief less of a free choice? How does that relate to your personal accountability for that belief?
?
Al-Razi argues that once you know the premises, you cannot not know the conclusion about the world being contingent. Which foundational beliefs about Allah are so clear to you that you feel you cannot deny them? If you can't deny them, are they truly from your own conviction or simply forced? Examine your certainty.
?
The objection claims that a necessary connection between premises and conclusion would make God’s act compelled, undermining His free will. Have you ever felt that rational proofs about Allah (e.g., proofs of His existence) leave no room for personal relationship or freedom? How do you reconcile the necessity of truth with the personal nature of faith?

Rational Judgments

Refuting the Objection: Necessity Compatible with Divine Freedom
Response to the objection and the classification of three Ash'ari schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response reconciles the necessity of knowledge from reasoning with divine free will, and the three schools of thought on this issue.

Core Idea
Necessity of entailment does not compel God; He can decline to create the cause.

The objection argued that necessity implies coercion of God. The response clarifies: the necessity is that if the reasoning (as cause) exists, the knowledge must follow, but God may choose not to create the reasoning at all, preserving His freedom. This is compared to other necessary concomitants like father and son. The passage concludes by summarizing the three Ash'ari positions: habitual with acquisition, habitual without, and rational necessary concomitance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key concept in the response
The response states that the necessity of the effect (knowledge) means its inseparability from its cause (reasoning), not that God is forced to create the cause. God may choose not to create the reasoning or its principal.
This preserves divine freedom: God can refrain from creating the conditions for knowledge.
2
Compare with examples of necessary concomitance
The response gives examples: existence of substance necessitates existence of accident, concept of father necessitates concept of son. These are necessary relations but do not compel the existence of the subject.
3
Distinguish between rational (aqli) and habitual (adi) concomitance
According to the response, the Ash'arites hold that the relation is rational (aqli), meaning separation is impossible (like father-son). The earlier view was habitual, where separation is possible by miracle (like burning by fire).
4
Recognize the three Ash'ari schools
The passage alludes to three schools: habitual with acquisition (kasb), habitual without acquisition, and rational necessary concomitance. Understand the distinctions.
These are listed in the matn: 'ada ma'a al-kasb aw bidunihi aw luzuman aqliyyan'.

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological debate about how reasoning (nazar) produces knowledge (ilm). Rational Judgments
intermediate
10 min
Is the Judgment that Reasoning Yields Knowledge Necessary or Theoretical?
Al-Taftazani addresses whether the judgment is necessary and responds to the charge of circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the epistemological status of the principle that reasoning yields knowledge against charges of circularity.

Core Idea
Disagreement does not disprove necessity; theoretical judgment can avoid circularity.

Al-Taftazani now tackles a critical objection: if the principle that reasoning yields knowledge is claimed to be self-evident, why is it disputed? He responds by showing that necessary truths can vary in clarity, and theoretical truths can be acquired without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: the objector presents a dilemma. If the judgment is necessary, it would be universally clear and undisputed; if theoretical, it would lead to circularity.
Notice the logical force of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two responses
Al-Taftazani gives two responses: either the judgment is necessary (with an explanation for disagreement) or theoretical (acquired by another necessary reasoning).
The Arabic phrases 'قلنا ضروري' and 'أو نظري' mark the two branches.
3
Examine the example
The example 'the world is changing, everything changing is new' shows how theoretical reasoning can yield necessary knowledge without circularity.
The conclusion is 'the world is temporally created' known necessarily.
4
Grasp the refinement
The author concludes that reasoning's effectiveness depends on its soundness and conditions, not its specific form.
The phrase 'بل لصحته وكونه على شرائطه' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قيل: الحكم بأن النظر يفيد العلم إن كان ضرورياً لم يختلف فيه العقلاء، ولكان مثل الحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين في الجلاء، وإن كان نظرياً كان موقوفاً على ما يتوقف عليه، وهو دور معلوم قبل أن يعلم، وهو تناقض قلنا ضروري وقد يقع الاختلاف والتفاوت في الضروريات لتفاوت في الإلف وخفاء في التصور أو نظري ويكتسب بنظر آخر ضروري في المقدمات من غير تناقض كما يقال: في قولنا العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث أنه نظر، وقد أفاد العلم بحدوث العالم ضرورة، فالنظر يفيد العلم ثم أن ذلك ليس بخصوصيته بل لصحته وكونه على شرائطه، فكل نظر يعلم
He said: For if it is said: The judgment that reasoning yields knowledge, if it were necessary, would not differ among rational people, and would be like the judgment that one is half of two in clarity; and if it were theoretical, it would depend on what it depends on, which is a circle of known before it is known, and that is a contradiction. We say: it is necessary, and difference and variation may occur among necessary matters due to variation in familiarity and subtlety in conception; or it is theoretical and is acquired by another reasoning that is necessary in its premises, without contradiction, as it is said in our statement 'the world is changing and everything changing is new' that it is reasoning, and it has yielded knowledge of the temporality of the world necessarily. So reasoning yields knowledge, and that is not due to its specificity but to its soundness and being upon its conditions. So every reasoning that knows.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the epistemological status of the principle that reasoning (nazar) yields knowledge (ilm). The objector argues that if this judgment is necessary (daruri), it would be as obvious as 'one is half of two' and would not be disputed—but it is disputed. If it is theoretical (nazari), it would require a proof, leading to an infinite regress or circle. The answer gives two options: (1) The judgment is necessary, but necessary truths can vary in clarity due to differences in habituation (ulf) or subtlety of conception (khafa' fi al-tasawwur); thus disagreement does not negate necessity. (2) The judgment is theoretical, but it is acquired by another reasoning that is itself necessary in its premises, avoiding circularity. An example is given: the reasoning 'the world is changing, and everything changing is new' yields necessary knowledge of the world's temporality. The author then emphasizes that reasoning's effectiveness depends not on its particular form but on its soundness and fulfillment of conditions. The phrase 'every reasoning that knows' is incomplete, but the point is that correct reasoning leads to knowledge.

Objection and Answer: Does Reasoning Yield Knowledge?
Objection and answer on whether reasoning yields knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that reasoning cannot yield knowledge due to circularity and the detailed answer that resolves it by distinguishing between universal and particular judgments.

Core Idea
The circularity objection dissolves when we distinguish the universal concept from particular instances of reasoning.

In this passage, the author anticipates a powerful objection: if reasoning yields knowledge, the proposition itself cannot be necessary (since people disagree) nor theoretical (because that would require circularity). He then provides two resolutions and clarifies how the apparent circularity is resolved by distinguishing between the universal concept of reasoning and particular instances of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the objector's dilemma: either the judgment that reasoning yields knowledge is necessary (but then why do people disagree?) or theoretical (which would require a prior reasoning, leading to circularity).
Notice the example of 'one is half of two' for necessary judgments.
2
Examine the first resolution
The author first answers that the judgment could be necessary but subject to variation due to familiarity (ulf) or subtlety of conception. Think of how even necessary truths can seem unclear to someone without proper training.
3
Examine the second resolution
Alternatively, the judgment could be theoretical but acquired by another necessary reasoning from its premises, as in the example of the world changing and every changing thing being originated. This shows no circularity.
Notice the phrase 'كل نظر كذلك يفيد العلم' - every reasoning that meets its conditions yields knowledge.
4
Understand the distinction between universal and particular
The author resolves the circularity by distinguishing the unknown dependent judgment (a universal proposition about 'reasoning') from the known depended-upon judgment (a particular instance of reasoning). This is a key logical insight.
The same judgment can be expressed in different ways (e.g., 'the world is originated' vs 'the world is changing') which affects evidentness.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this passage defends the methodology of kalam: reasoning can yield knowledge, and apparent circularities can be resolved by careful logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قيل : الحكم بأن النظر يفيد العلم إن كان ضرورياً لم يختلف فيه العقلاء، ولكان مثل الحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين في الجلاء، وإن كان نظرياً كان موقوفاً على ما يتوقف عليه، وهو دور معلوم قبل أن يعلم، وهو تناقض قلنا ضروري وقد يقع الاختلاف والتفاوت في الضروريات لتفاوت في الإلف وخفاء في التصور أو نظري ويكتسب بنظر آخر ضروري في المقدمات من غير تناقض كما يقال : في قولنا العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث أنه نظر، وقد أفاد العلم بحدوث العالم ضرورة، فالنظر يفيد العلم ثم أن ذلك ليس بخصوصيته بل لصحته وكونه على شرائطه، فكل نظر كذلك يفيد العلم، فالموقوف المجهول هو المهملة المتصلة أو الكلية التي عنوانها مفهوم النظر، والموقوف عليه المعلوم هو الشخصية التي موضوعها ذات النظر المخصوص، وتحقيقه أن لوازم الحكم الواحد قد تختلف باختلاف التعبير عن موضوعه كالحكم بحدوث العالم تعبيراً عنه بالموجود بعد العدم، أو المقارن للحادث، أو المتغير
He said: For it is said: The judgment that reasoning (al-naẓar) yields knowledge (al-ʿilm) — if it is necessary (ḍarūrī), then the rational people would not differ about it, and it would be like the judgment that one is half of two in clarity. And if it is theoretical (naẓarī), then it would depend on that on which it depends, which is a circularity (dawr): something known before it is known, and that is a contradiction. We say: [It is] necessary, and difference and variation occur in necessary matters due to variation in familiarity (al-ulf) and subtlety in conception (taṣawwur). Or [it is] theoretical and is acquired by another necessary reasoning in the premises without circularity, as is said: In our statement 'the world is changing and every changing thing is originated' — that is reasoning, and it has produced knowledge of the origination of the world necessarily. Thus reasoning yields knowledge. Moreover, that is not due to its particularity but due to its soundness and its being upon its conditions. So every reasoning that is like that yields knowledge. Thus the unknown that is dependent is the unquantified (muhmala) or universal (kulliyya) categorical proposition whose subject is the concept of reasoning, and the known that is depended upon is the particular (shakhṣiyya) proposition whose subject is this particular reasoning itself. Its verification is that the concomitants (lawāzim) of a single judgment may differ with the difference of expression concerning its subject, like the judgment of the origination of the world expressed as 'existent after non-existence', or 'concomitant with the originated', or 'changing'.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer sequence. The objector claims that the proposition 'reasoning yields knowledge' cannot be necessary (because rational people disagree and it varies in clarity) nor theoretical (because that would require circularity). The answer provides two possible resolutions:

1. The judgment is necessary in itself, but differences and variations occur due to differences in familiarity (ulf) or subtlety of conception—similar to how even necessary truths can appear unclear to some people who lack proper training.
2. Alternatively, the judgment can be theoretical but is acquired by another necessary reasoning from its premises, without circularity. For example, the syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated' is reasoning that necessarily yields knowledge of the world's origination. So reasoning does yield knowledge, not because of its particularity, but because it is sound and meets its conditions.

The author then clarifies a deeper point: the apparent circularity is resolved by distinguishing between the unknown dependent judgment (e.g., the universal proposition whose subject is the concept of reasoning) and the known depended-upon judgment (the particular instance of reasoning). The same judgment—like 'the world is originated'—can be expressed in different ways (after non-existence, concomitant with the originated, changing), and its evidentness may vary accordingly. This shows that the judgment is not truly circular; it is simply that different expressions highlight different aspects.

Correction: The passage shows that the apparent circularity is resolved by distinguishing the universal claim about 'reasoning' from particular instances of reasoning. The universal claim is known through particular instances, not in a circular way.

Checkpoint

B) Because the unknown dependent judgment is a universal proposition, while the known depended-upon is a particular instance.
C) Because reasoning is not really necessary but only customary.
D) Because the objector's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The objection that reasoning involves circularity is resolved by distinguishing the universal claim about reasoning from the particular instances that ground it.

Does this passage challenge you to be patient with yourself and others when necessary truths are not immediately obvious? The Objection and Defense: Is Nazar Necessary or Theoretical?
Refutation of the claim that 'nazar yields knowledge' is neither necessary nor theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the judgment 'nazar yields knowledge' is neither necessary nor theoretical, and the author's two-part defense.

Core Idea
Nazar yields knowledge: either necessary with possible disagreement, or theoretical with necessary premises.

The author tackles a clever objection: if nazar yields knowledge, that claim must be either necessary or theoretical—but both seem problematic. This passage shows how a kalam scholar handles a logical dilemma by offering two viable solutions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
Read the passage and note the initial formulation: the judgment must be either necessary or theoretical, both are claimed invalid. Understand the two-pronged attack.
The objection is presented as 'taqdir al-su'al'—a hypothetical challenge.
2
Analyze the 'necessary' prong
Examine why the objector says it cannot be necessary: no disagreement among rational people, no variation in clarity. See how the author later challenges this assumption.
The author's final answer allows disagreement due to hiddenness and abstraction difficulty.
3
Analyze the 'theoretical' prong
Understand the circularity argument: proving nazar by nazar is a contradiction. Then follow the counter-objection and the author's detailed response.
The author distinguishes between truth and affirmation, and between implication and knowledge of implication.
4
Summarize the final resolution
The author offers two choices: accept it as necessary (with allowance for disagreement) or as theoretical (proven by a nazar with necessary premises). Note how both avoid the original objections.
The final resolution shows the judgment is defensible either way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقدير السؤال : أن الحكم بأن النظر يفيد العلم إما أن يكون ضرورياً أو نظرياً . وكلاهما باطل . أما الأول : فلأنه لو كان ضرورياً لما وقع فيه اختلاف العقلاء، كسائر الضروريات ولكان مثل قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين في الوضوح من غير تفاوت . لأن التفاوت دليل الاحتمال والاشتباه وهو ينافي الضرورة، وكلا اللازمين منتفى لوقوع الاختلاف، وظهور التفاوت. وأما الثاني : فلأنه لو كان نظرياً لكان إثباته بالنظر، وفيه دور من جهة توقفه على الدليل، وعلى استلزامه المدلول، وهو معنى الإفادة وتناقض من جهة كونه معلوماً لكونه وسيلة، وليس بمعلوم لكونه مطلوباً، وهذا معنى قولهم : إثبات النظر بالنظر تناقض. فإن قيل : معنى إثبات القضية النظرية أن العلم بها يستفاد من النظريات بعلم المقدمات مرتبة فيعلم النتيجة وهذا إنما يتوقف على كون النظر مفيداً للعلم، لا على العلم يذلك، فالموقوف هو التصديق ، والموقوف عليه هو الصدق، وهذا كما أن تصور الماهية مستفاد من الخاصة اللازمة بمعنى أنها تتصور فيتصور وإن لم يعلم الاختصاص واللزوم . قلنا : مبنى الكلام على أن اللازم في القياس هو صدق النتيجة والملزوم صدق المقدمات المرتبة وأما التصديق بالنتيجة أعني العلم بحقيقتها فإنما يستلزمه التصديق بالمقدمات المرتبة وكونها مستلزمة للمطلوب بديهة أو اكتساباً على ما تقرر من أن العلم بتحقق اللازم يستفاد من العلم باللزوم، ويتحقق الملزوم، وهذا بخلاف التعريف بالخاصة فإن اللزوم متحقق بين التصورين حتى لو كان التصديق بالمقدمات مع التصديق بالنتيجة كذلك سقط السؤال. وتقرير الجواب أنا نختار أنه ضروري ولا نسلم امتناع الاختلاف والتفاوت في الضروريات، بل قد يختلف فيها لخفاء في تصورات الأطراف وعسر في تجريدها عن اللواحق المانعة ظهور الحكم، وقد يقع فيها التفاوت لتفاوتها في ذلك وفي كثرة التفات النفس إليها، أو يختار أنه نظري يثبت بنظر مخصوص ضروري المقدمات ابتداء وانتهاء من غير لزوم دور أو تناقض بأن يقال في قولنا العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث أن هذا الترتيب المخصوص أو العلوم المرتبة نظراً إذ لا معنى ثم إنه يفيد بالضرورة العلم بأن العالم حادث ينتج أن نظراً ما له سوى ذلك
The formulation of the question: The judgment that nazar (inference) yields knowledge is either necessary or theoretical, and both are invalid. As for the first: because if it were necessary, then rational people would not have disagreed about it, like other necessary truths, and it would be like our saying 'one is half of two' in clarity without variation. For variation is evidence of possibility and confusion, and that contradicts necessity, and both consequences are negated due to the occurrence of disagreement and the appearance of variation. As for the second: because if it were theoretical, then its proof would be by nazar, and that involves circularity from the perspective of its dependence on the evidence and on its implying the implication, which is the meaning of 'yielding', and a contradiction from the perspective that it is known because it is a means, and not known because it is sought; and this is the meaning of their saying: 'proving nazar by nazar is a contradiction.' If it is said: The meaning of proving a theoretical proposition is that knowledge of it is obtained from theoretical matters by knowledge of the premises arranged, so the conclusion is known, and this only depends on nazar being productive of knowledge, not on knowledge of that, so the dependent is the affirmation (tasdiq) and the dependent-upon is the truth (sidq), and this is like how conception of the essence is obtained from the necessary property, in the sense that it is conceived and then [the property] is conceived even if the specificity and implication are not known. We say: The basis of the discussion is that the necessary in the syllogism is the truth of the conclusion, and that on which it depends is the truth of the arranged premises. As for the affirmation of the conclusion, meaning knowledge of its truth, it is necessitated by the affirmation of the arranged premises and their being implicative of the sought proposition, either self-evidently or by acquisition, according to what has been established: that knowledge of the actuality of the necessary is obtained from knowledge of the implication, and the actuality of that on which it depends [is also obtained]. And this is unlike definition by a property, for the implication is actual between the two conceptions, such that if the affirmation of the premises together with the affirmation of the conclusion were such, the question would drop. And the summary of the answer: We choose that it is necessary, and we do not grant the impossibility of disagreement and variation in necessary truths. Rather, there may be disagreement in them due to concealment in the conceptions of the extremes and difficulty in abstracting them from the accidents that prevent the appearance of the judgment, and variation may occur in them due to their variation in that and in the frequency of the soul's attention to them. Or one may choose that it is theoretical, established by a specific nazar (inference) whose premises are necessary from beginning to end, without circularity or contradiction, as when we say: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated' — that this specific arrangement or the arranged sciences is nazar, for there is no other meaning; then it yields necessarily knowledge that the world is temporally originated, resulting in that there is some nazar besides that.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a complete objection-and-answer argument defending the claim that nazar yields knowledge. It begins by formulating the objection (taqdir al-su'al): the judgment 'nazar yields knowledge' is either necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari), and both options are invalid. The author then proves both invalidity claims. For the first option (necessary), if it were necessary, there would be no disagreement among rational people about it, just as other necessary truths like 'one is half of two' are universally agreed upon with no variation. Variation indicates probability and confusion, contradicting necessity. But we observe disagreement and variation, so it cannot be necessary. For the second option (theoretical), if it were theoretical, proving it would require nazar, leading to circularity (dawr): proving nazar by nazar depends on the very thing being proved, which is a contradiction. The author then presents a counter-objection (fa-in qila): the objector argues that proving a theoretical proposition only requires that nazar be productive of knowledge in reality, not that we know that it is productive. The dependency is between affirmation and truth, not between knowledge and the fact. An analogy is given: conceiving an essence through a necessary property does not require knowing the specificity or implication. The author answers (qulna) by distinguishing between the syllogism's necessary implication (luzum) and the knowledge of that implication. In a syllogism, the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, but the affirmation (knowledge) of the conclusion depends on the affirmation of the premises and their being known to imply the conclusion. This is unlike definition by property, where the implication is already established between the concepts. Therefore, if the objector's analogy held, the question would be settled, but it does not. The final resolution (taqrir al-jawab) offers two solutions: either accept that the judgment is necessary and deny that disagreement and variation cannot occur in necessary truths (explaining that they can due to hiddenness, difficulty in abstraction, and differing attention), or accept that it is theoretical but proven by a specific nazar whose premises are themselves necessary (e.g., 'the world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated'), thus avoiding circularity because the nazar used to prove the principle is different from the principle itself. This completes the defense of nazar's efficacy.

Key Terms

نظري
دور
إفادة
تصديق
صدق
لزوم
مقدمات ضرورية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary truths cannot have any disagreement or variation among rational people.
Correction: The author argues that necessary truths can be subject to disagreement due to hiddenness, difficulty in abstraction, and varying levels of attention.

Checkpoint
According to the final resolution, what are the two possible ways to defend the judgment that nazar yields knowledge?
A) It is necessary, and disagreement in necessary truths is impossible; or it is theoretical, proven by nazar without circularity.
B) It is necessary, and disagreement in necessary truths is possible due to hiddenness; or it is theoretical, proven by a nazar with necessary premises.
C) It is neither necessary nor theoretical, but known by revelation.
D) It is self-evident, and no proof is needed.

Key Takeaway
The claim that nazar yields knowledge can be defended either as a necessary truth (with allowance for disagreement) or as a theoretical truth proven by a nazar with necessary premises.

Objection and Answer on Proving Reasoning by Reasoning
Objection and answer about circularity in proving reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to resolve the circularity objection in proving the value of reasoning by distinguishing between the truth of a proposition and our belief in it.

Core Idea
Truth depends on truth of premises; belief depends on belief in premises and implication.

The objector argues that proving reasoning by reasoning is circular: we need reasoning to prove reasoning. The author shows that this conflates two different dependencies: the truth of a conclusion depends on the truth of premises, while our belief in the conclusion depends on our belief in the premises and their implication. Understanding this distinction clears the charge of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening objection (starting 'فإن قيل') and identify the core circularity claim. Notice the example of definition by a property.
The objector says the proof depends on reasoning being useful, which is what we are trying to prove.
2
Follow the author's response
Read the answer (starting 'قلنا') and spot the key distinction between truth (صدق) and belief (تصديق).
The author redefines 'necessary' and 'necessitating' in terms of truth, not belief.
3
Understand the logical relation
The truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of premises (this is 'لازم' and 'ملزوم'). But belief in the conclusion depends on belief in premises and the implication. The implication can be intuitive or acquired.
This avoids circularity because we are not using the very thing we are proving.
4
Compare with the example of definition
The author contrasts with definition by a property: conceptualization does not require explicit knowledge of implication. Similarly, truth does not require belief in reasoning's utility.
The example helps clarify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: معنى إثبات القضية النظرية أن العلم بها يستفاد من النظريات بعلم المقدمات مرتبة فيعلم النتيجة وهذا إنما يتوقف على كون النظر مفيداً للعلم، لا على العلم بذلك، فالموقوف هو التصديق، والموقوف عليه هو الصدق، وهذا كما أن تصور الماهية مستفاد من الخاصة اللازمة بمعنى أنها تتصور فيتصور وإن لم يعلم الاختصاص واللزوم. قلنا: مبنى الكلام على أن اللازم في القياس هو صدق النتيجة والملزوم صدق المقدمات المرتبة وأما التصديق بالنتيجة أعني العلم بحقيقتها فإنما يستلزمه التصديق بالمقدمات المرتبة وكونها مستلزمة للمطلوب بديهة أو اكتساباً على ما تقرر من أن العلم بتحقق اللازم يستفاد من العلم باللزوم وتحقق الملزوم، وهذا بخلاف التعريف بالخاصة فإن اللزوم متحقق بين التصورين حتى لو كان التصديق بالمقدمات مع التصديق بالنتيجة كذلك سقط السؤال.
If it is said: The meaning of proving a theoretical proposition is that knowledge of it is derived from theoretical matters by knowing the premises arranged, and the conclusion is known. This depends only on reasoning being productive of knowledge, not on knowledge of that. So what is dependent (al-mawqūf) is the belief (al-taṣdīq), and what it depends on (al-mawqūf ʿalayhi) is the truth (al-ṣidq). This is like how the conceptualization of an essence is derived from a necessary property, meaning that it is conceptualized and so it is conceptualized even if the specification and necessity are not known. We say: The basis of the discourse is that what is necessary in the syllogism is the truth of the conclusion, and what necessitates is the truth of the arranged premises. As for the belief in the conclusion, i.e., knowledge of its reality, it is only entailed by the belief in the arranged premises and their being necessary for the sought conclusion, either intuitively (badīhatan) or by acquisition (iktisāban), according to what is established: that knowledge of the existence of the necessary is derived from knowledge of the implication and the existence of the necessitating. This is unlike definition by a property, for the implication is established between the two concepts, so that if belief in the premises [were] with belief in the conclusion, then the question falls away.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that proving a theoretical proposition (like the value of reasoning) by reasoning leads to circularity: the proof depends on reasoning being useful, but that very fact is what we are trying to prove. The author responds by distinguishing between the truth of the conclusion (which depends on the truth of premises) and the belief in the conclusion (which depends on believing the premises and their implication). The truth of the conclusion (its being true) depends on the truth of premises—this is what is meant by 'the necessary is truth, the necessitating is truth.' But the belief in the conclusion requires belief in the premises and in their implying the conclusion. This belief can be intuitive or acquired. The author clarifies that the objector confuses the two: the dependence of the proposition's truth on reasoning's utility is different from the dependence of our belief on that utility. The example of definition by a property shows that even without explicit knowledge of the implication, the conceptualization is achieved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any dependence of a proposition on reasoning constitutes circularity.
Correction: The author shows that dependence of truth (the proposition's being true) is not the same as dependence of belief. Circularity only arises if belief in the conclusion depends on belief in the same reasoning we are trying to prove. Here, the truth of the conclusion depends on truth of premises, which is a different relation.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, what does the truth of a theoretical proposition (صدق القضية النظرية) depend on?
A) The belief in the truth of the premises
B) The truth of the arranged premises
C) The knowledge that reasoning is useful
D) The conceptualization of the essence

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conclusion depends on the truth of its premises, while belief in the conclusion depends on belief in the premises and their implication; confusing these leads to the false charge of circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are convinced of a truth, do you ever confuse the fact that it is true with your personal conviction? Have you ever rejected a truth because you felt uncertain, even though the evidence was sound? Reflect on a specific instance and commit to distinguishing between truth and your emotional state.
?
The author distinguishes between truth and belief. In your own life, are there truths you believe but have not fully internalized? What prevents your belief from matching the truth? What step will you take today to align your belief with that truth?
?
The objection in this passage assumes that proving reasoning is circular. Have you ever avoided seeking knowledge because you thought you needed to already know something? How does this lesson challenge that excuse? Write a resolution to pursue knowledge even when you feel unprepared.

The Intuitive Necessity of Reasoning
Al-Sanusi explains that the necessity of reasoning may be intuitive, with variation due to conceptual difficulties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of reasoning can be considered intuitive (ḍarūrī) even if differences occur.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can vary due to subtlety and abstraction.

The author begins by presenting the first option: that the necessity of reasoning is intuitive. He addresses a potential objection about differences in necessary matters, explaining that such differences arise from concealment in conceptualizations or difficulty in abstraction. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the author's claim.
Read the Arabic passage once and note that the author chooses the intuitive option.
The passage is dense; focus on the key phrases.
2
Understand why differences in necessary matters do not disprove intuition.
The author argues that variation occurs due to conceptual difficulties and variation in mental attention.
This is a defense against a potential objection.
3
Reflect on the role of conceptualization and abstraction in knowledge.
Consider how clarity of concepts and abstraction affect the recognition of necessary truths.
This is a key insight for understanding intuitive knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرير الجواب أنا نختار أنه ضروري ولا نسلم امتناع الاختلاف والتفاوت في الضروريات، بل قد يختلف فيها لخفاء في تصورات الأطراف وعسر في تجريدها عن اللواحق المانعة ظهور الحكم، وقد يقع فيها التفاوت لتفاوتها في ذلك وفي كثرة التفات النفس إليها، أو يختار أنه نظري يثبت بنظر مخصوص ضروري المقدمات ابتداء وانتهاء من غير لزوم دور أو تناقض بأن يقال في قولنا العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث أن هذا الترتيب المخصوص أو العلوم المرتبة نظراً إذ لا معنى ثم إنه يفيد بالضرورة العلم بأن العالم حادث ينتج أن نظراً ما له سوى ذلك، وإن شئنا إثبات القاعدة الكلية على ما ادعاه الآمدي: قلنا معلوم بالضرورة أن هذه الإفادة ليست لخصوصية هذه المادة، بل لصحة النظر المخصوص مادة وصورة وكونه على شرائطه فكل نظر يكون كذلك يفيد العلم وهو المطلوب.
And the formulation of the answer: We choose that it [i.e., the necessity of reasoning] is necessary (ḍarūrī), and we do not concede the impossibility of difference and variation in necessary matters. Rather, they may vary due to concealment in the conceptualizations of the extremes and difficulty in abstracting them from the accidents that prevent the appearance of the judgment, and variation may occur in them due to their variation in that and in the frequency of the mind's attention to them. Or he chooses that it is theoretical (naẓarī), established by a specific reasoning (naẓar makhṣūṣ) whose premises are necessary from beginning to end, without necessitating circularity or contradiction, by saying in our statement 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' that this specific arrangement or arranged sciences [are] a reasoning, for there is no meaning then that it necessarily yields the knowledge that the world is originated, resulting that some reasoning other than that [is needed]. And if we wish to establish the universal principle (al-qāʿida al-kulliyya) according to what al-Āmidī claimed: We say it is known necessarily that this yielding is not due to the particularity of this matter, but to the soundness of the specific reasoning in matter and form and its being according to its conditions. So every reasoning that is like that yields knowledge, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the author's resolution. He offers two options: either the necessity of reasoning is intuitive (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī). If intuitive, then differences in necessary matters are explained by subtlety of concepts or difficulty in abstraction. If theoretical, it can be proven by a specific reasoning whose premises are necessary (e.g., 'the world is changing, everything changing is originated'). This specific reasoning yields knowledge necessarily. Then, to prove the universal principle that all sound reasoning yields knowledge, one can observe that this particular reasoning's effectiveness is not due to its specific content but to its sound form and conditions. Thus, any reasoning meeting these conditions yields knowledge. This avoids circularity because the specific reasoning is known intuitively, while the universal principle is derived from it without assuming the point at issue.

Key Terms
ضروري
تصورات
اللواحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reasoning were intuitive, everyone would agree on its necessity.
Correction: The author explains that due to differences in conceptual clarity and abstraction, people may differ even on necessary matters.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can there be variation in necessary matters?
A) Because necessary matters are not truly necessary
B) Due to concealment in conceptualizations and difficulty in abstraction
C) Because reasoning is theoretical, not intuitive
D) Because human minds are inherently flawed

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge can be intuitive even if not immediately recognized by all, due to conceptual and attentional factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear truth of faith that others around you deny, do you attribute their denial to a lack of sincerity or to a genuine difficulty in conceptualization? What does this lesson demand you examine first in yourself and in them?

?
On a matter you are absolutely certain of, can you now trace the clarity of your understanding to the 'frequency of your mind's attention'? Are you willing to give that same sustained attention to other truths you find difficult, or do you excuse yourself with 'it's not intuitive'?

The Theoretical Proof of Reasoning's Necessity
Al-Sanusi explains the theoretical proof of reasoning's necessity and the derivation of a universal principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of reasoning can be proven theoretically using a specific reasoning with necessary premises, and how this establishes a universal principle.

Core Idea
A single sound reasoning with necessary premises proves the universal efficacy of reasoning.

Having presented the intuitive option, the author now considers the theoretical alternative. He shows that even if reasoning's necessity is not intuitive, it can be proven by a specific argument whose premises are necessarily known. This argument also grounds the universal principle that any sound reasoning yields knowledge, avoiding circularity.

The example is a standard kalam argument.
3
Understand how the universal principle is derived from the specific example.
The author argues that the effectiveness is due to form and conditions, not specific content.
This avoids circularity by relying on an intuitive specific reasoning.
4
Observe how circularity is avoided.
The specific reasoning itself is known intuitively, so no circular inference is needed.
This is the key insight for the theoretical option.

Resolving the Objection of Proving a Thing by Itself
The author resolves al-Rāzī's objection by distinguishing essence from consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between essence (dhāt) and consideration (iʿtibār) resolves the objection that proving all reasoning by one instance constitutes proving a thing by itself.

Core Idea
The same thing can be both known and unknown under different aspects.

Imām al-Rāzī objected that Imām al-Ḥaramayn's argument proves a thing by itself, which is contradictory. The author resolves this by showing that the same reasoning can be considered in two ways: in its essence as a known means, and as an instance of reasoning itself as an unknown conclusion. This distinction avoids circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from objection to answer.
Pay attention to the terms 'dhāt' and 'iʿtibār'.
2
Identify the two considerations
The answer says the same thing in terms of essence can differ in terms of consideration. Find where this distinction is applied to reasoning itself.
Look for 'min ḥaythu dhātuhu' vs. 'min ḥaythu kawnihi'.
3
Understand the particular vs. universal propositions
The detail explains that the unknown sought is the universal proposition about all reasoning, while the known premise is a particular proposition about a specific instance of reasoning.
The particular proposition is about a concrete reasoning, e.g., 'the world is changing...'.
4
See why circularity is avoided
Because the thing (reasoning) is considered under different aspects, it is not the same under the same consideration. Thus, it is not prior to itself under the same aspect.
Circularity would require the same thing to be both premise and conclusion under the same consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا ما قال إمام الحرمين: إنه لا يعد في إثبات جميع أنواع النظر بنوع منها يثبت نفسه وغيره، إلا أنه لما اعترف بإثبات الشيء بنفسه اعترض الإمام الرازي بأن فيه تناقضاً وتقدماً للشيء على نفسه. وجوابه أن نفس الشيء بحسب الذات قد تغايره بحسب الاعتبار فتخالفه في الأحكام كهذا الذي أثبتنا به كون كل نظر مفيداً للحكم، فإنه من حيث ذاته وسيلة ومتقدم و معلوم، ومن حيث كونه من أفراد النظر مطلوب ومتأخر ومجهول - وتفصيله أن الموقوف المجهول المطلوب بالنظر هو القضية الموجبة المهملة أو الموجبة الكلية، التي عنوان موضوعها مفهوم النظر، أعني قولنا النظر يفيد العلم، أو كل نظر مقرون بشرائطه يفيد العلم والموقوف عليه المعلوم بديهة هو القضية الشخصية التي موضوعها ذات النظر المخصوص، أعني قولنا: العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث يفيد العلم بأن العالم حادث من غير اعتبار كون هذا الموضوع من أفراد النظر فلا يكون الشيء الواحد بالذات والاعتبار متقدماً على نفسه، ومعلوماً حين ما ليس بمعلوم، ليلزم الدور والتناقض.
And this is what Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: That it is not considered [circular] in proving all kinds of reasoning by one kind of it, which proves itself and others. But when he conceded the proof of a thing by itself, Imām al-Rāzī objected that there is contradiction and priority of a thing over itself. Its answer: The very same thing in terms of essence (dhāt) may differ in terms of consideration (iʿtibār), and thus differ in judgments. Like this [reasoning] by which we have established that every reasoning is productive of judgment. For it, in terms of its essence, is a means, prior, and known; and in terms of its being an individual of reasoning, it is sought, posterior, and unknown. The detail: The unknown sought-by-reasoning (al-mawqūf al-majhūl al-maṭlūb bi-l-naẓar) is the affirmative particular proposition or the affirmative universal proposition, whose subject term is the concept of reasoning, i.e., our statement 'reasoning yields knowledge' or 'every reasoning accompanied by its conditions yields knowledge.' And what it depends on, known intuitively, is the particular proposition whose subject is the very specific reasoning, i.e., our statement 'the world is changing and everything changing is originated yields knowledge that the world is originated' without considering that this subject is an individual of reasoning. So the one thing in terms of essence and consideration is not prior to itself, nor known when it is not known, to necessitate circularity and contradiction.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection by Imām al-Rāzī against Imām al-Ḥaramayn's position that one kind of reasoning can prove all kinds, including itself. Al-Rāzī objects that this is proving a thing by itself, which is contradictory. The author resolves this by distinguishing between two considerations (iʿtibār) of the same thing. The specific reasoning (e.g., 'the world is changing…') when considered in its essence is a means and known intuitively. But when considered as an instance of reasoning (i.e., as a member of the class 'reasoning'), it is something sought and unknown. So the particular proposition (about this specific reasoning) is the known premise, while the universal proposition (about all reasoning) is the unknown conclusion. Thus, the thing is not the same under the same consideration, avoiding circularity and self-priority.

Key Terms
إمام الحرمين
الإمام الرازي
الذات
الاعتبار
القضية الشخصية
القضية الكلية
النظر المخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving a thing by itself is always invalid.
Correction: The author shows it is valid if the thing is considered under different aspects, not under the same consideration.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction used to resolve al-Rāzī's objection that proving all reasoning by one instance is self-proving?
A) Essence vs. accident
B) Essence vs. consideration (dhāt vs. iʿtibār)
C) Universal vs. particular
D) Necessary vs. theoretical

Key Takeaway
Proving a thing by itself is only invalid when the same consideration applies; under different aspects it is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the same thing can be both known and unknown under different aspects, what does that say about your confidence in your own judgments? Will you now pause and ask yourself: 'Am I looking at this in the right aspect?' before insisting on your view?

How Expression Affects Judgment's Implications
Sanusi explains how the expression of the subject changes the judgment's implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the way we express a subject in a judgment changes whether the judgment is self-evident, acquired, or useless.

Core Idea
The same judgment can be useful or useless depending on how we express the subject.

Sanusi introduces a crucial principle: the implications of a judgment—whether it needs proof, is self-evident, or is useless—depend on how we verbally express the subject. Using the example of judging the world to be originated, he shows three different expressions that yield three different epistemic outcomes. This principle also helps resolve objections against the first figure of the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the three examples.
The passage is cut off at the end; focus on the examples.
2
Identify the three expressions and their categories
The passage gives three ways to express the subject in the judgment 'the world is originated.' Match each expression to its category: useless, intuitive, or acquired.
The second expression is called 'intuitive' (badīhī) and the third 'acquired' (kasbī).
3
Understand the resolution of first figure objections
The author says that by this principle, objections against the first figure of the syllogism are resolved. Consider how the choice of expression could make a syllogism valid or invalid.
The text is incomplete here; think about how a better expression could avoid objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل الباب أن الحكم بالشيء على الشيء قد تختلف لوازمه من الاستغناء عن الدليل أو الافتقار إليه أو إلى التنبيه أو إلى الإحساس أو غير ذلك باختلاف التعبير عن المحكوم عليه مثلا، إذا حاولنا الحكم على العالم بالحدوث، فربما يقع التعبير عنه بما يجعل الحكم غير مفيد أصلاً. كقولنا: كل موجود بعد العدم حادث، أو مفيداً بديهياً. كقولنا: كل ما يقارن تعلق القدرة والإرادة الحادثة فهو حادث، أو مفيداً كسبياً. كقولنا كل متغير فهو حادث وبهذا ينحل ما يورد على الشكل الأول من
And the basis of the chapter: The judgment of a thing about a thing may have different implications (lawāzim) — from independence from proof, or need for it, or need for prompting, or need for sensation, or other — due to difference in the expression (taʿbīr) of the subject (al-maḥkūm ʿalayhi). For example, when we attempt to judge of the world that it is originated, the expression about it may be such that the judgment is not useful at all, like our statement 'everything that exists after non-existence is originated'; or it may be useful intuitively, like our statement 'everything that is accompanied by the attachment of power and will that is originated is originated'; or useful by acquisition, like our statement 'everything changing is originated.' And by this is resolved what is raised against the first figure from
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage states a general principle: the implications of a judgment (whether it needs no proof, needs proof, needs reminding, or needs sensation) can vary based on how the subject is expressed. Three examples are given for the judgment 'the world is originated': (1) 'everything that exists after non-existence is originated' — this is not useful because it is merely tautological or unclear; (2) 'everything accompanied by originated power and will is originated' — this is intuitive; (3) 'everything changing is originated' — this is acquired (kasbī). The author then says that by this principle, objections raised against the first figure [of the syllogism] are resolved, but the text cuts off before completing that resolution.

المحكوم عليه

البديهي

الشكل الأول

Misconception: Some may think that the same subject always yields the same type of judgment regardless of how it is expressed.
Correction: The passage shows that the expression (taʿbīr) of the subject directly changes whether the judgment is self-evident, acquired, or even useless.

Checkpoint

A) The truth of the premises
B) The expression (taʿbīr) of the subject
C) The knowledge of the judge

Key Takeaway
The way we verbally frame the subject determines whether a judgment is self-evident, acquired, or useless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Resolving Circularity in Proving the General Principle of Nazar
Al-Taftazani resolves the circularity objection by distinguishing essence and aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani resolves the accusation of circularity in proving that all valid nazar yields knowledge by distinguishing between essence and aspect.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided by distinguishing between a thing's essence and its aspect of consideration.

Al-Taftazani addresses a key objection to the proof that all valid reasoning (nazar) yields knowledge: Is it circular to prove the general rule by a particular instance? He shows that this objection arises from conflating two different aspects of the same thing. By distinguishing between the essence of a nazar and its consideration as an individual of nazar, he demonstrates that no circularity or contradiction exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part where Imam al-Razi's objection is presented: that proving the rule by a particular case is circular and self-contradictory. Note the key terms: تناقض (contradiction) and دور (circularity).
2
Grasp the essence-aspect distinction
Al-Taftazani introduces the concept of i'tibar (aspect/consideration). The same thing can be viewed from different angles, leading to different rulings. For example, the same reasoning act is a means (prior and known) in one aspect and an instance of nazar (posterior and unknown) in another.
This distinction is central to resolving apparent paradoxes in kalam.
3
Analyze the resolution
The unknown proposition is a general statement about nazar, while the known starting point is a specific example. Since these are not the same under the same aspect, no circularity occurs. Read the detailed explanation carefully to see how this works.
Notice that the general proposition is sought, while the specific one is known by intuition.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument protects the validity of rational reasoning (nazar) from a skeptical challenge. Understanding this distinction helps you appreciate how Islamic theologians defended the reliability of rational inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا ما قال إمام الحرمين: إنه لا يعد في إثبات جميع أنواع النظر بنوع منها يثبت نفسه وغيره، إلا أنه لما اعترف بإثبات الشيء بنفسه اعترض الإمام الرازي بأن فيه تناقضاً وتقدماً للشيء على نفسه. وجوابه أن نفس الشيء بحسب الذات قد تغايره بحسب الاعتبار فتخالفه في الأحكام كهذا الذي أثبتنا به كون كل نظر مفيداً للحكم، فإنه من حيث ذاته وسيلة ومتقدم ومعلوم، ومن حيث كونه من أفراد النظر مطلوب ومتأخر ومجهول - وتفصيله أن الموقوف المجهول المطلوب بالنظر هو القضية الموجبة المهملة أو الموجبة الكلية، التي عنوان موضوعها مفهوم النظر، أعني قولنا النظر يفيد العلم، أو كل نظر مقرون بشرائطه يفيد العلم والموقوف عليه المعلوم بديهة هو القضية الشخصية التي موضوعها ذات النظر المخصوص، أعني قولنا: العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث يفيد العلم بأن العالم حادث من غير اعتبار كون هذا الموضوع من أفراد النظر فلا يكون الشيء الواحد بالذات والاعتبار متقدماً على نفسه، ومعلوماً حين ما ليس بمعلوم، ليلزم الدور والتناقض.
This is what Imam al-Haramayn said: it is not considered in establishing all types of nazar (reasoning) by one type of them establishing itself and others, except that when he acknowledged the establishment of a thing by itself, Imam al-Razi objected that it involves contradiction and precedence of a thing over itself. Its answer is that the same thing in terms of essence (dhat) may differ from it in terms of aspect (i'tibar) and thus differ in rulings, like this [reasoning] by which we established that every nazar is beneficial for judgment; for it, from the perspective of its essence, is a means, prior, and known, and from the perspective of being an individual of nazar, it is sought, posterior, and unknown. Its detail is that the unknown thing that is sought by nazar is the affirmative universal or particular proposition whose subject term is the concept of nazar, namely our statement: 'nazar yields knowledge', or 'every nazar together with its conditions yields knowledge', and the known thing that is presupposed by intuition is the singular proposition whose subject is the essence of the particular nazar, namely our statement: 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated' yields knowledge that the world is originated, without considering that this subject is an individual of nazar. So the one thing in terms of essence and aspect is not prior to itself, and known at a time when it is not known, so that circularity and contradiction would follow.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents and resolves a key objection regarding the proof that all valid nazar yields knowledge. Imam al-Haramayn argued that proving the general rule by a particular case is not circular because it establishes a thing by itself. Imam al-Razi objected that this is contradictory and involves self-precedence. The answer is that the same thing can be considered under different aspects: in its essence it is a means, prior, and known, but as an individual of nazar it is sought, posterior, and unknown. The resolution is that the unknown proposition sought is a general statement about nazar, while the known presupposition is a specific application (e.g., 'the world is changing, everything changing is originated yields knowledge'). Since these are different aspects of the same thing, there is no circularity or contradiction. The explanation proceeds in the author's own order: first stating the objection, then the answer by distinguishing aspects, then detailing the distinction between the general and specific propositions.

قضية شخصية

تناقض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proving the rule that all nazar yields knowledge by using a particular nazar is necessarily circular.
Correction: Circularity is avoided because the general proposition and the specific proposition are considered under different aspects: the general is sought, the specific is known by intuition. The same thing can be both known and unknown in different respects.

D) By rejecting Imam al-Razi's objection as invalid

The distinction between essence and aspect allows the same thing to be both known and unknown without contradiction, thus saving the proof of nazar from circularity.
