How Different Expressions of a Subject Resolve the Circularity Objection in the First Figure
Al-Taftazani presents the principle of differing liabilities and resolves the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the liabilities of a judgment vary with the expression of the subject, and how this resolves the apparent circularity in the first figure of the syllogism.

Core Idea
The same judgment can be known from one aspect and unknown from another.

In this passage, al-Taftazani introduces a key principle: the way we express the subject of a judgment affects whether the judgment is intuitive, acquired, or useless. He then applies this principle to resolve a famous objection against the first syllogistic figure—that knowledge of the conclusion would depend on itself. By distinguishing aspects, the paradox dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle and examples
Start by reading the passage's first part where al-Taftazani states that judgments have differing liabilities based on expression. Then note the three examples: a non-beneficial judgment, an intuitive one, and an acquired one.
The three examples illustrate how the same judgment (e.g., 'the world is originated') can be expressed in ways that yield different epistemic statuses.
2
Understand the objection about circularity
The objection claims that in the first figure, knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowing the major premise, which includes the subject of the conclusion, leading to self-dependence.
This objection is a known logical puzzle.
3
Analyze the answer: distinction of aspects
Al-Taftazani answers that the judgment is known from the aspect that its subject is under the middle term (e.g., 'changing') but unknown from the aspect that it is the minor term (e.g., 'world'). Since these are different aspects, no circularity occurs.
The key is that 'knownness' and 'unknownness' can coexist under different descriptions.
4
Reflect on the significance
This resolution shows how careful attention to expressions and aspects avoids paradoxes in logical reasoning, a crucial skill in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل الباب أن الحكم بالشيء على الشيء قد تختلف لوازمه من حيث الدليل أو الافتقار إليه أو إلى التنبيه أو إلى الإحساس أو غير ذلك الاستغناء باختلاف التعبير عن المحكوم عليه مثلا، إذا حاولنا الحكم على العالم بالحدوث، فربما يقع التعبير عنه بما يجعل الحكم غير مفيد أصلاً. كقولنا: كل موجود بعد العدم حادث، أو مفيداً بديهياً: كقولنا: كل ما يقارن تعلق القدرة والإرادة الحادثة فهو حادث، أو مفيداً كسبياً: كقولنا: كل متغير فهو حادث وبهذا ينحل ما يورد على الشكل الأول من أن ضروري العلم بالنتيجة لما توقف على العلم بالكبرى الكلية التي من جملة أفراد موضوعها موضوع النتيجة لزم توقف النتيجة على نفسها وكونها قبل أن تعلم وهو تناقض وذلك لأن معلومية الحكم كحدوث العالم من جهة كون المحكوم عليه من أفراد الأوسط كالمتغير لا يناقض مجهوليته من جهة كونه من أفراد الأصغر، أعني العالم
The root of the matter is that the judgment of a thing about a thing may have differing liabilities depending on the proof or need for it or for prompting or for sensation or other self-sufficiency by difference of expression about the subject, for example, if we attempt to judge the world as originated, sometimes the expression occurs in a way that makes the judgment not beneficial at all, like our saying: 'everything that exists after non-existence is originated', or beneficial intuitively, like our saying: 'everything that accompanies the attachment of originating power and will is originated', or beneficial acquiredly, like our saying: 'everything changing is originated'. And by this is resolved what is objected concerning the first figure, that the necessary knowledge of the conclusion, when it depends on knowledge of the universal major premise whose subject includes the subject of the conclusion, necessitates dependence of the conclusion on itself and its being before it is known, which is a contradiction. And that is because the knownness of the judgment, like the origination of the world, from the aspect that the subject is an individual of the middle term like 'changing' does not contradict its unknownness from the aspect that it is an individual of the minor term, namely the world.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage introduces a general principle about how judgments can differ in their liabilities (such as being obviously necessary or requiring reasoning) depending on how the subject is expressed. Three examples are given: a statement that is not beneficial (e.g., 'everything existing after non-existence is originated'), one that is intuitively beneficial (e.g., 'everything accompanied by originating power and will is originated'), and one that is acquiredly beneficial (e.g., 'everything changing is originated'). This principle resolves an objection about the first syllogistic figure: that if knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the universal major premise which contains the subject of the conclusion, then the conclusion would depend on itself (circularity). The answer is that the knowledge of the judgment (e.g., the world's origination) is known from the aspect that its subject is under the middle term (e.g., 'changing') but unknown from the aspect that its subject is the minor term (e.g., 'world'). Since these are different aspects, there is no contradiction. The passage follows the author's order: stating the principle, giving examples, raising the objection, and providing the resolution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the dependence of the conclusion on the major premise in the first figure leads to circularity.
Correction: The passage shows that the conclusion is known from one aspect (under the middle term) and unknown from another (as subject of the minor), so no circularity occurs.

Key Takeaway
The same judgment can be known from one aspect (under the middle term) and unknown from another (as subject of the minor), thus avoiding circularity.

The First Figure and the Other Figures: Conditions for Necessary Knowledge
Al-Taftazani answers an objection about necessary vs. theoretical knowledge in syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that all syllogistic figures yield necessary knowledge when full conditions are met, contrary to the initial objection.

Core Idea
All syllogistic figures yield necessary knowledge when full conditions are met.

This passage addresses a key objection about the epistemic status of syllogistic figures. The objection claims only the first figure provides necessary knowledge, while others are merely theoretical. Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between general statements about nazar and particular cases, and emphasizing that with all conditions—especially noting the mode of implication—all figures become equally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objection that the first figure is necessary and others theoretical. Ask yourself why this seems plausible at first glance.
The objection is based on the apparent clarity of the first figure's premises.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
Notice that al-Taftazani distinguishes between considering nazar in general (subject term) and particular instances with full conditions. This is key to resolving the objection.
3
Analyze the role of conditions
Focus on the condition of noting the mode of implication (jiha al-dalala) and how it makes all figures clear. Without this condition, even the first figure may fail to produce necessary knowledge.
The mule example illustrates failure due to inattention.
4
Reflect on the mule example
The example shows that one may have both premises but still not draw the conclusion if not attentive to the implication. This demonstrates that clarity depends on conditions, not the figure itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا خفاء في أن كون النظر مفيداً للعلم ضروري في الشكل الأول، نظري في باقي الأشكال. فكيف يصح اختيار أنه مطلقاً على ما ذهب إليه الرازي أو نظري مطلقاً على ما ذهب إليه إمام الحرمين؟ قلنا: الكلام فيما إذا أخذ عنوان الموضوع هو النظر، فيقال: النظر أو كل نظر على شرائطه يفيد العلم، وما ذكر من التفصيل قطعاً إنما هو في الخصوصيات مثل قولنا العالم متغير، مع قولنا وكل متغير حادث أو ولا شيء من القديم بمتغير فإن العلم بإفادة الأول ضروري والثاني نظري على أن هذا التفصيل إنما هو بالنظر إلى مجرد ترتيب المقدمتين ووضع الحد الأوسط عن الحدين الآخرين، وأما بعد حصول جميع الشرائط فالحكم بإفادة كل من خصوصيات النظر العلم ضروري في جميع الأشكال على ما يراه بعض المحققين من أن من جملة الشرائط ملاحظة جهة دلالة المقدمتين على المطلوب، وكيفية اندراجه فيهما بالقوة حتى قال الإمام حجة الإسلام إن هذا هو السبب الخاص لحصول النتيجة بالفعل وبدونه ربما يذهل عن النتيجة مع حضور المقدمتين، كما إذا رأى بغلة منتفخة البطن فتوهم أنها حامل مع ملاحظة أنها بغلة عاقر وكل بغلة عاقر ولا خفاء في أن مع ملاحظة جهة الإنتاج والتفطن لكيفية الاندراج يتساوى الأشكال في الجلاء حتى ذهب بعض أهل التحقيق
For if it is said: there is no hiddenness in that the fact that nazar is beneficial for knowledge is necessary (daruri) in the first figure and theoretical (nazari) in the remaining figures. So how is it valid to choose that it is absolute, as al-Razi held, or theoretical absolutely, as Imam al-Haramayn held? We say: the discussion is about when the subject term is taken as nazar, so it is said: 'nazar' or 'every nazar with its conditions yields knowledge', and what was mentioned of detail is definitely only regarding particularities, like our saying 'the world is changing' together with our saying 'and everything changing is originated' or 'and nothing eternal is changing', for the knowledge of the benefit of the first is necessary and the second is theoretical, although this detail is only with respect to the mere arrangement of the two premises and placing the middle term in relation to the other two terms. But after the attainment of all conditions, the judgment that every particular nazar yields knowledge is necessary in all figures, according to what some investigators hold, that among the conditions is noting the mode of implication of the two premises to the sought conclusion, and how it is potentially included in them, such that Imam Hujjat al-Islam said: this is the specific cause for the actual attainment of the conclusion, and without it one may be heedless of the conclusion despite the presence of the two premises, as when one sees a mule with a swollen belly and imagines it is pregnant, while noting that it is a barren mule, and every barren mule [is not pregnant], and there is no hiddenness that with noting the mode of production and paying attention to the how of inclusion, the figures become equal in clarity, to the extent that some investigators held...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents and answers an objection about the varying epistemic status of syllogistic figures. The objection states that the first figure yields necessary knowledge while others are theoretical, so how can one claim either that all are necessary (al-Razi's view) or all are theoretical (Imam al-Haramayn's view)? The answer distinguishes two contexts: when the subject term is 'nazar' itself, the statement is general; but the detailed distinction between necessary and theoretical applies only to particular cases (like 'the world is changing' with 'everything changing is originated') when considering the mere arrangement of premises. However, when all conditions are met, including noting the mode of implication (jiha al-dalala) and how the conclusion is potentially included, every particular nazar yields necessary knowledge in all figures. The example of the mule illustrates: one may have both premises but fail to draw the conclusion if not attentive to the implication. With full attention, all figures become equally clear. The passage follows the order: objection, answer, distinction, and illustration.

Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the epistemic difference disappears when all conditions—especially noting the mode of implication—are met; all figures become equally necessary.

Key Takeaway

The First Argument: Knowledge from Correct Inference is Self-Certifying
Argument and rebuttal about knowledge from inference being necessary or theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument defending that correct inference yields certain knowledge, and how the author rebuts both horns of a dilemma about the nature of that knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge from correct inference is self-certifying and needs no further proof.

In this passage, the author presents the first argument to defend that correct inference yields certain knowledge. He begins by excluding a previous doubt that negates assent altogether, then sets up a dilemma: the knowledge that inference yields knowledge is either necessary or theoretical, with rebuttals to both horns. He also addresses the objection about counter-evidence and criticizes a flawed formulation in al-Ṭawāliʿ.

Guided Path
1
Context: Why exclude the previous doubt?
Read how the author explains that the previous doubt is not included because it negates inference being beneficial for assent absolutely, while the actual debate is about whether it yields complete certainty or just opinion.
2
Analyze the dilemma and its rebuttal
Identify the two possibilities (necessary or theoretical) and see how each leads to a problem, then how the author rebuts by selecting either horn with additional clarifications.
The author's rebuttal involves two choices: either accept it's necessary and deny that any contrary appears from correct inference, or accept it's theoretical and deny the need for another inference.
3
Counter-evidence doubt and final criticism
Understand the objection about needing knowledge of no counter-evidence, and how the author resolves it. Then note his critique of al-Ṭawāliʿ's formulation.
The author emphasizes that after correct inference, no counter-evidence can appear in certainties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لم نورد الشبهة السابقة في ضمن الوجوه لأنها لنفي أن يكون التصديق الحاصل عقيب النظر علماً مطلقاً أو في الطبيعيات والإلهيات : أو في الإلهيات خاصة على ما ذكره الإمام من أنه لا نزاع في إفادة النظر الظن وإنما النزاع في إفادته اليقين الكامل، وينبغي ألا تكون العدديات محل الخلاف والشبهة السابقة تنفي كون النظر مفيداً للتصديق مطلقاً . الوجه الأول : أن العلم بان الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم إن كان ضرورياً لم يظهر أي لم يقع عقيب النظر خلاف ذلك، أو لم يظهر بعد هذا خلاف ذلك، لامتناع أن يقع أو يظهر خلاف الضروري، واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من الناس لا يحصل عقيب نظرهم إلا الجهل وكثيراً ما ينكشف للناظر خلاف ما حصل من نظره ويظهر خطؤه، ولذلك تنتقل المذاهب، وإن كان نظرياً افتقر إلى نظر آخر يفيد العلم بأنه علم ويتسلسل ورد بأنا نختار أنه ضروري، ولا نسلم ظهور الخلاف من هذا النظر أو بعده إذ الكلام في النظر الصحيح، ولازم الحق حق قطعاً، أو نختار أنه نظري ولا نسلم افتقاره إلى نظر آخر، فإن النظر الصحيح كما أفاد العلم بالنتيجة أفاد العلم بأن ذلك علم لا جهل أو ظن، وكذا حال العلم بعدم المعارض إذ لا يتصور المعارض للنظر الصحيح في القطعيات، وبهذا تندفع شبهة أخرى وهي أن النظر لو أفاد العلم فلا بد من العلم بعدم المعارض إذ لا جزم مع المعارض . ثم إنه ليس بضروري، إذ كثيراً ما يظهر المعارض بل نظري فيفتقر إلى نظر آخر موقوف على عدم المعارض ويتسلسل فقوله : كالعلم بأنه لا معارض معناه أنه يجوز أن يكون ضرورياً، ولا نسلم ظهور المعارض بعد المنظر الصحيح ، وأن يكون نظرياً ، ولا نسلم توقفه على نظر آخر. وههنا بحث نطلعك عليه في آخر المقصد . وفي تقرير الطوالع ههنا قصور. حيث قال - العلم الحاصل عقيب النظر إما أن يكون ضرورياً أو نظرياً، وكلاهما محال كأنه على حذف المضاف أي علمية العلم الحاصل أعني كونه علماً صح منه اختبار أنه ضروري، وإلا فالحاصل بالنظر لا يكون ضرورياً إلا بمعنى أنا نضطر إلى الجزم به للجزم بالمقدمات، لكنه بهذا المعنى لا يقابل ولهذا النظرى .
We did not present the previous doubt among the arguments only because it is to negate that the assent (taṣdīq) obtained after inference is knowledge absolutely, or in the natural sciences and metaphysics, or in metaphysics specifically, as the Imam mentioned that there is no dispute about inference yielding opinion (ẓann), but the dispute is about its yielding complete certainty (yaqīn kāmil). And it should be that the mathematical sciences are not a point of disagreement, and the previous doubt negates inference being beneficial for assent absolutely. The first argument: That the knowledge that the belief obtained after inference is knowledge, if it is necessary (ḍarūrī), then no contrary would appear—i.e., would not occur after the inference—or no contrary would appear after this, because it is impossible for a contrary to the necessary to occur or appear. The consequent is false, because many people obtain only ignorance after their inference, and often what the investigator obtains from his inference is disclosed to be contrary and his error appears, hence schools of thought change. And if it is theoretical (naẓarī), it would need another inference to yield the knowledge that it is knowledge, leading to infinite regress. It was rebutted by our choice that it is necessary, and we do not accept the appearance of a contrary from this inference or after it, for we are speaking of correct inference (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ), and the necessary of truth is absolutely true. Or we choose that it is theoretical, and we do not accept its need for another inference, for correct inference, just as it yields knowledge of the conclusion, yields knowledge that that is knowledge, not ignorance or opinion. And similarly the case of knowledge of the absence of counter-evidence (al-muʿāriḍ), for counter-evidence to correct inference in certainties cannot be conceived. By this, another doubt is repelled: that if inference yields knowledge, there must be knowledge of the absence of counter-evidence, for there is no certainty with counter-evidence. Then it is not necessary, for often counter-evidence appears, but rather it is theoretical, so it would need another inference dependent on the absence of counter-evidence, leading to infinite regress. So his saying: like knowledge that there is no counter-evidence, means that it is possible that it is necessary, and we do not accept the appearance of counter-evidence after correct inference; and that it is theoretical, and we do not accept its dependency on another inference. Here there is a research we will inform you of at the end of the objective. And in the account of al-Ṭawāliʿ here there is deficiency. Where he said: The knowledge obtained after inference is either necessary or theoretical, and both are impossible—it is as if there is omission of the genitive, i.e., the knowledge-ness of the obtained knowledge, meaning that it is knowledge, it is correct to test that it is necessary. Otherwise, what is obtained by inference is not necessary except in the sense that we are compelled to be certain of it due to certainty of the premises, but in this sense it is not opposed to, and for this reason, the theoretical (al-naẓarī).
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage first explains why a certain previous doubt was not included among the arguments: it negates that assent from inference is knowledge absolutely, whereas the real debate is about whether inference yields complete certainty (yaqīn kāmil) or just opinion (ẓann). Then the author presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) in two parts. The argument is framed as a dilemma: the knowledge that the belief from inference is knowledge must be either necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī). If it were necessary, then no contrary could appear after inference, but in reality people often get ignorance and change their views—so the consequent is false. If it were theoretical, an infinite regress would result. The rebuttal (radd) offers two choices: either accept that it is necessary (and deny that any contrary appears from correct inference) or accept that it is theoretical (and deny the need for another inference, because correct inference itself proves its own correctness, just as it proves the conclusion). The author then addresses a related doubt about counter-evidence (al-muʿāriḍ): knowledge from inference requires knowledge that there is no counter-evidence, but that knowledge itself would be either necessary or theoretical, leading to regress. The author denies that counter-evidence can arise after correct inference in certainties. Finally, the author criticizes the author of al-Ṭawāliʿ for incorrectly stating that knowledge from inference is both impossible, and clarifies that the intended meaning is the 'knowledge-ness' of the knowledge, not the knowledge itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the dispute is about whether inference yields any knowledge at all, or that the knowledge from inference must be either necessary or theoretical without any qualification.
Correction: The debate is specifically about whether inference yields complete certainty (yaqīn) or just opinion (ẓann), not whether it yields any knowledge. The author also clarifies that correct inference is the subject, and the knowledge of its result being knowledge is self-evident or proven by the same inference.

Key Takeaway
Correct inference yields certain knowledge that is self-certifying and does not require external verification.

The Objection of Ignorance and Its Rebuttal
The author refutes the second objection that inference is conditional upon ignorance of the sought conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that inference requires ignorance is rebutted by distinguishing entailment as consequence from simultaneous causation.

Core Idea
Inference does not demand ignorance; it transforms ignorance into knowledge upon completion.

The second objection argues that inference must be conditioned on not already knowing the conclusion, otherwise it would be seeking what is already obtained. But if inference entails knowledge, how can it be conditioned on its absence? The rebuttal resolves this by redefining entailment as consequence: knowledge follows inference at its completion, so the condition applies only to the starting state.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage which states that inference is conditioned on ignorance of the sought conclusion. Understand the logical necessity behind this condition: to avoid seeking what is already known (ṭalab al-ḥāṣil).
The objector is concerned about the absurdity of seeking knowledge one already possesses.
2
Understand the paradox
The objector then points out a contradiction: if inference entails knowledge (intellectually or habitually), then it cannot be conditional on the absence of that knowledge. This is because it would require the cause (al-malzūm) to be conditional on the absence of its effect (al-lāzim), which is impossible.
This paradox challenges the logical coherence of inference's epistemological role.
3
Follow the rebuttal
The author responds by redefining the meaning of 'entailment' (istilzām) in this context. Instead of simultaneous entailment, it means consequence (istiʿqāb): knowledge is obtained upon completion of the inference, not during it.
This distinction resolves the paradox by separating the condition from the entailment relation.
4
Synthesize the resolution
Now understand that the 'entailer' for knowledge is the termination of inference (intihāʾuhu), and what is conditioned on the absence of knowledge is the persistence of ignorance (baqāʾuhu). Thus, the objection is refuted.
The condition applies to the starting point, not to the logical relation between inference and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : : أن النظر مشروط بعدم العلم بالمطلوب، لئلا يلزم طلب الحاصل فلو كان مفيداً للعلم أي مستلزماً له عقلاً أو عادة لما كان مشروطاً بعدمه ضرورة امتناع كون الملزوم مشروطاً بعدم اللازم ورد بأن معنى الاستلزام ههنا الاستعقاب عقلاً أو عادة، بمعنى أنه يلزم حصول العلم بالمطلوب عند تمام النظر، فالملزوم للعلم انتهاؤه، والمشروط بعدم العلم بقاؤه .
The second: That inference is conditioned on the absence of knowledge of the sought (al-maṭlūb), lest seeking what is already obtained (ṭalab al-ḥāṣil) be necessary. So if it were beneficial for knowledge, i.e., entailing it intellectually or habitually, it would not be conditioned on its absence, necessarily because it is impossible for the entailer (al-malzūm) to be conditioned on the absence of the entailer (al-lāzim). It was rebutted that the meaning of entailment (al-istilzām) here is consequence (al-istiʿqāb) intellectually or habitually, meaning that the acquisition of knowledge of the sought occurs upon completion of the inference. So the entailer for knowledge is its termination (intihāʾuhu), and what is conditioned on the absence of knowledge is its persistence (baqāʾuhu).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second objection against the epistemological value of inference. The objector argues that inference (naẓar) is conditioned on not already knowing the conclusion (al-maṭlūb), otherwise it would involve seeking what is already obtained (ṭalab al-ḥāṣil). If inference entails knowledge intellectually or habitually, then it cannot be conditioned on the absence of the entailed thing (knowledge), because that would require the cause (al-malzūm) to be conditional on the absence of its effect (al-lāzim), which is impossible. The author's rebuttal (ward) redefines the meaning of entailment (istilzām) here as consequence (istiʿqāb): inference is followed by knowledge upon its completion, not that inference necessitates knowledge simultaneously. Thus, the 'entailer' for knowledge is the completion of inference, not the inference itself, and that which is conditioned on the absence of knowledge is the persistence of ignorance (or the act of seeking), not the inference's entailment. In other words, the condition for engaging in inference is not knowing the conclusion yet, but that does not negate that inference, when completed, produces knowledge.

Key Terms
نظر
مشروط
مطلوب
طلب الحاصل
استلزام
استعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If inference entails knowledge, it cannot be conditioned on ignorance because that would be contradictory.
Correction: The author clarifies that the entailment is a consequence (istiʿqāb), not simultaneous causation, so the condition applies to the starting state, not the entailment itself.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that inference cannot be conditioned on ignorance if it entails knowledge?
A) He denies that inference entails knowledge.
B) He redefines entailment as consequence (istiʿqāb), so inference is followed by knowledge at completion, not simultaneous.
C) He says the condition is impossible anyway.
D) He accepts the objection and modifies the claim.

Key Takeaway
The objection that inference requires ignorance is resolved by distinguishing entailment as consequential rather than simultaneous, allowing inference to be conditioned on ignorance at the start while still producing knowledge at completion.

?

Defending Obligation to Seek Knowledge
Third objection and its answer concerning inference and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational obligation to seek knowledge through inference (nazar) is defended against the objection that it negates human agency and the possibility of reward.

Core Idea
Obligation applies to actions, not to the quality of knowledge itself.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if inference necessarily produces knowledge, then people have no choice in the matter, making obligation pointless. The author responds by clarifying that knowledge is a quality, not an action, and obligation applies to the means of acquisition, not the quality itself. This distinction preserves both rational obligation and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of objection and answer become clear.
The passage begins with 'الثالث' indicating it is the third objection.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objector argues: if inference yields knowledge necessarily (either intellectually or habitually), then knowledge becomes like necessary knowledge—beyond human power and choice—so commanding it would be absurd.
Note the terms: 'قبح التكليف' (absurdity of obligation), 'خروج عن القدرة والاختيار' (beyond power and choice).
3
Focus on the answer's key distinction
The answer accepts the principle of rational absurdity but distinguishes between actions (afʿāl) and qualities (kayfiyyāt). Knowledge is a quality, not an action, so obligation applies only to acquiring it through actions.
The key phrase: 'التكليف إنما يكون بالأفعال دون الكيفيات'.
4
Understand the implication for inference
Thus, the obligation is to perform the actions that lead to knowledge—directing one's power, engaging in nazar, using the senses—not to directly possess knowledge. This preserves human agency and the meaningfulness of reward and punishment.
The author notes this is the position of al-Āmidī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو أفاد النظر العلم بمعنى لزومه عقيبه عقلاً أو عادة لقبح التكليف، بالعلم، لكونه بمنزلة الضرورى في الخروج عن القدرة والاختيار وعن استحقاق الثواب والعقاب، وأجيب بعد تسليم قاعدة القبح العقلي بأن التكليف إنما يكون بالأفعال دون الكيفيات والإضافات والانفعالات والعلم عند المحققين من الكيفيات دون الأفعال، فالتكليف لا يكون إلا بتحصيله، وذلك بمباشرة الأسباب كصرف القوة والنظر واستعمال الحواس، فكان هذا مراد الآمدي بما ...
The third: If inference yielded knowledge in the sense of its being necessary after it intellectually or habitually, then obligation (al-taklīf) with knowledge would be absurd (qabuḥ), because it would be like the necessary in being beyond power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār) and the deserving of reward and punishment. And it was answered, after accepting the principle of rational absurdity (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), that obligation only pertains to actions (afʿāl), not to qualities (kayfiyyāt), relations (iḍāfāt), or passions (infiʿālāt). And knowledge, according to the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqīn), is among the qualities, not the actions. So obligation only applies to its acquisition (taḥṣīluhu), and that is by undertaking means such as directing one's power, inference, and using the senses. So this was the intent of al-Āmidī in what ...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objector argues that if inference yields knowledge necessarily (either intellectually or habitually), then obligating people to have that knowledge would be morally absurd (qabuḥ), because necessary knowledge is beyond human power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār), and thus cannot be rewarded or punished. The author's answer, after accepting the principle of rational absurdity (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), distinguishes between actions (afʿāl) and qualities (kayfiyyāt). Knowledge is a quality, not an action. Obligation (taklīf) applies to actions, not qualities. Therefore, the obligation is not to possess knowledge directly, but to acquire it through means such as directing one's mental power, engaging in inference, and using the senses. The author notes that this is the position of al-Āmidī, though the sentence is cut off. The key point is that inference does not produce knowledge in a way that bypasses human agency; rather, it is the means by which one actively acquires knowledge, and thus the obligation to perform inference is meaningful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If inference necessarily produces knowledge, then obligation to know is impossible and pointless.
Correction: Obligation applies to the acquisition of knowledge through actions like inference, not to the knowledge itself as a quality. Thus, even if inference produces knowledge necessarily, the obligation to perform the means (nazar) remains meaningful and preserves human agency.

Key Takeaway
Obligation to seek knowledge through inference is meaningful because it applies to the actions that lead to knowledge, not to the knowledge itself as a quality.

Obligation Falls on Nazar, Not Its Object
The author states that obligation applies to nazar, not the object considered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation in Islam pertains to the act of reasoning (nazar) and not to the object of reasoning, and how this distinction relates to theoretical and necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Obligation is on the act of consideration, not on the object considered.

Sanusi addresses a key objection about obligation before the acquisition of knowledge. He clarifies that what is actually required is the act of reasoning (nazar) — which is within human power — not the knowledge itself or its object. This distinction resolves the apparent problem of being obligated to know something beyond one's control. The passage also distinguishes theoretical knowledge from necessary knowledge to reinforce the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms like al-nazar and al-manzur fih.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where Sanusi states that obligation applies to nazar, not to the object of consideration. Understand why he says nazar is 'within capability' (مقدور).
This is the central argument of the passage.
3
Examine the distinction between knowledge types
Note the difference between theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) and necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri). Theoretical knowledge can be acquired or abandoned; necessary knowledge cannot.
This distinction supports the main claim.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why it is possible in a theoretical proposition to believe its contradictory, unlike an axiomatic proposition. How does this relate to the obligation on nazar?
This shows the flexibility of theoretical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : إن التكليف لم يقع بالمنظور فيه ليصح بل بالنظر وهو مقدور، وإلا فلا خفاء في وقوع التكليف بمعرفة الصانع ووحدانيته ونحو ذلك، وبالجملة، فالعلم النظري مقدور التحصيل والترك، بخلاف الضروري ولزومه بعد تمام النظر لا ينافي ذلك. ومن ههنا أمكن في القضية النظرية اعتقاد النقيض بخلاف القضية البديهية.
He said: Indeed, obligation did not fall upon what is considered (al-manzur fih) in order to be valid, but rather upon consideration (al-nazar) and it is within capability. Otherwise, there is no obscurity in the occurrence of obligation for knowledge of the Creator, His oneness, and so on. In summary, theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) is within capability to obtain and to abandon, unlike necessary knowledge (al-daruri). And its necessity (luzum) after completing consideration does not contradict that. From this, it is possible in a theoretical proposition to believe its contradictory, unlike an axiomatic proposition (al-qadiyya al-badihiyya).
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the obligation of theoretical knowledge. The author clarifies that the religious obligation (taklif) applies to the act of consideration (al-nazar), which is within human capability, not to the object being considered (al-manzur fih), which may not be directly within our power. He then distinguishes theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) from necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri): theoretical knowledge can be acquired or abandoned at will, whereas necessary knowledge is unavoidable. He also notes that the necessity that results after completing consideration does not contradict the fact that the act of consideration was within our power. Finally, he points out that in a theoretical proposition it is possible to believe its contradictory, unlike an axiomatic proposition where the opposite is impossible. This distinction shows that nazar is the proper locus of obligation.

Key Terms
التكليف
النظر
المنظور فيه
العلم النظري
العلم الضروري
القضية البديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religious obligation applies directly to the object of knowledge (e.g., knowing God) rather than to the act of reasoning.
Correction: The passage states that obligation is upon nazar (the act of consideration), not upon the object considered, because nazar is within human capability.

Checkpoint

A) The object of consideration (al-manzur fih)
B) The act of consideration (al-nazar)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The obligation in Islam is upon the act of reasoning, not upon the object of reasoning, and this is consistent with the nature of theoretical knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If obligation falls on your act of reasoning, not on the content, then your worship of God begins with thinking carefully. When you study your faith, do you seek certainty through effort or do you want instant indoctrination? Which path does your heart truly desire?

Fourth Objection: Difficulty Does Not Entail Impossibility
The objector argues that even self-knowledge is uncertain; answer: difficulty, not impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that even self-knowledge is uncertain due to philosophical disagreements, and the key response that difficulty does not imply impossibility, preserving the possibility of nazar in divine matters.

Core Idea
Disagreement about the self shows difficulty, not that knowledge is impossible.

This lesson presents the fourth objection against the ability of rational consideration (nazar) to yield certainty in divine matters. The objector points to the profound disagreements among philosophers about the nature of the self—something closest to us—and argues that if we cannot be certain of that, how can we be certain of the distant divine? The response clarifies the crucial distinction between difficulty and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's logic
The objector uses the self as the closest thing to a person. If even that is disputed, then more distant things are even less certain.
2
Note the different theories about the self
The passage lists several philosophical views: the self as a sensible structure, subtle particles, an indivisible part, or a separate substance. This shows disagreement.
These are not all possible theories, just representative examples.
3
See the extension to distant things
The objector argues from the immediate (self) to the distant (heavens, elements, immaterial entities, divine matters). If certainty fails for the near, it fails for the far.
4
Grasp the answer: difficulty ≠ impossibility
The response concedes difficulty but denies impossibility. The point of dispute is whether nazar yields any knowledge, not whether it is easy or hard.
This is a crucial distinction in kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان اتصالاً ومناسبة هويته التي يشير إليها بقوله : أنا . وقد كثر فيها الخلاف، ولم يحصل من النظر الجزم بأنها هذا الهيكل المحسوس أو أجزاء لطيفة ساريه فيه، أو جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب، أو جوهر مجرد متعلق به أو غير ذلك فكيف فيما هو أبعد كالسماوات والعناصر وعجائب المركبات وأبعد كالمجردات والإلهيات ومباحث الذات والصفات ؟ وأجيب : بأن ذلك إنما يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذه العلوم بالنظر لا على امتناعه والمتنازع هو الامتناع لا الصعوبة.
The fourth: Indeed, the closest of things to man in connection and suitability is his identity (huwiyya) which he points to by saying 'I'. And disagreement has abounded concerning it, and certainty has not been obtained by consideration that it is this sensible structure, or subtle particles permeating it, or an indivisible part in the heart, or a separate substance attached to it, or other than that. So how about what is more distant, like the heavens, the elements, and wonders of composites, and more distant like the immaterial entities, the divine matters, and investigations of the Essence and attributes? And it was answered: That only indicates the difficulty of acquiring these sciences by consideration, not its impossibility. And the point of dispute is impossibility, not difficulty.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection against the validity of nazar in divine matters. The objector argues that even the thing closest to a person—his own identity (huwiyya)—is subject to great disagreement among thinkers, and nazar has not yielded certainty about its true nature. He lists various philosophical theories about the self: sensible structure, subtle particles, indivisible part, or separate substance. If nazar cannot provide certainty about something so immediate, how can it provide certainty about more distant things like the heavens, elements, and especially the immaterial and divine? The answer to this objection is given: the disagreement only indicates the difficulty (su'uba) of acquiring knowledge through nazar, not its impossibility (imtina'). The point in dispute is whether nazar yields any knowledge at all, not whether it is easy or difficult.

Key Terms
الهوية
الهيكل المحسوس
الجوهر المجرد
الصعوبة
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That difficulty or disagreement in attaining knowledge through nazar proves that nazar cannot yield certainty at all.
Correction: The passage teaches that difficulty and disagreement only indicate the effort required, not the impossibility of obtaining knowledge. The objector confuses difficulty with impossibility.

Checkpoint
What does the answer to the fourth objection conclude about the disagreements concerning the self?
A) They prove that nazar cannot yield certainty
B) They show the difficulty but not impossibility of nazar
C) They are irrelevant
D) They show that the self is unknowable

Key Takeaway
Disagreement and difficulty in rational investigation do not prove that certainty about divine matters is impossible; they only show that such knowledge requires effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreement about a religious truth, do you use that as an excuse to stop seeking? How does this lesson challenge you to persevere?
?

Fifth Objection: Conception Prerequisite Answered
Objection: conception impossible; answer: minimal conception suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against nazar in divine matters and how the answer shows that minimal conception is sufficient for reasoning to proceed.

Core Idea
Perfect conception is not needed; a minimal grasp allows nazar to operate.

This passage presents a key objection against the possibility of rational reasoning about Allah: that we cannot even conceive of divine matters properly. The objection argues that definition and description fail. The answer is both subtle and practical—showing that even an imperfect conception is enough for reasoning to begin.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the objector's claim: that conception is a condition for nazar, but conception is impossible.
Notice that the objection breaks down conception into necessary and acquired, then acquired into definition and description.
2
Understand the three premises
The objection has three main points: (1) conception is a condition for assent, (2) definition is impossible for divine matters due to their simplicity, (3) description does not yield true conception of the essence.
Think about why definition requires composition and why divine simplicity prevents that.
3
Study the answer's structure
Read the answer: it grants that description may give some conception even if not complete, and even if that is not accepted, a minimal conception is enough.
The answer does not claim full conception, only enough for reasoning to function.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this objection matters for kalam. If this objection held, no rational theology would be possible.
The answer safeguards the possibility of theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لو أفاد النظر العلم أي التصديق في الإلهيات لكان شرطه هو التصور متحققاً لكنه منتف إما بالضرورة فظاهر وإما بالكسب فلأن الحد ممتنع لامتناع التركيب والرسم لا يفيد تصور الحقيقة. وأجيب بأن الرسم قد يفيد تصور الحقيقة وإن لم يستلزمه ، ولو سلم فيكفي التصور بوجه ما.
The fifth: If consideration were to yield knowledge, i.e., assent (tasdiq), in divine matters, then its condition would be that conception (tasawwur) is realized, but it is nonexistent—either necessarily, which is obvious, or by acquisition, because definition (hadd) is impossible due to the impossibility of composition, and description (rasm) does not yield conception of the essence (haqiqa). And it was answered: Description may yield conception of the essence even if it does not necessitate it; and even if it is granted, conception in some manner suffices.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a fifth objection against the usefulness of nazar in divine matters. The objector argues that if nazar were to yield assent (tasdiq) in divine matters, it would require that conception (tasawwur) of the subject is already present. But conception is either necessary or acquired. Acquired conception depends on either definition (hadd) or description (rasm). Definition is impossible for divine entities because they are not composite, and description does not yield true conception of the essence. Therefore, conception is not possible, so nazar cannot proceed. The answer: description may still yield some conception of the essence even if it does not necessitate it, and even if one grants that description does not give full conception, a minimal conception in some manner is sufficient for nazar to operate. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
التصديق
التصور
الحد
الرسم
الحقيقة
الإلهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That perfect conception is necessary for nazar to proceed.
Correction: The answer shows that even a minimal or partial conception (via description) is sufficient for nazar to yield assent.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fifth objection, why is description (rasm) sufficient?
A) Because it necessitates full conception
B) Because it may yield some conception and minimal conception is enough
C) Because divine matters have no essence
D) Because nazar does not need conception

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection fails because description can provide enough conception for nazar to proceed, requiring only minimal understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Self-Refuting Objection Against Denying Nazar
A final objection and its refutation against the denial of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of self-refutation is used against the denial of nazar and how it is turned back against the opponent.

Core Idea
The claim that nazar is useless is self-defeating if it relies on nazar.

This final objection targets all arguments that claim nazar does not produce knowledge. It shows that such a claim cannot be consistently maintained. The passage then considers a possible counter and refutes it, strengthening the original position. Read carefully to see the dialectical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noticing how the objection is presented against all proofs that nazar does not yield knowledge.
The objection is leveled against 'all the points' mentioned earlier.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objection splits the claim into two cases: if the knowledge that nazar is not useful is theoretical (derived by nazar) or necessary (known without reasoning). Observe the consequence of each.
The first leads to contradiction; the second goes against the fact that many disagree.
3
Understand the opponent's reply
The opponent attempts to escape by saying his argument is not meant to yield knowledge, but only to oppose. Note this move.
He says he is opposing false with false.
4
See the answer turns the tables
The proponent's response: if the opponent's argument succeeds, then nazar has been shown useful (desired conclusion); if it fails, it is idle and the original position stands.
Thus the objection is neutralized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على جميع الوجوه بل على كل ما يحتج به لإثبات أن النظر لا يفيد العلم أن العلم بكون النظر غير مفيد للعلم، إن كان نظرياً مستفاداً من شيء من الاحتجاجات يلزم التناقض، إذ النظر قد أفاد في الجملة وإن كان ضرورياً، والوجود المذكور تنبيهات عليه لزم خلاف أكثر العقلاء في الحكم الضروري، وهو باطل بالضرورة، وإنما الجائز خلاف من العقلاء وهو لا يستلزم جواز خلاف الأكثر ، فإن قيل: نحن نعترف بأن الاحتجاج لا يفيد العلم لكن لما احتججتم على الإفادة احتججنا على نفي الإفادة معارضة للفاسد بالفاسد قلنا ما ذكرتم من الوجوه إن أفادت فساد كلامنا. كان النظر مفيداً للعلم وهو المطلوب، وإن لم يفد كان لغواً وبقي ما ذكرنا سالماً عن المعارض.
And it was objected against all the points—indeed against every proof used to establish that consideration does not yield knowledge—that the knowledge that nazar is not useful for knowledge, if it is theoretical, being derived from some proofs, then a contradiction follows, since nazar has in fact been useful in some way. And if it is necessary, then the aforementioned existence of admonitions would entail opposition to the majority of rational people regarding a necessary judgment, and that is false necessarily. Only opposition by some rational people is possible, and that does not entail the possibility of opposition by the majority. If it is said: We acknowledge that the proof does not yield knowledge, but since you have argued for [the usefulness], we argued for the negation of usefulness out of opposition of the false with the false. We say: The points you have mentioned, if they prove the corruption of our statement, then nazar is useful for knowledge—which is what we want. And if they do not prove [it], then they are idle and our point remains safe from opposition.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides a final objection against all the arguments used to prove that nazar does not yield knowledge. The objection points out that the very claim that nazar is not useful is self-refuting: if it is derived by nazar, then nazar has been useful (contradiction); if it is a necessary truth, then it would be known by all rational people, but in fact many disagree, so it cannot be necessary. A possible response by the opponent is then addressed: the opponent may say that his arguments are not meant to yield knowledge but only to oppose the proponent's arguments. The proponent's answer: if those arguments succeed in refuting the proponent's claim, then nazar has been proven useful (which is the desired conclusion); if they fail, they are useless and the proponent's position remains intact. Thus the objection is turned back on the opponent.

Key Terms
الاحتجاج
التناقض
الضروري
المعارضة
الفاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can argue against the usefulness of nazar without relying on nazar.
Correction: The objection shows that the very claim that nazar is not useful must be derived either by nazar (self-refuting) or known necessarily (but not universally acknowledged), so one cannot consistently deny nazar.

Checkpoint
What does the objection argue about the claim that nazar does not yield knowledge?
A) It is a necessary truth
B) It is self-contradictory if derived from nazar
C) It is supported by all rational people
D) It is impossible

Key Takeaway
The opponent cannot deny the usefulness of nazar without falling into self-contradiction, and any attempt to argue against it only reinforces the proponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have argued that reasoning about God is futile, but have you ever noticed that you used reasoning to make that very claim? How does this self-refutation reveal a hidden inconsistency in your own heart? Will you stop using reasoning, or will you humbly accept its proper place as a tool for truth?
?
When you engage in debates about faith, do you sometimes argue just to 'oppose false with false'—not caring about truth but only about winning? When you do that, your argument is either successful (proving reasoning useful) or empty (proving you don't care). Which is more honest: to seek truth even if it humbles you, or to score points even if it contradicts your own stance?
?
The opponent claimed their argument was not meant to yield knowledge. Is there an area in your life where you avoid drawing conclusions because you fear what they might require of you? Are you willing to let your reasoning lead you to a truth that demands change, or do you prefer the safety of 'just opposing' without commitment?

Defective Reasoning Can Still Yield Truth
Sanusi explains that defective reasoning does not necessarily entail ignorance, distinguishing two types of corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that defective reasoning in kalam does not automatically produce ignorance and that truth can accidentally arise from corrupted premises.

Core Idea
Defective reasoning can accidentally lead to truth, not just ignorance.

This short passage tackles a subtle methodological point: just because reasoning is flawed does not mean we automatically get ignorance. The author, Imam al-Sanusi, explains that corruption can be of two types, and one type may still accidentally produce a true conclusion. This insight helps us evaluate arguments more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation side by side. Notice the two cases the author distinguishes.
The passage uses technical terms: fasad al-sura (corruption of form) and fasad al-madda (corruption of matter).
2
Understand the two types of corruption
Identify what 'corruption of form' and 'corruption of matter' mean. The author says form corruption clearly cannot yield knowledge, but matter corruption might.
Form corruption is a logical error in structure; matter corruption uses a false premise.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the two examples given for matter corruption. See how a false premise (premise is false) can still lead to a true conclusion.
The examples show that even with a false premise, the conclusion can be true.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Understand why this matters: it shows that defective reasoning is not a guarantee of ignorance. This helps us avoid overgeneralizing when criticizing arguments.
The author says 'the false may entail the true' (الكاذب قد يستلزم الصادق).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما النظر الفاسد فالصحيح أنه لا يستلزم الجهل، أما عند فساد الصورة فظاهر، وأما عند فساد المادة فقط فلأن الكاذب قد يستلزم الصادق كنا إذا اعتقد أن العالم أثراً لموجب وكل ما هو أثر لموجب فهو حادث . نعم قد يفيده كما إذا اعتقد أنه غني وكل غني قديم.
He said: As for defective consideration (al-nazar al-fasid), the correct view is that it does not necessarily entail ignorance (jahl). As for when the form is corrupted, it is obvious. As for when only the matter is corrupted, because the false may entail the true, such as when one believes that the world is an effect of a necessitator and that everything that is an effect of a necessitator is originated. Yes, it may even yield it, as when he believes that He is rich and everything rich is eternal.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on defective reasoning (al-nazar al-fasid) in kalam. The correct view, according to the author, is that defective nazar does not necessarily lead to ignorance (jahl). Two types of corruption are distinguished: corruption of form (fasad al-sura) and corruption of matter (fasad al-madda). If the logical form is flawed, it obviously cannot yield knowledge. If only the material premises are false, it is possible that a false premise may entail a true conclusion. The author gives two examples: believing that the world is an effect of a necessitator (true) and that everything that is an effect is originated (false) can lead to the true conclusion that the world is originated. Another example: believing that God is rich (true) and that everything rich is eternal (false) can lead to the true conclusion that God is eternal. Thus, defective reasoning does not necessarily produce ignorance; it might accidentally lead to truth, though it is not reliable.

Key Terms
النظر الفاسد
فساد الصورة
فساد المادة
الكاذب
الصادق
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defective reasoning always leads to ignorance.
Correction: The passage shows that defective reasoning can accidentally produce a true conclusion when only the matter (premises) is corrupted, though it is not a reliable path to knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when only the matter of a nazar is corrupted, what is possible?
A) It necessarily leads to falsehood
B) It may lead to a true conclusion
C) It is always invalid
D) It is better than form-corrupted nazar

Key Takeaway
Defective reasoning can accidentally produce truth if only the premises are false, so do not assume ignorance follows every flawed argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an important belief you hold about God or the world that you have not examined carefully. Is it possible that you arrived at this belief through defective reasoning? What would it cost you to honestly test the premises?
?
When you defend your faith in discussions, do you ever use arguments that sound convincing but whose premises you have not verified? This lesson shows that even a false premise can accidentally yield truth—but is that enough for your certainty? Will you commit today to only rely on sound reasoning in matters of creed?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Why did you believe this truth?' If your reasoning was defective—even if the conclusion was correct—would you be satisfied with 'It turned out to be true by chance'? How does this lesson change the urgency of learning proper methodology?

Objection and Answer on the Nature of Proof
Sanusi presents an objection about proof and answers it by distinguishing conditional from causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of reflection on a proof does not negate its status as a proof, and how the author distinguishes between causal efficacy and conditional entailment.

Core Idea
A proof doesn't create knowledge; it makes knowledge accessible upon reflection.

This lesson examines a subtle objection about the relationship between proof (dalil) and reflection (nazar). The objection argues that if a proof leads to knowledge, then without reflection it cannot be a proof. But the author, Imam al-Sanusi, clarifies that the proof's benefit is conditional, not causal. Let's read the passage and unpack this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection. Notice the two possibilities given: either the proof leads to the Maker or to knowledge of Him, and the implications if reflection is absent.
The objection tries to show a contradiction in the definition of proof.
2
Identify the answer
Read the answer starting with 'وأجيب'. Note how the author redefines the proof's role: it does not cause knowledge like a cause, but rather it is such that whenever one reflects, knowledge follows.
Key phrase: 'إنه بحيث متى وجد وجد ذلك الشيء ومتى نظر فيه علم ذلك الشيء.'
3
Grasp the implication
Understand the final clarification: absence of reflection does not invalidate the proof's capacity. The proof remains a valid indicator; only the actualization of knowledge is contingent on reflection.
The author uses the analogy of entailment (ملزوم ولازم) to explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصانع أو العلم به، وإلا لما كان دليلا عليه، فإن كان الأول لزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه ضرورة انتفاء المفاد بانتفاء المفيد وإن كان الثاني لزم من عدم النظر في الدليل ألا يكون دليلاً لأن هذا وصف إضافي لا يفرض إلا بالإضافة إلى المدلول الذي فرضناه العلم . وهو منتفي عند عدم النظر وأجيب بأنا لا نعني بكون الدليل مفيداً لشيء وموجباً له أن يوجده ويحصله على ما هو شأن العلل، بل إنه بحيث متى وجد وجد ذلك الشيء ومتى نظر فيه علم ذلك الشيء.
العلم
وحاصله (۱) أن وجوده مستلزم لثبوته ، والنظر فيه مستلزم للعلم به، ومعلوم أن انتفاء الملزوم لا يوجب انتفاء اللازم، وأن عدم النظر فيه لا ينافي كونه بحيث متى نظر فيه علم المدلول
The Maker or knowledge of Him, otherwise it would not be a proof for Him. If the first, then from its non-existence follows its non-existence necessarily because of the non-existence of the benefit due to the non-existence of the benefiter. And if the second, then from the lack of reflection on the proof it would not be a proof, because this is a relational description that is only posited in relation to the indicated thing which we supposed is knowledge. And it is absent when reflection is absent. And it was answered: we do not mean by the proof being beneficial for something and necessitating it that it brings it into existence and obtains it as is the case with causes, but rather it is such that whenever it exists, that thing exists, and whenever one reflects on it, that thing is known. Knowledge. And its sum is that its existence entails its establishment, and reflection on it entails knowledge of it. And it is known that the negation of the necessitator does not necessitate the negation of the necessary, and that lack of reflection on it does not contradict its being such that whenever one reflects on it, the indicated thing is known.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the nature of proof (dalil) and its relation to knowledge (ilm). The objection states that if a proof leads to knowledge, then the absence of reflection on the proof would negate its status as a proof. The author clarifies that a proof does not bring about the object of knowledge like a cause, but rather it is a conditional: if the reflection occurs, knowledge follows; absence of reflection simply means the knowledge is not actualized, but the proof's potential remains. The author further explains that the existence of the proof entails its validity, and reflection on it entails knowledge, but negating the reflection does not negate the proof's capacity.

Key Terms

علم
نظر
مفيد
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proof must always produce knowledge in the thinker at any time, otherwise it is not a true proof.
Correction: The author explains that a proof is a reliable indicator; it only yields knowledge when one reflects on it, but its absence of reflection does not diminish its validity.

Checkpoint
In the answer given, how is the proof's benefit understood?
A) It brings the known into existence like a cause.
B) It is a conditional such that if one reflects, knowledge follows.
C) It is independent of reflection.
D) It ceases to be a proof without reflection.

Key Takeaway
A proof is a reliable indicator that yields knowledge upon reflection; absence of reflection does not negate its potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have the proofs of Allah's existence in the universe and in revelation. When you neglect to reflect on them, do you blame the proofs or yourself? What will you change today to ensure you reflect?

The passage distinguishes between a proof's inherent indicative capacity and your actual knowledge. Are you living as though the proofs are only valid when you think about them? How does this affect your certainty in times of doubt?
?
If a proof remains a proof even without your reflection, then your ignorance is not the proof's fault. Which proof have you been ignoring, and what step will you take this week to reflect on it?

The Circularity Objection: Can You Prove That Reasoning Doesn't Work?
Objection and answer on reflection not leading to knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection against the claim that reasoning does not lead to knowledge and the author's response that the objection either proves the opposite or is irrelevant.

Core Idea
You cannot consistently deny that reasoning leads to knowledge while using reasoning to prove that denial.

This passage presents a clever objection to the position that reflection (nazar) does not yield knowledge. The objector argues that the very knowledge of that claim must itself come from reflection or be a necessary truth that contradicts consensus. But the author turns the tables: if your counterargument succeeds, you have proven that reflection works; if it fails, your objection is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the objection and response.
The text is dense; read slowly to grasp the logical moves.
2
Identify the two prongs of the objection
The objector presents two scenarios: if the knowledge that nazar is non-productive is theoretical (nazari), it leads to contradiction; if it is necessary (daruri), it contradicts the majority of rational people.
Notice the dilemma: either way, the objector claims the position is untenable.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author responds by considering the opponent's counterargument. If the opponent's arguments succeed in falsifying the original claim, then nazar has been productive (contradicting their own position). If they fail, they are futile and the original proof stands.
This is a classic 'performative contradiction' argument.
4
Reflect on the logical conclusion
Ask yourself: what does this passage teach about the necessity of reasoning in theological discourse? The author shows that any attempt to deny the value of reasoning actually relies on reasoning.
This connects to the broader Islamic theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على جميع الوجوه بل على كل ما يحتج به لإثبات أن النظر لا يفيد العلم أن العلم بكون النظر غير مفيد للعلم، إن كان نظرياً مستفاداً من شيء من الاحتجاجات يلزم التناقض، إذ النظر قد أفاد في الجملة وإن كان ضرورياً، والوجود المذكور تنبيهات عليه لزم خلاف أكثر العقلاء في الحكم الضروري، وهو باطل بالضرورة، وإنما الجائز خلاف من العقلاء وهو لا يستلزم جواز خلاف الأكثر ، فإن قيل: نحن نعترف بأن الاحتجاج لا يفيد العلم لكن لما احتججتم على الإفادة احتججنا على نفي الإفادة معارضة للفاسد ،بالفاسد قلنا ما ذكرتم من الوجوه إن أفادت فساد كلامنا. كان النظر مفيداً للعلم وهو المطلوب، وإن لم يفد كان لغواً وبقي ما ذكرنا سالماً عن المعارض.
And it was objected on all accounts, rather on everything adduced to prove that reflection does not lead to knowledge, that the knowledge that reflection is not productive of knowledge, if it is theoretical acquired from some proofs, then contradiction follows, for reflection has been productive in some measure. And if it is necessary, and the mentioned existence is taken as indications of it, then it would contradict the majority of rational people regarding a necessary judgment, which is false necessarily. And only a minority of rational people may differ, and that does not entail the permissibility of difference among the majority. For if it is said: we acknowledge that proof does not lead to knowledge, but when you argued for productivity, we argued for non-productivity as a counterargument of false against false, we say: the arguments you mentioned, if they succeed in falsifying our statement, then reflection becomes productive of knowledge, which is the required end; and if they do not succeed, they are futile and what we mentioned remains free from counterargument.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and its answer. The objector challenges all arguments that claim reflection does not lead to knowledge. He argues that if the knowledge that reflection is not productive is itself theoretical, then it must come from reflection, contradicting the claim that reflection is non-productive. If it is necessary knowledge, then it would contradict the consensus of most rational people, which is impossible for a necessary judgment. The opponent might say they are only arguing in kind against the claim. The author replies that if the opponent's counterarguments succeed in falsifying the original claim, then reflection has been productive (since they used reflection to falsify), which is the desired conclusion. If they do not succeed, they are futile and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
ضروري
نظري
معارضة
فاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can consistently deny that reasoning leads to knowledge while using reasoning to prove that denial.
Correction: This is a performative contradiction: any argument against reasoning's productivity itself relies on reasoning, thus demonstrating its productivity. The passage shows that such a position is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would happen if the opponent's counterarguments are successful?
A) The original claim remains valid.
B) Reflection becomes productive of knowledge.
C) The opponent proves their point.
D) It is a logical stalemate.

Key Takeaway
Any argument against the productivity of reasoning inevitably relies on reasoning, making the opposition self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to argue that reason has no place in faith, while using reason to make that argument? How does this passage expose the inconsistency in your own heart?
?
When you face a truth that demands change, do you seek counterarguments to avoid submitting? The opponent in this passage offered counterarguments knowing they were false—do you ever do that with Allah's signs?
?
The author says if your reasoning fails, it's futile and the truth remains. What truth about your own soul are you using futile reasoning to avoid right now? What would it cost you to stop?

The Two Types of Invalid Reasoning
The author states the correct view and distinguishes between formal and material invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between invalid reasoning in form and in matter, and why invalid reasoning does not necessarily lead to ignorance.

Core Idea
Invalid reasoning can still yield true conclusions—don't judge by premises alone.

This lesson introduces a key logical distinction in Islamic theology: invalid reasoning can be flawed either in its form (logical structure) or its matter (content of premises). The author argues that neither type automatically produces false belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the first part of the text where the author states the correct view and explains the two types of invalidity with examples.
Pay attention to the terms صورة (form) and مادة (matter).
2
Identify the two types of invalidity
Find where the author distinguishes between invalidity in form and invalidity in matter. Notice that for form, it is 'clear' (ظاهر) that ignorance is not entailed.
Invalidity in form means the logical structure is flawed.
3
Understand the example of false premises yielding a true conclusion
The author gives an example: believing the world is an effect of a necessitator and that every such effect is originated leads to a true conclusion (world is originated) even though the first premise may be false.
This shows that false premises do not always lead to false conclusions.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for theological argumentation: one cannot automatically reject a conclusion just because the reasoning is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما النظر الفاسد فالصحيح أنه لا يستلزم الجهل، أما عند فساد الصورة فظاهر، وأما عند فساد المادة فقط فلأن الكاذب قد يستلزم الصادق كنا إذا اعتقد أن العالم أثراً لموجب وكل ما هو أثر لموجب فهو حادث . نعم قد يفيده كما إذا اعتقد أنه غني وكل غني قديم).
(۱) ما بين القوسين سقط من (أ) .
القائلون بأن النظر الصحيح المقرون بشرائطه يستلزم العلم، اختلفوا في أن النظر الفاسد (۱) اقد يستلزم الجهل، أي الاعتقاد الغير المطابق . فقال الإمام : يستلزمه لأن من اعتقد أن العالم ،قديم، وكل قديم مستغن عن المؤثر استحال أن لا يعتقد أن العالم غني عن المؤثر.
وقيل : إن كان الفساد مقصوراً على المادة يستلزمه وإلا فلا.
أما الأول فلأن لزوم النتيجة للقياس المشتمل على الشرائط ضروري ابتداء أو انتهاء، سواء كانت المقدمات صادقة أو كاذبة، كما في المثال المذكور.
وأما الثاني : فلأن معنى فساد الصورة أنه ليس من الضروب التي تلزمها النتيجة، والصحيح أنه لا يستلزم الجهل على التقديرين، أما عند فساد الصورة فظاهر كما مر (۲) وأما عند فساد المادة فقط بأن تكون الصورة من الضروب المنتجة (۳) فلأن اللازم من الكاذب قد لا يكون كاذباً كما إذا اعتقد أن العالم أثر الموجب بالذات وكل ما هو أثر الموجب بالذات فهو حادث، فإنه يستلزم أن العالم حادث . وهو حق مع كذب القياس بمقدمتيه نعم قد يفيد الجهل كما إذا اعتقد أن العالم قديم (٤) وكل قديم مستغن عن المؤثر.
والتحقيق : أنه لا نزاع في أن الفاسد صورة لا يستلزم بالاتفاق، والفاسد مادة فقط قد يستلزم . وقد لا يستلزم فمراد الإمام الإيجاب الجزئي كما في المثال المذكور.
(۱) في (ب) هل بدلاً من (قد) . (۲) سقط من (أ) لفظ (كما مر
(۳) راجع كلمة عن الضروب المنتجة في هذا الجزء .
(٤) القديم في اللغة : ما مضى على وجوده زمان طويل ويطلق في الفلسفة العربية على الموجود الذي ليس لوجوده ابتداء ويرادفه الأول. قال ابن سينا : ويقال قديم الشيء إما بحسب ذاته وإما بحسب الزمان. فالقديم بحسب الذات هو الذي ليس لذاته مبدأ . هي به موجوده. والقديم بحسب الزمان هو الذي لا أول لزمانه (النجاة (٣٥٥) .
He said: As for invalid reasoning, the correct view is that it does not entail ignorance. As for invalidity in form, it is clear. As for invalidity in matter only, because the false may entail the true, as when one believes that the world is an effect of a necessitator and whatever is an effect of a necessitator is originated. Indeed, it may entail it, as when one believes that He is rich and whatever is rich is eternal. (1) What is between the brackets is missing from (A). Those who say that valid reasoning with its conditions entails knowledge differed concerning whether invalid reasoning entails ignorance, i.e., non-conforming belief. So al-Imam said: it entails it, because one who believes that the world is eternal, and everything eternal is independent of an effect, it is impossible that he does not believe that the world is independent of an effect. And it was said: if the invalidity is confined to matter, it entails it; otherwise no. As for the first, because the entailment of the conclusion for the syllogism containing the conditions is necessary initially or finally, whether the premises are true or false, as in the mentioned example. As for the second, because the meaning of invalidity in form is that it is not among the moods that entail a conclusion. And the correct view is that it does not entail ignorance in both cases. As for when form is invalid, it is clear as passed. As for when matter only is invalid, such that the form is among productive moods, then because the necessary from the false may not be false, as when one believes that the world is an effect of the Essential Necessary, and whatever is an effect of the Essential Necessary is originated, it entails that the world is originated, which is true despite the falsity of the syllogism with its two premises. Indeed, it may yield ignorance, as when one believes that the world is eternal and everything eternal is independent of an effect. And the investigation: there is no dispute that invalid in form does not entail by agreement, and invalid in matter only may entail and may not entail. So the Imam meant partial affirmation as in the mentioned example. (1) In (B) 'hal' instead of 'qad'. (2) The phrase 'kama marra' is missing from (A). (3) See the discussion about productive moods in this part. (4) Al-qadim in language: that whose existence has passed a long time, and in Arabic philosophy it is applied to the existent that has no beginning to its existence, and it is synonymous with al-awwal. Ibn Sina said: a thing is said to be qadim either in itself or in time. Qadim in itself is that which has no beginning to its essence by which it exists. Qadim in time is that which has no beginning to its time (al-Najat, 355).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether invalid reasoning (al-nazar al-fasid) leads to ignorance (jahl). It begins by stating the correct view that it does not entail ignorance, distinguishing between invalidity in form (sura) and invalidity in matter (madda). The author then reports differing opinions: al-Imam (al-Ghazali) holds that it does entail ignorance because from false premises one may deduce a false conclusion necessarily; another view limits that to material invalidity only. The author supports the second view, giving examples: from false premises a true conclusion may follow (e.g., 'world is effect of a necessitator' and 'all such are originated' yields a true conclusion that the world is originated, though the premise 'world is effect of a necessitator' may be false). However, a false conclusion may also follow (e.g., 'world is eternal' and 'all eternal are independent' leads to the false belief that the world is independent). The investigation (al-tahqiq) concludes that there is no disagreement that formal invalidity never leads to ignorance, and material invalidity may or may not; thus al-Imam's statement is understood as partial affirmation, not universal.

جهل

مادة
